GEOFFRY HAMLYN.
Henry Kingsley is fortunate in his pub-
lishers ; or, rather, his fame is. The com-
plete edition of his works now being issued
by Ward, Lock and Bowden is one of the
prettiest series anywhere to be seen.
Marcus Clarke is said to have dubbed
“ Geoffry Hamlyn ” “the begt Australian
novel that has ever been written, or pro-
bably will be written or words to that
effect. The exact authority for the quota-
tion is not given by the quoters, and Clarke
was by no means as good a critic as he was a
writer. Anyhow, if he said such a thing, he
was decidedly wrong.
“ Geoffry Hamlyn ” is not an Australian
novel, but a novel about Australia —a very
different thing. The point of view is Eng-
lish : the characters are all English (Austra-
lians are only introduced as stupid, leggy
youths like the spitting Hawbucks) ; and the
story ends with a triumphal return to Eng-
land where Sam, it is to be hoped, makes a
suitable Shallow, and Alice duly dispenses
; old gowns to the village clothing-club. There
Lj is nothing Australian in the book but the
;i| scenery ; and that, one admits, is lovingly
, I and well described.
No wonder Englishmen admire “Geoffry
Hamlyn,’ which flatters their foibles so
naturally and continually. And “Geoffry
Hamlyn ’ is an excellent romance, with a
perfect right to admiration. But when it is
called “ Australian ” —Australia must protest
. warmly.
, “Geoffry Hamlyn" is a pleasant, ram-
-1 bling story of the old school, patchy in
; i interest, and very patchy , in merit, it is
, ■ never quite dull enough to bore, and rarely
■ j bright enough to excite. You put it down
I without difficulty, and take it up without
! ; anticipation. There is one good “lift”
M when the bushrangers come, and Sam starts
for his ride on Widderin : this gives the same
exaltation as (c. g. ) the death of Porthos,
when “ the giant slept the eternal sleep, in
the sepulchre that God had made to his
measure.”
The pictures of the old-fashioned squatters
of 40 years ago are en joyable ; and the author
has a peculiarly vivid phrase-eloquence that
is very effective. (Note commencement of
chap, xviii., p. 14G ; the bush fire, p, 198;
or the lost child, p. 279—how superior to
Marcus Clarke’s laboured Dickensy version !)
There is no style or the semblance of it:
Kingsley just prattled with his pen as he
would have with his tongue, and the story
comes less from his head than his heart.
He is simple, manly, unaffected ; and
his John Bull prejudices in favour of
“ Grand Seigniors ” and against the
independent Australians (perhaps they
ca. :ij,d them when the convict leaven
leavened the whole lump, 40 years ago ?) are
the natural prejudices of his day and
generation, birth and breeding. They jar
on us. now : bin Kingsley can’t help that.
His work has no particular intellectual
merit, but it will always have a kind of
value as a picture (from one point of view)
of a bygone Australian time. Good old
Tory I Maybe he lived better according to
his lights than we to ours, though we have
(or think we have) the better lights. /
Geoffry Hamlyn, Ravenshoe, Silcote of Silootes,
Madem nselle Mathilde, Old Margaret, See.,
Hillyars and Burtons, Stretton —cloth, 4s. each.
SOME RECENT BOOKS.
Max Pemberton’s The Little Huguenot
(Is. 7d ) is a pretty unsuccessful excursion
into the well-trodden field of French
romance. Luckily the book (like the
Huguenot) is a little one.
On a dull day, with a dull liver, next to
Dumas comes Anstey. His is abuut the
; only bright copy contributed to London
Punch (like flowers at a funeral), and there
is no other English writer who sparkles so
admirably and so consistently. “ Vice
Versa,” “The Pariah,” “A Fallen Idol”
what a delightful concatenation of chuckles !
“Lyre and Lancet,” Mr, Anstey’s latest
(3s. fid.), cannot possibly surpass these, bub
it almost equals them ; and leaves all the
other dialogue-makers (Anthony Hope in-
cluded) ever so many parasangs behind.
“ When Yalmond Came to Pontiac ” (3s. ;
cloth, 45.), Gilbert Parker regards as his
best story. It is ; though none of his long
stories equal his short ones, and Yalmond
himself would have been almost more im-
pressive in, say, 2| Bulletin columns. Yet
“ Yalmond ” is interesting, clever, and good
—a fine imagination (was there a germ 1)
and a good illusion, though the explanatory
pages after 292 are a bad anti-climax,
Yalmond should have died with his friends
around him and a woman on each side his
bed. As for example :
Then Madame Chalice, her handkerchief pressed
to her eyes, shaken by sobs that she made no pre-
tence of concealing, was led to the door by the
avocat. Bub Elise Stooped down again till her
fingers touched Yalmond’s white hands. “They
ran . . . lost Napoleon ” (304-5). Finis.
This would have been an effective close, and
left a permanent picture. The author’s zeal
for explanation spoils his story. One rises
in his fiction-balloon t-> ineffable heights and
is dropped to earth by a facts-parachute,
which breaks, and leaves sensations crushed
and lifeless.
Two recent volumes in Macmillan’s Col.
Library are “ A Martyred Fool,” by D.
Christie Murray, and “By Order of the
Brotherhood ” (3s each ; cloth, 45.). The
former is a good and readable yarn ; the
latter a trash-sample which does this most
indiscriminate of “colonial” collections no
ci'edit.
PERIODICALS.
The coming close of “ The Badminton
Library” series (for want of further material)
naturally suggested a sporting periodical
like “ The Badminton Magazine ” —of which
the first number (Is. 7d.) is just to hand.
That first number is by no means as good as
it ought to be, or as (if critical nuisances will
only have time and patience) the magazine
may yet become. It is too English, too
society, and too recherche. It wants vigor,
interest and breadth—articles something
like the graphic diary of E. A. Fitzgerald
“In the New Zealand Alps,” in August
Contemporary, a vivid extract to show
the pleasures of mountaineering. (Mr.
Fitzgerald and a Swiss guide are making the
ascent of Mount Befton) :
In front of us rose what we had always thought
to be the worst part, looking at it from the Her-
Looking at it from where we were now,
it seemed worse if anything. The rock was
slightly better, but, on the other hand, the loose
pieces were so much larger that we had to re-
double our care. Zurbriggen now crossed over on
to the Copeland side of this bit; it was just
about perpendicular here for about 300 feet, and
we were almost on the crest of the arete, with
some (5000 feet almost sheer drop below us, both
on the Copeland and the Mueher side. There
seemed some peculiarly insecure rooks nere ; some-
times we had to throw them down purposely. We
moved here one at a time with the utmost pre-
caution. I carried the two ice-axes in my hand ;
I found these considerably in my way in climbing.
All of a sudden, as I was coming up one place,
a large boulder that I touched with my right land
gave way with a great crash, falling on my chest.
Zurbriggen was just about to take the ice-axes
from me. I had them in my left hand and was
handing them to him ; the slack rope between us
lay coiled at his feet; the stone as it fell hurled me
down head first. I fell for about eight feet, when
I felt the rope jerk, and I struck against the side
of the mountain with great force. I was afraid
lest I should be .stunned, and drop the two ice-
axes in my hand, for I knew that on these our
lives depended; we should never have succeeded
in getting down the glacier through all the seracs
without them. After the rope had jerked me up,
I Lit it slip and give way, and I came down
slowly for a couple of yards. I thought Zur-
briggen was being wrenched from his position,
and I was just considering how it would feel
dashing down the 0000 feet below us, and how
many times we should strike the rocks on our way
down. _ I saw the rock that I had dislodged going-
down in big bounds; as far as I remember, it
struck the side three times, and then took an
enormous plunge of about 2uoo feet, and landed in
a crevasse in a glacier which has now been named
the Tuckett Glacier. Then I felt the rope stop,
and pull me up short.
I called out to Zurbriggen, and asked him if he
were solidly placed—l was swinging like a pen-
dulum with my back to the mountain, scarcely
touching the rock face. I should have required to
make a great effort to turn round and grasp the
rocks, and I was afraid that the strain which
would necessarily be placed on the rope from this
effort would dislodge Zurbriggen. He thought
that I had been half-killed, as he saw the rock
fall almost on top of me. As a matter of fact, it
struck my chest and glanced off to the right under
my right arm, thus saving me. His first words
were, “Are you very much hurt?” I answered,
“No,” and again I asked him was he firmly
placed? “No,” he said, “I am very badly
situated here. Turn as soon as you can, as I can-
not hold on much longer,” I gave a kick at the
rooks with one foot and managed to swing myself
round. Luckily there was a ledge near me, and I
was able to get some hand-hold almost at once. I
then scrambled up a little way and passed the ice-
axes to Zurbriggen, I held on to these during
the whole manoeuvre. We were in too bad a
place to stop or to speak to one another, so Zur-
briggen climbed up a little further and got him-
self into a firm position; then I scrambled up
after him, and in about ten minutes we had passed
this steep bit.
Here we sat a moment and took a mouthful of
brandy to recover ourselves, for our nerves had
been badly shaken by what had been so nearly a
fatal accident. At the time we did not think so
much of it, as we had to keep our nerve and take
immediate action; but when it was all over, we
felt the effects of it, and we both sat there for
about half an hour before we could move again. I
was considerably hurt by the stone ; it made a cut
in my side which did not heal for a couple of
weeks, and which bled a good deal. However,
we determined to go on and finish the ascent.
I found that Zurbriggen, when I fell, snatched
up the coil broke at his feet. Luckily, he picked
up the right piece in the coil, so that soon ho was
able to bring me nearly to rest, but the strain was
so great upon him, and he was so badly placed,
that he had to let the rope slip through his fingers
to ease his position while he placed himself a little
better. This operation cut all the skin off his
fingers, as the rope heated, slipping through his
hands, and burnt him with the friction. When he
was able finally to stop me, he said that had I
been unable to turn and grasp the rocks he must
have been dragged from his position. He declared
that in all his life he never had been so nearly
killed. Two strands of the rope were cut clean
through by the falling rock.
Now, that is real good stuff, either from an
editor’s or a reader’s point of view ; and the
Badminton wants stuff precisely similar.
Some decent Australian sporting writers
should try and liven it up.
Outing, the American sporting magazine
to which the Badminton will be a rival, has
an interesting August number (Is. Id.), with
two specially good articles on “Polo” and
“ The Yachting Cup Champions of 1896” —
the latter coming very apropos to the recent
races between Defender and Valkyrie 111.,
of which boats admirable pictures are given.
The illustrations in Outing are always re-
markably good.
Chapman's Magazine for August (Is.)
improves on the last number, though the
want of illustrations to some extent tells
against its popularity. But there are three
or four excellent specimens of literature,
and to many readers Chapman’s will appeal
as the most satisfactory of all the magazines.
NOTES.
Arthur Patchett Martin’s fugitive poems
are collected and to be published at last.
They include some uncommonly pretty and
dainty verses, and should reach Australia in
November.
Ethel Turner, like many another, finds
that somebody else has appropriated her
title. Her sequel to “Seven Little Aus-
tralians” was to be “ Growing Up ” ; it will
be “The Family of Misrule.” The book will
arrive about the end of next month ; but
Miss Turner’s short “ Story of a Baby”
should be obtainable rather earlier.
The closing of Remington’s Sydney agency
doesn t look as if so very many Australians
yearn to get into print, after all. Reming-
ton & Co. publish more trash than any other
house in London, and it is generally sup-
posed that the authors pay for their privi-
leges beforehand. Maybe the local genius
doesn’t like this reversion of old-fashioned
beliefs.
Sarorny’s Pictures discontinued publica-
tion with the June issue.

We with lb to be understood that the character of the
business we do, and the absolutely inclusive variety of the
yeede we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are alio not far out, considering the low prices we
Ask for everything we sell, and the consequent blessings
wc are continually showering upon the people, when we
Aggert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of eoufldenoe in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourielvti p UBLIO BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in bold relief against the majority, w-hose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who in self-abnegation and contentedness, are willing to
aooept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is worthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
never cease from their toil; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel f rost of
somewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it nas
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain
it has been. Generations passed away have bought irom
the great house, and generations yet to come will do _tne
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.

Bards of the Backblocks.
THE STOCKMAN’S CHEQUE.
There’s a hut in Riverina where a solitary hand
May weaken on himself and all that’s his,
There’s a pub. in Riverina where they keep a
smashing brand
Of every sort of liquor short o’ fizz.
And I’ve been an’ blued another fifty-pounder at
the pub.—
You’re very sorry for me, I’ll be bound !
But when a man is fit up free with hut, an’ horse,
an’ grub,
What the blazes does he want with fifty pound ?
Why the devil should he hoard his fifty quid?
Who would be a bit the better if he did?
Though they slithered in a week,
When I couldn’t see or speak,
Do you think I’m here to squeak?
Lord forbid.
The boss was in the homestead : when he give me
good advice
I took my oath, but took his cheque as well.
And to me the moonlit shanty looked a pocket
paradise,
Though the boss had just been calling it a hell.
Then the shanty-keeper’s daughter, she’s an edu-
cated lass,
And she bangs the new pianner all for me ;
And the shanty-keeper’s wife she sticks me up
as bold as brass,
An’ the shanty-keeper’s wife is good to see.
Two petticoats between ’em whisk you far !
But the shanty-keeper smoked behind the bar.
Oh, his words were grave and few,
And he never looked at you,
But he just uncorked a new
Gallon jar.
We fed, and then we started in the bar at nine
o’clock ;
At twelve we made a move into the cool;
The shanty-keeper he was just as steady as a rock,
And me as paralytic as a fool.
I remember the verandah like a sinkin’ vessel’s
deck,
And a brace of moons suspended in the sky. . .
And nothing more till waking and inquiring for
my cheque,
And the oath of all them three I’d drunk it dryl
So that was all I got for fifty notes !
The three of ’em stood lying in their throats :
There was one that must have seen
I’d have beat him blue an’ green
If I hadn’t gone an’ been
Off my oats.
Thank the Lord I’m back at last—though back
(wrecked and whisky-logged 1
Yet the gates have not come open that I shut,
And I’ve seen no broken fences, and I’ve found
no weak sheep bogged,
An’ my little cat is purring in the hut.
There’s tea, too, for the billy-can, there’s water
in the tanks,
The ration-bags hang heavy all around ;
! An’ my good old bunk an’ blanket beat the bare
verandah planks
Of the shanty where I blued my fifty pound 1
Here I stick until I’m worth fifty more,
When I’ll take another cheque from the store ;
And with Riverina men
All the betting is that then
I shall knock it down again
As before.
in “ The Speaker”
E. W. HORNUNG
SPARE NOT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s an old tree standing lonely by the river,
Last of all to brave the fates for ever sweeping
Through the storm winds and the forest fires
upleaping,
Last of all the leafy monarohs on the river.
High it towers like the spirit of a Glory,
O’er the waters singing harmonies unknowing
To the fledglings as they listen to the flowing
Weaving music through its softly-murmured story
Here the birds have ever found a welcome bower
At the close of day to rest their weary winging,
And the morn is ushered in by songsters singing
Gladsome litanies unto the dawning hour.
Out to north the burning chariot rolls over
Plains all shadowless through days of fiercest
glowing,
But there’s always here a limpid river flowing,
And the old tree’s shade is timely for the rover.
It has felt the life and death of seasons sundered,
As their whirlwinds swept to meet the final
changing
From the flaming days of summer to the rang-
ing
Of the Storm-Kings when their bright battalions
thundered.
Yet, though Nature’s wrathful demons did not
sever
Bird from restful tree and river-song unending,
It is straight-grained and the fence wants badly
mending,
So to-morrow must it crash farewell for ever.
North Fitzroy.

It is in their political aspect that the latest
reforms in the British War Office are potential.
In their personal aspect, merely a gaudy general,
armed with a guide-book and manual, is substi-
tuted for a gouty royal duke, armed with a gamp
and goloshes.

us for New South Wales fur the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS Limited^
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as ’ ’
TIEIIRIEIE CASTLES” and £c CAPSTALT ”

cigarette smokers in New South Wales_to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise

Yankee Doodle, ” blended Polities.
The above is, of course, a caricature of the famous American national picture, which will be
given later on.
“YANKEE DOODLE” is everywhere spoken of as the finest plug-tobacco that has ever
appeared here, and it has won greater success during the short time it has been on the market than
anything previously. This is no idle boast, and, to prove it, any smoker can test the statement for
there are few towns in N.S.W. where “YANKEE DOODLE” is not known and sold. Its sale
has tins year increased nearly one hundred (100) per cent over that of last year. And why is this?
Simply that “YANKEE DOODLE ” has struck the popular taste. In the first place, it comes, after
years of careful study, from a combination of certain choice Virginian tobaccos, so blended and
exquisitely flavoured, that any smoker who can stand a tobacco heavier than the pure gold-leaf is
forced to admit that he has at last discovered something far and away better than anything he has
been accustomed to. DIXSON & SONS have taken over half-a-century to accomplish this; and, as
their leading business has been built up on their reputation for QUALITY, they could not afford to
make the foregoing statement if any doubt existed. DIXSON & SONS, who use more Amerieon loof
ranKs supreme.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added inhere required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in alack and White,
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paii for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
A ustralasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal :—The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also :—R. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 28, 1895.
An Economic Problem.
One day recently a deputation waited on
N.S.W. Railway Commissioner Oliver and
asked a question of much larger importance
than it was seemingly aware of. It drew
attention to the fact that the fares on the
Government tramways were somewhat
heavier, in many cases, than those charged
by the omnibuses, and it wanted to know
why the State could not reduce its rates to
the omnibus level 1 In reply to which Mr.
Oliver pointed out that the tramway em-
ployes worked eight hours per diem and
received decent wages— varying, The Bul-
letin believes, between £2 and £3 per week
according to grade while the omnibus
drivers were paid a miserable pittance of 30s.
or 355. a week for 12 or even more hours’
labor. This meant that the wages of the
tramway were double those of the
’bus-driver, and, unless they were cut down
to something like the same level, it was im-
possible to put the two services on a par.
The deputation was apparently staggered by
this simple explanation, and fled in con-
siderable disorder. Next day, as might be
expected, the usual correspondent wrote to
the S.M. Herald demanding to know if the
public was to pay needlessly high fares in
order that the tram-driver or conductor
might receive a vastly higher wage than he
could be obtained for in these hungry times
on free-competition principles, and should
also get a holiday annually, and otherwise be
treated as a white man and not as a nigger
at the public expense. And as nobody
so far appears to have answered this question,
The Bulletin hereby offers a reply on
behalf of the community in general. Said
reply is that, if no other means of economy
can be discovered, the public is to go on pay-
ing the high fares aforementioned for the
exact reason set forth by the Herald’s cor-
respondent, that reason being, in this paper’s
opinion, quite sufficient for all practical pur-
poses.
• * #
Nevertheless, the deputation and the
Herald’s indignant correspondent represent
a very extensive and very unreasonable
sentiment. In Australia a large section of
the working community demands, as a mat-
ter of right, that the eight-hour system
should be established throughout the country,
by law if necessary. So far it has not been
legally established, and the principle is only
recognised in a comparatively small number
of industries ; still, the desire for its establish-
ment is a highly commendable one. The
same section desires to obtain the recognition
of a minimum-wage, which shall enable
every worker to maintain himself in comfort,
and prevent the reproduction in Australia of
the degrading conditions that exist in
Europe. This is also a commendable ambi-
tion. But, in addition to this, a very
large number demand, on Freetrade
principles, that the products of local industry
shall be as cheap here as in Europe, and
that even in industries where wages are
the heaviest outlay, goods produced by men
working at Is. an hour shall cost no more
than goods produced in European factories
by men who are only paid 4d. an hour or
less. And, failing this, they will buy in pre-
ference European goods made on the 4d.-an-
hour plan, and imported under the blessed
conditions of Freetrade. Therefore, when
the eight-hour system and the minimum-
wage and the great Freetrade principle are
applied all together, the natural result will
be the entire shrivelling-up of many Aus-
tralian industries, so that there will be no
work whatever, and no wage, minimum or
otherwise. The Labor man who votes the
Foreign-trade ticket demands that the Aus-
tralian should work for the shortest hours
and the highest wages on earth, and yet
produce as cheaply as the European who
accepts half or a quarter the rate. So
far as concerns the hours and the wages his
theory is admirable, but, in the matter of
cheapness, under present conditions he is
asking the impossible.
* * »
The three recognised taxes on every in-
dustry are rent, interest or profit on capital,
and wages. Also, there is indirectly a fourth
and very serious tax which is not generally
recognised. The cities of Australia, like
those of many other countries, are much too
large for the population, but in Australia
this evil has assumed a more aggravated
shape than in most other lands. And these
cities are enormously overcrowded with
merchants, dealers, clerks, agents, lawyers,
bagmen, sharebrokers, people who do
miscellaneous things on commission, finan-
ciers of innumerable varieties, and hun-
dreds of other kinds of non-producers, all
of whom must needs live somehow on the
producing industries, simply because there is
nothing else—except the temporary influx of
borrowed money—on which they can live. Of
the recognised taxes on productive industry
—-those whose amount can be ascertained
with reasonable accuracy—wages are the
largest in the aggregate, though in some
exceptional instances, such as those of Broken
Hill and Mount Morgan, the amount spent
under this head may be miserably small
compared to the interest on capital. The
unrecognised tax that imposed by the
superfluous city population which does
nothing useful for its daily crust—is prob-
ably the heaviest of all, but its amount can
hardly even be guessed at. If any means
can be found of reducing by one-half or
thereabouts the outlay on rent and interest
and the indirect tax imposed for the support
of the superfluous non-producing population
of Australia’s bloated capitals, then it may
be possible to enforce an eight-hour system
and a respectable minimum wage, and yet
produce so cheaply as to compete with the
underpaid, overworked foreigner upon Free-
trade or moderately Protective conditions.
But unless this reduction is made first, the
eight-hour system and the minimum-wage
only mean a large increase in the cost of
production, and without a heavy protective
tariff to equalise matters Australian indus-
tries must go down like a house of cards.
• # #
The Sydney tramway management pays
reasonable wages, and works its employes
moderate hours, and yet holds its own for
various exceptional reasons. It is an enter-
prise which involves a very small outlay for
rent. The money for construction purposes
was obtained at less than 4 per cent, on a
State guarantee, whereas a private enter-
prise would, in most cases, have to pay con-
siderably more. It is subsidised by the rail-
way department, its shortage being lumped
in with the railway accounts, and further
subsidised by the nation which makes up the
combined shortage of the two departments ;
and it is an undertaking which, by its
nature, is unaffected by foreign competition.
By strict economy and by charging higher
fares than the public approves of, and by
virtue of the other advantages enumerated
above, it is enabled to live on the eight-hour
and minimum-wage principle where a pri-
vate co. would promptly go insolvent. If its
rivals, the omnibuses, were compelled to
pay their employes the same wages and work
them the same hours—in other words,
if the tramways were a protected industry—
the department would doubtless acquire
much more business and would pay hand-
somely. And if the rivals of the Australian
manufacturer or agriculturist—the German,
Italian, Englishman, American, Chinaman,
Japanese, JJand all the rest—could also be
compelled to observe the same laws as to
hours and wages, he also might be able to
prosper without Protection of the Customs
variety. As they can’t be so compelled, the
case for Freetrade, a minimum-wage, and
the compulsory eight-hours, is hopeless
under present conditions.
* # *
With the two exceptions of mining and
wool-growing it is questionable if there is a
single producing industry of any importance
which could hold its own under the condi-
tions suggested. The farmer pays wretched
wages and requires his employes to work
unspeakable hours, yet under Freetrade he
held his own with the utmost difficulty
because his foreign rivals paid much worse
wages and worked as hard, if not harder. If
the eight hour system and the “living wage”
were applied in his case under a Freetrade
policy, the cheap Asiatic would deliver
wheat in all the populous parts of New
South Wales at prices with which he could
not possibly compete. The manufactur-
ing industries which are exposed to foreign
competition would “go broke ’ unanimously.
The wool industry would hold its own so
10. ig as the world’s supply is, as at present,
just about equal to the demand, but if the
supply ever exceeded the demand this
country would be the first to suffer from the
change. If there is ever to be in Australia,
as there certainly ought to be, a universal
eight-hours’ system and a universal wage-
rate, which will enable the people to live
according to a civilised standard, it cannot
be done under the Reid tariff, nor under
the social conditions now existing. With a
heavy and mathematically correct Protective
tariff and the other conditions as they
now are, it would be possible in a
certain limited sense. That is to say,
there would be more employment, higher
wages, higher prices all round as the natural
result of the rise in wages, and a wider
diffusion of what comfort existed. The man
who now earns 10s., a day would then, most
probably, find his income only worth Bs.,
and the man who got the minimum wage of,
say, 6s. 6d. per diem would probably find
his income equal to ss. at present rates.
The difference in his favor would be that he
would get the 6s. 6d. a day, whereas, at pre-
sent, in many instances, he receives nothing
at all. But it must be recognised that it is
impossible to fix a general minimum-wage of
any reasonable amount, or a general eight
or even nine-hour standard without raising
the cost of production, unless the extra cost
can be saved oft' some of the other charges
upon industry. And there is no sense of
justice in fixing a wage or hour standard
unless it is intended to apply ultimately to
the whole community, except in those cases
where no hour-standard is possible. And,
further, to raise the cost of production
means to seriously injure the country’s
export-trade in any case, and—except under
a heavy Protective tariff—to destroy most of
its industries altogether.
» * •
No one has yet devised a really effectual
method of lifting any of the dead-weights off
industry, so that Labor may get a more
reasonable share of the proceeds. Henry
George has stated eloquently the blissful
results to be arrived at by the removal of
the incubus of rent, but, unfortunately, his
scheme only shows the advantages to be
derived when the community has got there,
and the method of getting there without
causing unspeakable disasters en\ route is left
a mystery. No one has yet devised a work-
able scheme to regulate interest—though the
State Land Bank promises to alleviate a
part of the existing evil—nor to prevent the
employer taking an undue share of the
profits. In some instances this cormorant
annexes anything up to 95 per cent, or so of
the net earnings while paying starvation
wages ; in many others, he at present
makes next to nothing, or even less. There-
fore, no general rule or general malediction
applies. And, above all, no one has pro-
posed a satisfactory remedy for the worst
burden of all—the vast multitude of non-
producers, who constitute nearly half the
adult male population, and of whom at least
a third may safely be set down as a super-
fluous burden upon industry. And until
some of these dead weights are removed, any
attempt to enforce a minimum wage or a
fixed standard of working hours, except in
those industries which are not subject to
foreign competition, or except by shutting
out foreign competition altogether, is merely
enacting that a pint-pot shall henceforth be
a quart.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Go on the Land.
It ia an axiom nowadays that every really
right-minded young man should go on the land,
or in some other way become a direct producer
of wealth; and large quantities of serious old
men who never were producers, and never went
on the land, and don’t mean to, keep incul-
cating this lesson with great energy. Never-
theless, the young men of Australia stay off the
land with great success, and the proportion of
clerks, shopkeepers, lawyers, bagmen, parsons,
and every other variety of non-producing urban
population keeps on increasing out of all ratio
to the growth of population. And, as all these
people have to live on the miners, artisans, and
pastoral and agricultural workers, and as the pro-
portion of workers who support them is steadily
diminishing, and as the young man of to-day
won’t go on the land, partly for the very excellent
reason that, somehow or other, the land doesn’t
seem to support him when it is gone on, the
prospects for the future of this country are dis-
tinctly unpromising. In N.S. Wales, at the
census of 1881, the male “ professional classes ”
—solicitors, barristers, clergymen, doctors,
musimans, architects, &c., &c formed less
chan 4| per cent, of the male workers, whereas
in 18!)1 they formed over per cent. The
commercial classes—the clerks, bagmen, book-
keepers, accountants, financial agents, im-
porters, book and insurance fiends, shopkeepers
and miscellaneous hangers-on in and around
offices increased from 15 to 21 per cent, of the
working male population. The artisan class
meanwhile declined from 37 per cent, to 32 per
cent., and the agricultural, mining and pastoral
population fell from 36 per cent, to 35. The
net result is that where there were 73 actual
producers out of every 100 male workers in
1881, ten years later there were oidy 67 ; in
other words, instead of 73 men supporting' 27,
it became a case of 67 men supporting 33. In
Victoria during the same decade the professional
class increased from 19 per 1000 of the total
male population to 22 per 1000. The com-
mercial class increased from 75 to 99 per 1000.
The miners diminished, in proportion to popula-
tion, nearly one-half. The agricultural and
pastoral class fell from 182 to 159 per 1000, and
the only really good feature in the record is
that the artisan class increased from 224 to 291
per 1000—equal to about 40 per cent, of the
adult male population. Still, the total result is
that from 1881 to 1891 the young man kept
going on the land less and less every year, and
that the habit of sitting in an office has been
growing on the community to an alarming ex-
tent. Some day some Australian Government
will awake to a full knowledge of the situation,
and then it will take desperate steps to drive
the young man on to the land by taking him
out there under police supervision, and confis-
cating his pants to prevent him coming back.
The Ikey Mo. Problem.
It seems that the Victorian Supreme Court re-
fuses to recognise a street or right-of-way as a
“place” within the meaning of the Betting
Act, although the English law (when driven
into a corner) interprets the word in its widest
possible sense. Cuthbert’s bill to suppress
wagering in public thoroughfares will meet
this deficiency, which might have been equally
well filled by the obnoxious “insulting-be-
haviour ” clause in the police instructions. To
stand around and take small silver bets from
all and sundry is “insulting behaviour,” if a
myrmidon of the law cares to so call it. Any-
thing is 1.8. in Melbourne, as one or two mem-
bers of the N.S.W. Assembly can testify.
However, the Supreme Court admits a fixed
place, such as a building, a stall, or a
particular stand, to be a place where bet-
ting is illegal, and since the general
public seldom or never bet away from fixed
places, and bookmakers’ clubs are buildings,
the law remains as impudent a humbug as ever,
else would the racing game be promptly
squelched. Cuthbert’s contemptible little Bill
provides that betting in any public place within
100 ft. of a roadway is an offence punishable by
fine or imprisonment. But why within 100 ft.
of a roadway? A “great moral evil” is not
affected one way or other by questions of
locality. The offence is the same whether
committed at Flemington or Little Bourke-
street. is betting illegal in one
place, yet permitted to go on else-
where, with police to control the crowd and
arrest defaulting bookmakers if necessary ?
Why can a detective seize, on his own tobacco-
shop premises, the very same Ikey Mo who gets
a special license from the racing-clubs ? Hoes
Ikey Mo, who is not individually a moral evil,
cease to he a moral evil when he mobs with 100
other Ikeys all laying the odds at once? And,
if so, why ? Let the parsons preach the immor-
ality of betting legislation before turning their
snuffle against the sin of gambling one’s money
in the Tote.
The Government Stroke.
Mr. H. 0. L. Anderson, now head of Sydney
Public Library and formerly N.S.W. Director of
Agriculture, gave the following astonishing tes-
timony before the recent N.S. W. Civil Service
Inquiry Board :
When you were Director of Agriculture did you ever
have people thrust upon you against your will ?—Yes. I
submitted a scheme to the Minister by which the colony
should be divided into four parts, and administered by a
resident inspector in each division. The first division
was the sub tropical one, from the Tweed to the Clarence,
to be in charge of an inspector, who should be an expert
in the cultivation of sugar-cane and other tropical plants.
Secondly, I proposed that there should be a farming dis-
trict in New England, to be in charge of an expert in sheep-
raising, turnip-growing, and English farming, with its
head-quarters at Armidale. The third district was to be the
Western Plains, where wheat and sheep are the specialties
at present. The fourth division was to be the Coast
District, south of the Manning, which should have a
dairy expert to. teach the farmers. I laid this scheme
before the Minister, who said it was an excellent
idea, but that the country could not afford it. He
was willing, however, to start with one inspector. We
thereupon.advertised for an inspector, and got 26 appli-
cations, only one of which was good enough to please the
Minister, who therefore postponed the matter and made
no appointment, nor did he make any provision on the
Estimates then being framed. I never heard anything
more about it. The Minister (Mr. Sydney Smith) went
out Of power in October, 1891, and Mr. Slattery came in.
One day a gentleman came to me and said, “ I want you
to appoint my son-in-law inspector of agriculture. He is
downstairs, in the Stock Department, earning 10s. a day.
He has had great experience down at Fiji.” I said, “ You
must see the Minister. I have nothing to do with it.” I
hinted that the inspector should be a man of good scien-
tific and practical training. He replied, “It is all right,
I will get him appointed.” I said, “ Don’t say that. The
Minister will never do it without consulting me.”
He laughed and said, ‘‘l will have him appointed
in a few days.” Previously to that time no appoin;-
ment had ever been made in the department with-
out my having been first consulted as to the necessity for
the position and the qualification of the candidate. Mr.
Smith never appointed even an office-boy without con-
ferring with me. A few days after this interview five in-
spectors of agriculture were appointed, and another
appointment was made of inspector of agricultural shows,
the last being to fill a vacancy. This man had never seen
an agricultural show in New South Wales.
What salary did the inspectors receive ?—The five new
ones £3OO a year each. I had recommended to the
Minister that they should have graduated salaries, from
£2OO to £3OO.
They were all bracketed at £3OO a year ?—Yes.
And they were not required ?—No, because they were
not agricultural experts; I could not find work for them.
Then men were appointed in the Public Service who
were not required?—Certainly. If they had been.good
men they would have been very valuable, and would have
done most necessary work. I could have done magnificent
work for the colony with highly-trained practical men,
men trained in an agricultural college, with expert know-
ledge in their respective lints. But the five men were all
appointed on the recommendation of political friends—
alw'ays irresponsible, and often incompetent to judge of
the candidate's qualifications. I had picked out the
eight candidates with the best testimonials, and expected


a rigid examination of these candidates and their records
before even one should be appointed on probation.
Are these men in the department now?—One is. The
Minister who prepared the Estimates only provided tor
one.
Are they still in the service?—l do not know if all are.
I can give the history of one who is ; he charged travel-
ling expenses of 12s. a day on an occasion when I saw him
in town. 1 asked him to explain how it was that he was
charging for travelling expenses when he had not left
Sydney. He said he had been out of town, and gave me
full details of his proceedings on the day in question. I
told him to look at his diary. He afterwards confessed
that he was not travelling that night on departmental
business, and it transpired that he stayed behind a day,
sent in a false return to show that hg was in the country,
falsified his diary, and charged his travelling expenses. I
reported him. I said he ought not' to remain in our ser-
vice. He gave as an explanation that it was an oversight,
and it was accepted. He repeated this serious offence
within a few weeks, and was then admonished—nothing
more. All this can be proved from the papers. He was
reported afterwards for drunkenness in my office. Wit-
nesses were not even asked for evidence. His explanation
was deemed satisfactory. •
Is he still in the service?—Yes. He was retrenched
from the Department of Agriculture, and soon afterwards
appointed to another posHion.
You spoke of a gentleman who* asked you to appoint
his son-in-law inspector ?-»He was not a trained expert in
agriculture; but he was a gentleman. I had no trouble
with him at all. He did his routine work very satis-
factorily, and improved very much in usefulness.
Did he know his business?—He was not an expert.
Another of the men appointed celebrated his first day in
the civil service by getting drunk, and he came to my
office in that slate, his Excuse b|tftg that he had been
travelling all night. I told him’jfo go and get sober,
which he did. He caiaae hack afterwards fairly sober.
He was a very- intelligent man, and I sent him up the
country to represent the department, and visit the farmers
to instruct them. He was reported as drunk there. I
went into the country to investigate the case, and found
he had been beastly drunk for two weeks, and had done
no work. I reported him, and recommended that he hi
dismissed. He was allowed to continue for two weeks
longer, and draw his salary all the time, when he resigned.
He was nob censured, but I was for suspending a drunken
man from duty.
Daring the time you were in the Agricultural Depart-
ment were there many other cases of persons being put in
your department against i our will, or were the ones you
mentioned the only cases that occurred ?—There were
many other cases.
Mr. Slattery, who gives a general denial to
Mr. Anderson’s statements so far as they affect
himself, states that he has asked Premier Reid
to have a special Parliamentary committee
appointed to elicit the facts.
The Bogan Bridge at Monkey-
Some More.
Premier Reid’s new Loan Bill only differs from
theold one rejected by N.S. VV. Leg. Council inso-
mubh as it is an even more impudent fraud on
posterity than its predecessor. A smaller pro-
portion of it goes into works calculated to yield
revenue, and a larger percentage into small
jobs of everyday occurrence, which can give no
return, and which no honest Legislature could
charge to posterity, for the reason that posterity
will have similar liabilities of its own to meet
as long as the country lasts. Therefore, the
proposal to leave posterity to pay its own
bills and those of its fathers as well is
a mean device to avoid facing to - day’s
responsibilities. Among the more conspicuous
of these frauds upon the coming Australian,
£lO,OOO is to be spent in repurchasing lands
which were lately sold and the proceeds put
into revenue ; they will probably be sold again
and the proceeds put into revenue a second
time, so that the next generation will lose the
land and also have to pay for it. The famous
vote for “thinning out forests” has been in-
creased from £25,000 to £50,000. There is
£96,000 for building sundry bridges, including
the Bogan bridge at Monkey, which will be
worn-out long before the people are done pay-
ing the cost of them, and for patching old
bridges, and wood - blocking Enmore - road,
Sydney. The same old items for dredging
rivers, which will have to be dredged over and
over again so long as they keep on rim-
ing, are to come out of the borrowed funds
and are cheerfully classed as “permanent and
reproductive works !” despite the obvious fact
that they are neither permanent nor reproduc-
tive ; and the sums for patching old dredges,
repairing wharves, repairing public buildings,
clearing lands where the bush will grow again
before the second year’s interest falls due, buy-
ing stores, and making the Mosman’s Bay drain
are also in the list as formerly. The total
amount of the new borrowings is now set down
at £1,555,000, and of this £1,100,000 is for jobs
which should no more be charged to loans than
should Treasurer Reid’s official salary or bar-
ber’s bill, inasmuch as they will produce no re-
venue, and instead of being permanent as alleged
they are works of a kind which turn up every
year and will keep turning up every year for
centuries. Still the measure, so far as the
Assembly is concerned, has progressed with
very little opposition. Mr. Reid’s attitude in
the matter is simple and pathetic. If the Loan
Bill is passed he will find work for the unem-
ployed out of the proceeds. If it is not passed
he won’t raise the required amount by earnest
retrenchment, as could easily be done, and start
the proposed works without the loan, but he
will point to those who voted against his un-
principled finance as bowelless despots who
have no compassion on the poor. Therefore,
the poor will either eject them at next election,
or throw bricks at them, or both. Under
these circumstances the Loan Bill will pro-
bably pass, though The Bulletin, despite
its sympathy with the unemployed, trusts that
even at this late hour some disaster may happen
to it. The kind of compassion which consists
m helping the people of to-day, whose votes
keep G. H. Reid in office, by bringing down
worse disaster on those of to-morrow, whose
votes won’t be of any value to G. H. Reid, is
an emotion to be regarded with doubt. New
South Wales can and should pay for all its
current repairs out of revenub, and thereby find
employment and do much useful work for the
country, and if its Government won’t do this,
then it is time it got another Government which
will.
Economic Drivel.
Sydney Telegraph
We cannot bar imports without, to the same extent,
barring exports. The countries which buy our products
nave nothing to pay lor them with but products of their
own. According as we tax the bringing ih of those pro-
ducts, therefore, we tax the sending out of ours for
which they are sent here in payment. It is only a Free-
trade Government, therefore, that can consistently assist
m the development of an export industry.
Wherefore this paper rises to ask a question :
if the cou”tries which buy our products must
pay for them witli products of their own, and
a country will only buy our exports if we buy
its goods in return, how comes it that New
South Wales annually sells £1,100,000 worth of
goods to Belgium though it only buys £129,000
worth in return ? Also, how is it that Chili buys
from N.S. W. £120,000 worth of goods, though
New South Wales buys nothing in return ; and
France buys from New South Wales 23 times
as much as it sells to that province; Germany
three times as much as it sells ; German New
Guinea about 2500 times as much ; Hawaii 40
times as much ; Italy three times as much ;
while New South Wales buys from Ceylon 35
times as much as Ceylon buys from it? In
short, how is it that in no single case is the
balance of trade equal? The whole business
consists in buying from countries that don’t buy
in return, or selling to countries which don’t
sell in return, and yet the Telegraph keeps
repeating the old, old shibboleth that if we
don’t buy German beer and iron and shoddy, Ger-
many won’t buy our wool, and so on ad infinitum.
The simple and obvious answer is that other
countries will buy our goods if they need them,
and that the German who buys our wool in
London probably doesn’t even know whether
we admit German iron and beer or not, and
wouldn’t care a straw if he did know, and isn’t
sufficiently interested to inquire. When the
Telegraph bought its American type-setting
machines from a country which shuts down all
it can on New South Wales exports, it gave
the lie flat to its own theory. It bought the
machines because it wanted them, and even if
the States had admitted all Australia’s pro-
ducts free for ever and ever, it wouldn’t have
bought them if it hadn’t wanted them. The
theory that countries only take goods from
countries which buy in return, and that Pro-
tection is fatal to exports, and that only a
Freetrade Government can consistently foster
an export trade, is a pitiable variety of rot.
Does anyone, when he buys a German pipe,
first hunt up the tariff to see if Germany takes
frozen meat from this country or not ?
“ Outis,” of Sydney Telegraph, gets on to the
horrors of Protection once more :—-
There were recently submitted to the Vic. Government
tenders tor the supply of a large quantity of Gippsland
coal, and some disgust was expressed in good Protectionist
circles because Govt, did not at once close with the offers
made. The quantity of coal tendered for was 156,000
tons, and the Gippsland pit-owners offered among them
to supply this at a price averaging 16s. per ton, or 4s.
more than the best Newcastle coal, warranted to burn and
produce heat. The difference in cost to the Govt, is esti-
mated at not less than £19,000, and probably as much as
£30,000. It would pay the Vic. taxpayers just as well to
subsidise, say, 200 men at the rate of £2 a week for their
own personal benefit, and without consideration in return.
The facts of the case, as fully reported in the
Melbourne papers, are rather different. The
Vic. coal was offered at 11s. 7d. per ton, the
alleged 16s. being one of the elaborate fictions
by which the Telegraph keeps up the story
about the horrors of Protection. As Victorian
coal is sold retail in Melbourne at 12s. 6d. per
ton, the idea of the owners demanding 16s.
wholesale is unique. And the statement that
Newcastle coal could be supplied for £30,000 a
year less is another invention ; what was
stated was that the Vic. coal cost £30,000 less
to get out of the pits than the owners demanded
from the Government; also that the required
amount of Newcastle coal could be bought for
£40,000 per annum less at Newcastle — no allow-
ance being made for freight and other charges.
The beautiful mixture which “Outis” produces
out of these facts may not be lying, but it looks
like a bad case of jim-jams.
Extract from the N.S.W. gaol regulations
lately issued by Minister Gould :
Clause 22. Prisoners shall, when practicable, bathe
once a week. They shall wash each morning and (if
there be no means of bathing) their feet on Saturday.
Apparently, therefore, there are prisons in
N.S.W. where there are no recognised means
of bathing, and as no provision is made for the
coufinee “ cleaning himself ” with a bucket of
water and a rag, he may remain unwashed—
except as regards his feet—until the end of his
term. Therefore, The Bulletin suggests that
the form of sentence should be altered to fit the
case. “Prisoner, you are hereby sentenced to
be imprisoned and to remain dirty, except,
perhaps, as regards your feet, for 12
calendar months,” would be expressive. Also,
it would be explicit, which the present form
isn’t, as it says nothing at all about the prisoner
remaining dirty.
In the British Medical Journal of August 3,
1895 (page 285), is reported Sir William
Priestly’s opening address at the annual meet-
ing of the British Medical Association. An
extract: —
There .are always discontented women who magnify
their sufferings, and some neurotic patients will submit
to any martyrdom for the sake of evoking sympathy. As
an extreme example of what neurotic women will endure
and even crave for in the way of operation, I may mention
the case of a woman who suffered several successive
amputations, beginning with the finger and ending with
the removal of the shoulder-joint, for injuries which were
self-inflicted.
In view of recent Australian events, this ex-
tract is most suggestive. Again :
The Melb. police have abandoned the case of alleged
outrage on the girl Daisy Watson at Albert Park. The
girl informed the police that she thought the man’s name
was Burns, but on Burns being arrested and brought
before her, the girl said that she did not think Burns
was the man, and refused to take any further action.
Ed. Bulletin, —“Medicus,” in a recent issue,
draws attention to a matter that will require
further ventilation if the generous support
hitherto accorded to Sydney’s hospitals is to be
upheld. Any tiling like sectarian or racial
monopoly in organisation and management must
tend to alienate that broad public sympathy
with them which should exist, and, however we
may admire the desired success by Scotchmen,
no one wishes to see all other claims and quali-
fications passed over in the interest of Scotia’s
clannishness. Surely, if any such claim of birth
should operate, the claim of the native-born
might be advanced against the world. The last
three practitioners appointed to Sydney Hospital
are all Scotchmen, with Scotch qualifications.
Again there is a vacancy, and of the present
applicants two are Australian—one an M. D. of
London and one an M.B. of Edinburgh, while a
third is a Scotchman who has practised in Syd-
ney for a shorter period than either of the
other applicants ; yet the appointment may
almost be considered “cut and dried ” for this
gentleman, against whom nothing can be said,
but in favor of whom nationality is the main
consideration. The Alfred Hospital exhibits a
striking contrast to this spirit, for there men
with Australian diplomas (other things being
equal) are preferred to outsiders.— Patria.
At Prahran (Vic.) Court the other day a
marine-stores collector was brought up to show
cause why his license should not be cancelled,
on the ground that he had been convicted of
larceny in 1879 ! It was not shown that the
police intervention in this instance was due to
any offence recently committed by defendant,
nor was it shown that at any time during the
period from ’79 to ’95 the man had led other
than an honest life. Indeed, the police were
fain to admit, obviously with reluctance, that
his manner of earning a livelihood was perfectly
reputable, and equally that ‘ no suspicion
attached to the man himself other than that
engendered possibly by the fact that he had been
convicted for a petty theft 16 years ago. More-
over, it transpired that the offence in question
consisted in his having stolen a pair of boots,
his defence at t he time being that, while drunk,
he was given the boots to pawn by a com-
panion, and that he did not know they were
stolen. The Bench, after solemnly digesting
these facts, adjourned the case for a fortnight,
“to allow further inquiries to be made”—
which doubtless means that the representatives
of Law-’n’-Order are “tipped the wink” to
diligently rake over the dust-heap of 16 years
to find some more recent and convincing
iniquity perpetrated by the hapless man. Mean-
while the object of these gallant proceedings
spends a joyous interval of suspense with the
prospect of losing his license at the end of it.
In this case of the insignificant dealer, be
the previous circumstances what they may, the
police show up to advantage—as usual.
A suspicious circumstance in the Victorian
Closed Roads (politely called unused roads)
debate was the eagerness of the squatters to
pay rent for roads of which for years they have
had free use. There is a statutory obligation
on squatters to fence off these roads, but their
domination of the local Councils and the supine-
ness of Parliament has given them a free lease-
hold of lands that at a moderate rental would
bring in <£26,000 a year. Longmore made a
picturesque attack on the proposal for leasing.
He cited a Government road in Camperdown
shire closed by the occupant squatter with
stone walls, necessitating a d6tour of 20
miles to every man travelling from Derri-
nallum to Camperdown. For 20 years the
residents suffered under the grievance. The
grey old Radical quoted with gusto his one-
time leader’s opinion : “ Higinbotham said in
his hearing in that House that if once the roads
were allowed to get into the hands of the great
proprietors by any authority whatever, the
country would never have the roads again.”
First there had been swing-gates, and now it
was proposed to close the roads. The next step
would be the purchase of the roads, and, after
that, they could never be reclaimed. Longmore
had a drastic remedy for the grievance. The
squatters had already made a million out of the
roads. Their statutory obligation to fence
them would entail an expenditure of a million.
The maddest wag of the rain - beseeching
controversy has turned up in the man who
wails, in Sydney Daily Telegraph, over
the profanity of the astronomer who —in
view of the “facts” that the sun stood still
for Joshua, and “went back ten steps” for
the edification of Hezekiah —omits to append
“ D. V.” to the prediction of an eclipse, and of
the meteorologist who dares to prophesy mois-
ture without explaining at the same time that
it will probably be withheld “ through God’s
anger at the wickedness of the people.” But
meteorologists don’t “prophesy;” they only
“ forecast,” and “ even the astronomer,” says
Huxley, “ claims no more for the assumption
that the sun will rise to-morrow than a very
high degree of probability.” Science, with all
her wealth of corroboration, has never yet
ventured upon the absolute certainty so in-
solently arrogated by theology—with no corro-
boration whatever !
What the Sulphide Problem Is.
The 88 million ounces of silver and 380,000
tons of lead, which the Broken Hill mines have
produced, have been derived almost entirely
from oxidised ores. These ores, which for the
greater part can be simply smelted, are now
practically exhausted, except in the Central,
British, and B.H. Proprietary mines. The
supply in the Central is uncertain, and in the
British very small; and in the B.H. Proprie-
tary, which is infinitely the best off, it is esti-
mated that not more than two years’ supply
remains.
# * *
Underlying the oxidised ores, and extending
to unknown depths, is sulphide ore. Its extent
is uncertain, but enormous. It occurs in vast
bodies —has been found at a depth of 100 ft.,
and still exists at the lowest level reached,
1250 ft. This ore consists of sulphide of zinc,
commonly called blende; sulphide of silver;
and a silicions matrix of gangue. Both the
zinc and the lead particles carry silver. Hun-
dreds of thousands of tons of it average 25 per
cent, lead, 22 per cent, zinc, and from 7 to 45
oz, silver. The gross value of the metallic
contents per ton averages higher than that of
the oxidised ores, at least so far as the upper-
level sulphides are concerned. The aggregate
value of the metals in the ore is many tens of
millions. The problem of their extraction, if it
be a problem, is therefore of national impor-
tance.
# « #
The ore cannot be smelted in the ordinary
way on account of the high percentage of zinc.
Zinc flies into vapor rapidly, and chills rapidly.
Hence it chokes up the furnace if there is more
than (i per cent, present in the charge.
# # •
The ore cannot be smelted for zinc. Zinc
smelting is a distilling process. The ore,
powdered, is put into smaller retorts with coal
or coke-dust, and tiie vapor is collected. Any
silver and lead (or the greater part of it) is left
in the bottom, and this residue can be smelted.
But the retorts are necessarily made of some
silicions compound, and if there is much lead
present it eats out the silica, destroys the
retort, impedes the progress of distillation, and
spoils the zinc.
M * «
The ore cannot be effectively dealt with by
mechanical means—by jigging or concentration,
that is. The trouble lies in, first, the fineness
of the particles and the intimacy of their
association, and, then, in the fact that both
lead and zinc particles are silver-bearing. To
completely separate the zinc from the lead
would mean such fine crushing as to “ flour ”
or “slime” almost the lot. Water action on
slimes is in almost all circumstances ineffective.
# # #
In the laboratory it is easy with any one of
several acid solutions to leach out the zinc,
leaving only the silver and lead in the ore,
especially after a roasting. Dr. Schnabel sug-
gested sulphurous and sulphuric acids. They,
like the others that have been suggested, were
found too expensive. Treatment by any of
these is called a humid or chemical process.
# # #
To save acid and to assist in the deposition
of the zinc, which the acid solution has taken
up, electricity has been employed. The direct
electrical deposition of the zinc has also been
attempted. The obstacle is that the zinc ore is
a wretchedly poor conductor; the action is,
therefore, very slow, and cost correspondingly
heavy. Mr. Ashcroft’s process is an electrical
one ; it has yet to be tried on a commercial
scale. This is the electrolytic method. The
humid method, with electrolytic help, is the
ideal; the mechanical is the practical. Tired
of waiting for somebody to reach the ideal, the
directors of several companies have made a
beginning with the practical. They have,
therefore, adopted the admittedly imperfect
mechanical method. They do not get every-
thing out, and never will. They do get enough
to make it pay.
• * #
Concentration is based on the fact that the
specific gravity of zinc blende is 4, and of this
lead about 7‘5. The raw ore is first broken in
machines, then ground, then the particles are
separated by jigging, hydraulic classification,
&c. The object is to eliminate the zinc so that
what is left may be smelted. As it is impos-
sible to make a complete separation without
great loss in slimes, the ore is not ground very
fine. Some zinc is thus retained : the concen-
trates show from 6 to 13 per cent.
* # #
Concentrates that contain more than about 6
per cent, zinc, if not blended with ores heavy in
lead before smelting, must be prepared by a
desulphurising and chlorodising roasting. This
is done at Block 14, under Mr. Greenway’s
management.
# • •
Generally, then, the lower the percentage of
zinc the greater the quantity of “ fines,”
“ slimes,” or “zinc concentrates ” —all one and
the same thing. As they are generally rich in
silver, it pays to blend the best of them with
lead ores, making a smelting compound. But
heavy deductions are made on account of their
zinc contents.
* # •
It is considered a good concentration which
results in the saving of 70 per cent, of the lead
and 55 per cent, of the silver. As all the zinc
is lost, and as zinc is valued at <£ls per ton,
silver at 2s. 7d. per oz., and lead at .£lO ss. per
ton, it follows that nearly two-thirds in value
of the metal is not recovered. As this two-
thirds is the tailings, which contain, say, two-
thirds of the zinc that was originally in the ore
and only, say, one-fourth of the lead, it is pos-
sible that these tailings may yet be worked
primarily for zinc. But no process yet laid
before the public will allow of its being done.
* # *
The zinc market is very uncertain. It is
controlled by a Continental “ ring,” which is
very jealous of outside production. Steady
improvements have been made in the mechani-
cal process by the addition of buddies, keeves,
hydraulic classifiers, &c., and by special arrange-
ments of jigs. These permit of the treatment
of the slimes, and the consequent fine crushing
of the ore. This improvement is the most
hopeful feature of the whole business.

Quite a gloom has been cast over S. A.
provincial journalism by the killing of the far-
famed “ Tantanoola Tiger,” which turned out to
be a wolf. Through the shooting of the animal,
the Mount Gambler journalists have been robbed
of their bread and butter, and will have to return
to the abnormal cauliflower for a living :
The rural chap will miss him when he starts to
make things hum,
After days of good bush whisky or of strong new
season’s rum ;
He will miss old Tantanoola from his once familiar
lair,
As he shapes his course for homewards after hours
of thoughtful pray’r.
He will miss the zest of telling to the gaping.
fearsome throng,
How he saw the blessed tiger as he crossed the
billabong :
How he marked his vivid striping, and the color
of his eyes,
And took especial notice of his prehistoric size.
Yes, he’ll miss old Tantanoola when he’s out upon
a “ bust,"
And some passing neighbor finds him writhing
madly in the dust.
The tiger’s been disposed of—superfudge, is at an
end, ,v>.
And to time-worn snakes and devils must he once
again descend.

Town Hall Mark Twain.
Mark Twain is one of the very few im-
ported celebrities who can bear inspection— rea-
sonably near inspection, that is. He is a white-
haired, shaggy person, who presents a close and,
perhaps, intentional resemblance to his portrait
as it appeared recently in The Bulletin. His
head is like an amazed gum-tree, and his mane
sticks out in all directions, and gives him the wild
expression of a man who has just found a baby’s
shoe in the soup. Yet he is a very placid
humorist, all the same. He speaks slowly and
lazily, with a break between his sentences, so that
he can listen to Smythe counting the takings in
the back-room as he goes along. Also, he trots up
and down at times with a curious trot, but most
of the time he stands quite still in front of his
audience and muses aloud. There is practically
nothing new in his discourse. All Ids stories are
taken from his own old books, and, though he tells
them in a wildly comic fashion, their age is a con-
siderable drawback. Also, the extreme gradual-
ness of the speaker makes each story last a long
time; the inspiriting anecdote about a corpse,
which commenced the proceedings on the first
night, lasted 15min., and in the Australian
climate, where the deceased doesn’t keep for any
considerable period, this prolonged exposure was
unwise. Moreover, the Australian likes his
corpse fresh. Still, it was a most interesting
corpse, and Mark Twain must be set down
as a decided success. On his first night
six lank, soulful-looking people left early,
one of them, who evidently viewed Mark as a
frivolous person, saying that he “ couldn’t stand
this rot.” The rest of the audience remained and
applauded tremendously. One enthusiast even
stated audibly that he would have given 20 guineas
only to see the author of the “ Jumping Frog,”
and his friend said he would have given thirty,
though whether they wanted to see all of him for
the money, or only his head and his dress-suit,
didn’t appear. Anyhow, The Bulletin is of
opinion that M. Twain could make a considerable
sum by giving private back views of himself in his
shower-bath at a guinea a head. He wears a
cleaner collar than Talrnage, and lacks the funereal
aspect of most humorists this paper has known,
and, all things considered, he doesn’t look like a
man who deserved to have Parkes’s oratory let
loose upon him at a heavy dinner in a strange
land. The people who set up Parkes to talk at
Mark Twain had a wide knowledge of the unfit-
ness of things in general.
** * *
The Opera co. began its season at Sydney
Lyceum with the ever - welcome “Ma Mie
Rosette,” which went gaily and joyously from the
first line to the last. The change in the cast
hasn’t caused any serious damage. The fair May
Pollard puts rather more clothes on to her art-
less village maiden than her predecessor used to
do, though there is absolutely no reason, judging
from what of Miss Pollard still remains visible,
why this should be so. Her melodious voice is in
excellent condition, and when she states in verse
that she was dreaming she does it just as effec-
tively as even the departed Nellie, Also, she
throws her whole great soul into the part, and
when Miss Pollard throws her soul in real earnest
it generally hits something. Altogether the new
Rosette is a girl from whom anybody might be
proud to receive milk at a late hour, either in a
chateau or elsewhere, and why she has not
hitherto occupied a more prominent position on
the Firm’s bills is a mystery. Wallace Brownlow
has added some new touches to his picture of the
king, and there is a deeper villainy in his muffled
voice as he tells Tapley to start at once for the
seat of war, and leave him to be a father to his
best girl in his absence. Lauri, Joseph Tapley,
Misses Flo. Young and Marietta Nash admirably
fill their old parts, and Howard Vernon’s colonel
seems to have lost several more ears since he last
appeared in Sydney. “Madame Angot,” with
Miss Juliet Wray as Clairette, is announced to
make an intermittent appearance on Tuesday and
the two succeeding nights, too late for notice in
this issue. Then there is to be another fleeting
appearance of “Rosette,” and the “Old Guard ”
follows on Saturday.
** * *
George Rignold did his last night at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) on Saturday, and the big
theatre is now closed, pending the appearance of
the Woods-Marshall Co. on Saturday of next
week. “Clancarty” did a one-night revival on
this occasion, and- went remarkably well in the
presence of a large audience. Its best features
were the fine acting of Sterling Whyte as William
111., and Miss Maud Williamson’s excellent,
though at times rather shriekful, rendering of
Lady Clancarty. At a convenient interval,
Trickett, M.L.C., made his appearance with a
vast quantity of notes, and made a few long, sad,
slow observations, in the course of which he
remarked six times that Geo. Rignold was now
about to take his departure. The dreadful
protractedness of Trickett, and the manner
in which he wandered round himself in
circles, and lost himself at times, and fell
over himself at other times when his feet got
entangled in the' end of a sentence, became a
heavy burden after a while. But finally he
emerged sufficiently from his vocabulary to pre-
sent the retiring lessee with a silver tea and coffee
service, and then he departed with both hands
full of large adjectives, and Mr. Rignold replied
in a bright, effective speech, and had flowers
thrown at him. Also, Mrs. Rignold appeared in
response to a great public demand, and after that
—Trickett having presumably been removed from
the premises—the drama went on. G.R. is going
to do a season of “Joseph of Canaan” in Mel-
bourne, and when Joseph’s twelve red, scowling
brethren have settled in Egypt for the last time
he will take his departure for Europe.

able tenor of his marvellonsly-prosperous way at

** * *
The short-haired child with the heavenly
expression depicted on page 11 is Dudley Wyatt,
the Calculating Boy, whose astonishing logarithms
created a sensation at Sydney Empire. Dudley
is fully as young as he looks, his probable age
being about seven. Whether he really calculates,
or whether figures simply grow on him as d : rt
does on the common or back-yard infant, the
present writer doesn’t pretend to know. Any-
how, young Dudley always seems to get there,
and his calculations are presumably correct,
though the sums which he does in a few seconds
are so vast that no one else can check the calcula-
tion and get the answer out before the theatre
closes for the night. Still, one or two enthusiasts
have sat up all night to verify his figures, and
after finishing the calculation have broken down
beneath the mental strain and could never remem-
ber what the result came to. There is no known
recipe for making a calculating boy ; young Wyatt
simply grew and made the life of the house cat a
misery, and fell downstairs like other infants, and
then suddenly developed his gift and became
what he is.
** * *
The last night of the Gaiety girls at the
Lyceum was a tremendous occasion. The pro-
gramme consisted of sections from “In Town ”
“Gentleman Joe,” and the “Gaiety Girl,” but
that was the smallest part of the night’s doings.
The lower part of the house was enthusiastic ; the
accumulated johnnydom in the circle was appre-
ciative, though recent reductions in its salary
somewhat mitigated its fervour; and the gallery
boiled over incessantly, and threw little bunchesof
flowers, and shrieked forth its farewell till its
voice gave out, and heaved like a troubled sea of
hats and meat and vegetation. Of the ladies,
Grace Palotta, Decima Moore and Madge Rosseli
were evidently thefavorites, and these charmers got
away at last with the stentorian howl of the gods
still ringing in their ears. The gods simply
emptied themselves of all the howl that was in
them at the trio, and it will be a month before
they accumulate enough to disturb a prayer-
meeting with. The receptions accorded to Kaye
and Bradfield were something to remember.
The latest of the airy nothings confided to
the George Edwardes co.’s care was produced at
Melb. Princess’s on Saturday. Its character,
which is rather less airy and somewhat more
nothing than “In Town,” has been recently dis-
cussed by The Bulletin. Al 2 nights’ run ought
to meet all demands. Louis Brad field and his
colleagues are playing “ Gentleman Joe ” without
the advantage of knowing exactly how the trick
is done by Arthur Roberts—a circumstance of
much importance to the case, probably. Any-
how, this musical comedy doesn’t display Brad-
field and the others at their best in any one instance.
People vyho have seen them all do something
better will derive comparatively little joy from
“Gentleman Joe,” whereas new patrons will take
the bright visions as they come and find no fault
with them. The chief defect in “ Gentleman
Joe ” seems to be an entire lack of satirical pur-
pose. Sundry daughters are introduced with a
first appearance of having an end in view, but
they wander aimlessly around without getting the
least bit forarder. Nobody strikes up an original
flirtation. The girls who want millionaires at the
start are just as hopelessly in need of those handy
articles at the finish. Also, Miss Decima Moore’s
voice is several sizes smaller than of yore, and
Miss Palotta’s accent is a drawback instead of the
valuable item it ought to be, and the pas de trois
is injuriously affected by linen pants cut level
with the knee, and charming Florence Lloyd is
too big for her buttons. Yet, after all has been
said, the fact remains that the Gaiety Girl Co. is
a stylish organisation under any conditions. An
unclescribed something lends a special thingummy
to the show, or seems to, which is precisely the
same thing.

Outside of the Princess’s nothing is hap-
pening in Melbourne to-day (Thursday) with the
exception of two ’alls. They are taking place as
usual in an ordinary manner. At the Opera
House, where a necessary guard has been set upon
the wildly-improper joke which loves to sneak
into a variety entertainment in search
of gallery larfs, little Alina Grey and the
fresh young Banvard acrobats are strong
points of a stupendous programme. The
Cambridge show still asserts a just claim to exist-
ence, and its symptoms of increasing vitality
furnish gratifying proof that enterprise and —in
short, the Cambridge begins to make itself felt.
The trouble with all new shows of the kind is to
get a firm grip of the world’s understanding and
make themselves felt.

** * *
A rather nauseous joke is the father
(genuine or “ professional ”) of the youthful acro-
bat who dives from a dizzy height into a net. The
netting is there, invitingly spread for the boy
whenever he likes to let himself drop. The job is
entirely his own—the most personal matter in the
world. Bub the irresponsible parent obtrudes
himself upon the transaction by gazing up to the
roof and shouting, “Are you ready, ’Dolphus?”
When the boy has answered that he is ready, the
parent cries “ Go ! ” with an important expression
of countenance, and in such a fateful tone that he
scoops about tiO per cent, of the glory attaching to
the dive. The audience get a confused idea that
this adult magnate has a genius for discovering
the exact moment when he ought to give the
word. Also, he is supposed to be a splendid
judge of distance, and a few other details which
haven’t the least bearing'on the boy’s performance.
“ Are you ready, ’Dolphus?” It’s an impressive
gag.

“ It; ia now said,” writes The Bulletin’s
