Half Melbourne, on Saturday night,
Made tracks for the “Gaiety” show :
Excited, elated,
The city awaited
The advent'of “ Gentleman Joe.”
Smart girls, in the circle polite,
Attended by soft-headed beaux,
Gave over their flirting
The moment the cnrting
Was lifted from Gentleman Joe,
And the giddy old johnnies below
Sat close to the stage in a row
Whilst a god near the ceiling
Led “ popular feeling ”
To battle for Gentleman Joe,
Ho ! ho!
He barracked for Gentleman Joe.
The farce made a promising start,
It seemed almost certain to “go;”
The multitude, cheering
Each idol’s appearing,
Shed glory on Gentleman Joe.
His Nibs in the principal part
Was Coddington.Bradfield, so
Before he had spoken
They gave him a token
Of favor for Gentleman Joe.
The praise they had come to bestow
Diminished in vigor, although
That god near the ceiling
Was proudly revealing
His love for poor Gentleman Joe,
Ho ! ho I
He barracked for Gentleman Joe.
The critics were troubled with doubts
When Act number 2 travelled slow j
Few ditties and dances
To tickle their fancies
Are worked into Gentleman Joe.
But the god’s burly baritone shouts
Dispelled all suspicion of woe j
His singular gladness
Made mirth ot the sadness
Attaching to Gentleman Joe,
Each lady and gent in the co.
Doth a deep debt of gratitude owe
To that voice near the ceiling.
Persistently pealing
Gay laughter at Gentleman Joe.
Ho ! ho!
He barracked for Gentleman Joe.
The plot is as bald as an egg,
The characters slighter than snow;
Some songs of Palotta’s
And Ryley’s are “ rottahs,”
And, furthermore, Gentleman Joe
Is weak in the matter of “leg”—
Which is, weally, the cwuellest blow ;
And Dt cima’s what is
Called cronk in the glottis,
The place whence sweet melodies flow.
Maud Hobson gets darned little show,
Fred. Kaye’s bit of pudding is dough ;
Yet the god near the ceiling
Brought ointment for healing
Sore places in Gentleman Joe.
Ho! ho!
He barracked for Gentleman Joe.

Stanway, the shipping-clerk implicated in the Rosenfeld
Customs fraud (Vic.) some time ago, who got three
months’ gaol, and was released on account of bad health,
has since died of_ cancer. “Jack” Stanway, as he was
familiarly known in amateur-musical circles, was looked
on at the time of the trial as merely the tool of the prin-
cipals. The Judge was of that opinion—hence the small
sentence. Stanway was an enthusiastic helper in all
charitable projects, and was, perhaps, Melbourne’s best
amateur comedian. His was a sad end—he fell a victim
to the too-oommon alternative : do what we want you to
do, or go and starve.

Miss Gracie Dudley, paragraphed in a London paper as
coming from Melbourne and now playing parts in the
English provinces, was some years ago an infant-prodigy
in the “Curfew shall not toll to-night” line, and para-
lysed Melb. society with her precocious recitative talents.
She was then a baby-faced, golden-locked darling, with a
strong family likeness to Mabel Love. Her mother was
the popular manageress of Melb. Coffee Palace. In saying
that Gracie D. played the page in “ Brighton,” the
English paper quoted by Tub Bulletin is probably mixing
up the identities. It w'as her sister who was a member of
Harry St. M mr’s co., and who became “ The Second Mrs.
David Christie Murray.” But that is another story.

“Fridolin”: “Melbourne is suffering much, these
times, from a tendency to discover amateur contralto
warblers who have ‘ a finer voice than Ada Crossley,’ or
‘a stronger voice than Crossley’s,’ which latter recom-
mendation could be fairly claimed for a steam-roller or a
factory-encine. As a rule, the new wonder is a wild, un-
trammelled performer with a compass of six moans, and a
shocking facility for moaning out of tune. In the best
oases she has a voice of good quality which needs to have
a lot of superfluous noise taken from it, and can’t possibly
be truly judged by the standard of excellence in its pre-
sent state. And since the singer and her circle of admirers
are, generally speaking, satisfied that the voice has com-
pleted its education, the ‘Crossleys’ now most talked
about are the least likely to be heard of in five years’
time. A really line singer comes out with a bang, and
stays there.
During the stay of Stoddart’s professionals at the Old
White Hart, Melbourne, “Jimmie ” Norrie was instruct-
ing a quartette (himself included) to sing the old song,
“Scotland’s Burning.” They were getting along
famously, in the backyard, Norrie using a folded copy of
the Age as a baton. “Scotland’s burning,” &c., “Pour
on water,” yelled the quartette—when suddenly a power-
ful stream deluged the singers, it was Lockwood, the
Surrey pro., concealed at the other end of the yard, who
had turned on the hose and granted their request.
How these singers love one another! ’Tis said that
when the divine Patti reappeared in London a month
or so ago, Melba, for the time in the shade, rtmarked
spitefully: “ Well, she ought to be a good singer ; she has
had plenty of practice. Didn’t the great Duke of Welling-
ton admire her very much ?”

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

In the old cemetery at Newtown (Sydney) is a row of
four "professional” graves. One monument, adorned
with a harp and broken harp-strings, and the figure of a
kneeling, weeping woman, is inscribed to the memory of
Nicholas Charles Bocksa, died 6th Jan., 1858, aged 65
years, " erected by his loving pupil. Anna Bishop.” The
next is to the memory of George Peck, “ Leader of the
orchestra of the Royal Princess’s Theatre, Sydney.”
The third, a very modest one, to Carandini’s old friend,
Lavenu, A.R.A.M., who died Aug. I, 1859 ; and the fourth
to the memory of a Sydney favorite actor and manager,
John Gordon Griffiths, of the ‘‘Old Vic.,” Pitt-street, who
died 4th March, 1857, aged 47.
Dear Bulletin,— A recent “Poverty Point” par. says
Louise Pomeroy was “married on the Ballarat stage in
‘Romeo aid Juliet.”’ Now, I was best man; and she
wasn’t. Louise was married in Bendigo—not on the stage,
but in the church of the Rev. Garlick, who tied the knot.
(The piece being performed at the Princess’s, Bendigo,
was “Romeo and Juliet.”) Dr. J. E. Neild gave the bride
away. Sassiety folks played the larrikin exactly as at the
Essie Jenyns wedding in Sydney, and poor Pomeroy was
quite hysterical, and nearly fainted. We had trouble to
get the wedding-party into the church, as it was already
crammed to the doors, and the unruly mob in silks and
laces did a deal of damage to the seats and the church
furniture.— Best Man.
The Wild-Cat Column.
A third and altogether new account of the Bank
of New Zealand’s finances arrives by mail. This is the
statement which Treas. Ward laid before Parliament, so,
presumably, it must be authentic. It sets forth that the
Estates Cos., for which the B.N.Z. is responsible, owes
£4,376,427 and has assets worth £2,580,746 (though
another valuation apparently sets them down at consider-
ably less); shortage about £1,800,000. The bank has
also a net deficit of £376,000 on its own account ; total,
£2 176,000. This is not taking into account a possible
further shortage in the bank of £200,000. When
the B.N.Z. capital (£900,000) and the proceeds
(estimated at £450,000) of the call now’ coming
in are both written off, they will provide for
- 1 350,000; so that according to these figures there
will still apparently be a deficit of £726,000 on the
highest valuaoon of the assets, and saying nothing of
further possible losses in the bank. This explains the
otherwise mysterious proviso that the bank is to pay
£50,000 annually to the Estates Cos. towards reducing the
deficiency, which, even if there are no further “ revela-
tions,” will take a good many years to wipe off.
The Govt, has already guaranteed £2. 000,000 on
account of the bank. It is now to take up £500,000 in
preference shares. And in addition it is to lend to the
bank £2,734,000 worth of 3J per cent, bonds on the
security of the Estates Go’s landed properties, valued at
£1,879,000, and on the uncalled capital of the bank.
Therefore, the State guarantees now amount to
£5,234,000. The position of the shareholders is some-
thing like this: All their existing capital is lost. They
are called upon to pay up £500,000 which is also lost.
They are then to be called upon to pay another £500,000
as working capital, which is mortgaged to the Govern-
ment in case the remaining deficit cannot be paid out of
the bank’s profits ; and apparently they are liable for yet
another £500,000 if required. Altogether the saving of
the 8.N.Z., if it is fully saved, has been about the biggest
dead lift on record. Meanwhile the London shareholders,
w'ho represent over one-third of the shares, are still un-
settled and aggravated, and will possibly make further
trouble.
* * * *
Under the N.S.W. Land and Income-tax Bill,
it seems to be an open question whether banka are not
liable for income-tax on the interest which they pay to
their depositors and debenture-holders. Also, though a
man holding unencumbered land of less value than £475
is exempt from taxation, a man holding mortgages of less
value than £475 is not exempt. Therefore, it may come
to pass that a piece of land is exempt when the owner is
in good circumstances and his property is not mortgaged,
but if he is poor and his land is mortgaged then it becomes
taxable. The Bill is most indefinite, but it seems to
read that way, anyhow, though the Supreme Court may
decide differently. Said Court will have a good deal of
hard work to do before it is finished interpreting the mys-
teries of this hazy measure, and it will provide fees for
many expensive barristers and solicitors.
Of the mortgages registered in South Australia
last year more than a quarter were at 5 per cent. ; more
than another quarter were at from 6 to 10 per cent. ;
more than a-third were simply at “ current banking
rates," which probably average 8 per cent. ; and a small
proportion —about one-sixlieth —were at 10 per cent, or
more. The S.A. Savings Bank lends money at 4J per
cent., and the local Fat Man argues from this that there
is no need for a State Land Bank. But the above figures
-bow that all the Savings Bank has to lend is a mere drop
in the ocean compared to the demand, and the Sand 10
per cent. Shylock si ill flourishes exceedingly, and requires,
in the public interest, to be obliterated.

Cabled that the Colonial Bank of Maoriland has
made a net profit for the half-year of £11,279, against
£12,473 last half-year and £16,436 the one before. The
div. at Sept., 1894, was at the rate of 7 per cent, per
an. ; last half year it fell to 6 per cent, (equal to £12,000),
and this time, if there is a div., it will evidently have to
be cut down to five. Meanwhile, the question is hung up,
pending the arrangement for selling out the business to
the B.N.Z. Despite the emphatic assurances of the direc-
torate that all is well, rumors concerning the solidity of
the Colonial continue to circulate, and the apparent readi-
ness of everybody concerned to trade off the business to
the B.N.Z. looks as if there was a considerable lack of con-
fidence in its future. Another sign is that its £2 shares
were selling, when last heard of, at 17s.
• * * *
Melbourne’s big importing firms are bitterly
opposed to some high duties and secretly in favor of
others. It happens that in the case of woollens only two
or three wholesale houses (one especially) stand to profit
much by high duties, therefore the influence of the retail
tailoring trade is strong enough to bring the tariff down.
Every master-tailor—and his employes’ interests are iden-
tical with his own—is a manufacturer of clothes standing
in this position. He must either buy taxed foreign cloths
or untaxed goods from the local mills, and he wishes to
Heaven that the Victorian millers could supply him with
the stuff his customers demand. He is the friend, not the
enemy, of local production —a warm Protectionist up to a
certain point, say 15 per cent. But when the duty upon
goods which he can’t buy from local mills is higher than
he can afford to plank down in ready cash at the Customs,
the tailor has to get what he wants from the middleman
importer, who cheerfully pays the 40 per cent, tax and
adds it, plus usury, to the price of his cloths.^
Would the Reid Govt, have affixed a punitive
condition to the appointment of any member of the com-
munity except ex-Premier Dibbs to the vacant managing-
trusteeship of N.S.W. Savings Bank? The casting of the
condition in Dibhs’ face on the very threshold of the
undertaking, is calculated to excite an obstinate and able
foe to work his hardest to prevent that amalgamation of
the banks on which Premier Reid professes to have set
his heart.

■ATEWSPAPERS.—TENDERS will he received on or
before Thursday, 19th September, for the PUR-
CHASE, as a going concern, of the Plant, Slock,
Goodwill, Book Debts, &c., of an EVENING DAILY
PAPER. Further particulars by letter fropi Mr. G.
N. OAKLEY, 33 Exhibition-street, Melbourne,
The Victorian railway deficit for the year ended
30th June is £380,000. The Railway Commission pro-
poses various devices whereby it is reckoned that £365,000
may he saved annually, thereby reducing the shortage to
£15,000. It is proposed to save £85,000 off wages ;
£BO,OOO off fuel ; £107,000 by closing the Port Melbourne
and various other workshops, and concentrating the
building and repairing work in one or two large shops ;
£40,000 by a revision of fares and freights ; and £103,000
by abolishing all free passes, and charging the various
Govt, departments for services rendered. A large part of
the railway deficit is caused by carrying goods and per-
sons free for the benefit of other public departments, and
the Commissioners rightly object to having their deficit
enlarged so that other Govt, offices may show a bogus
economy. The Vic. railway accounts have not balanced
since 'B9, and if they can be squared now it will be quite
a new experience.

The Bulletin has hitherto thought that Aus-
tralia was the only country in which the assignee pest
was as great a curse to commerce as the rabbit is to agri-

Yanks are equally afflicted : “School-teacher (to youthful
Jewish pupil): ‘ What is an assignee?’ Ikey Failupski:
‘ Der brother-in-law of the man vot falls.’ ”
* • * *
The Queensland Mutual Assurance Cos. (paid-up
capital £15,477) has made the very handsome profit of
£4380 for the year ended 30th June. An 8 per cent. div.
is declared; £229 14s. lOd. is placed to investment-fluctua-
tion account, making it £6OO ; and £2912 added to profit
and loss account, making the credit balance £6133. The
Q. Mutual, though it has been doing business for seven
years, has no reserve-fund.
The case of the Queensland Mutual, like that of
all other such offices, is an evidence that fire-insurance, on
the present competitive system, doesn’t pay the insurer.
It doesn’t always pay the co. either, but it pays the
insurer worst of all. There are between 50 and 60 fire co’s
doing business in Australasia, and the public has to pay
for all their separate hoards, office staffs, agency
staffs, premises, advertising, and all the rest.
The natural consequence is that it gets a lot more
office expenses than fire insurance for its money.
Last year the public paid to the Q. Mutual
£14,033 in premiums and got bacK £4233 in fire losses.
The Victoria collected £115,000 premiums ; paid £35,000
losses. The Australian Mutual Fire Society, premiums,
£29,000 ; losses, £6700. N.Z. Insurance Cos., premiums,
£265,000; losses, £154,000, and so on through the whole
list. Evidently the public pays between twice and three
times as much for its insurance as the actual insurance is
worth, the balance going in working costs and divs.
—principally the former. Private co’s, so far as it is
possible to estimate, can’t do the business any cheaper
under existing conditions ; the biggest outlay is for com-
mission and office expenses, and the offices must pay
these and other charges or get no business. But if it
were possible to establish a system of compulsory muni-
cipal insurance—all buildings and contents within the
municipality 7 , except possibly some extra hazardous
risks, to be kept permanently insured, and the
premiums collected in the same manner as other
rates, only a good deal more stringently ; and if
it were possible to run the system honestly and intelli-
gently, and the premiums were, say 7 ,lo per cent, less than
those now charged, and if several other tilings could come
to pass, fire insurance, with the commission and a lot of
the office expenses saved, should furnish a handsome
income to the municipalities. The Central Govt, couldn’t
run such a business profitably, as it requires local know-
ledge ; but municipal fire insurance, if conducted on plain
business lines, should be a practicable speculation. The
chief difficulty would be in the constant inclination to
put the alderman’s brother Bill in chatge of the business,
instead of engaging an expert who was nobody’s brother
in particular. Brother Bill is the bane of many an other-
wise promising undertaking.

• • ♦ •
The balance-sheet of the Standard Bank (Melb.)
presents some striking features. The profit and loss
account shows a deficiency of £17,979 (the shortage of the
half-year being £4900), yet the bank proudly exhibits a
reserve-fund of £4OOO. The directors might have added
£96,000 more to the reserve and made the deficiency on
the other side £114,000, but somehow they didn’t. The
deposits, current accounts, interest, accrued, and amounts
due to other banks are £766,000, and the institution has
apparently £10,228 in cash against them, W'hioh is about
the most abbreviated cash reserve on record. The board
regretfully remarks that many accounts are so bad
that it didn’t even go through the formality of debiting
them with interest, doubtless because it knew the interest
couldn’t be collected. Nothing was said about the chance
of collecting the principal. Also, £32,000 of interest has
been charged but not reckoned as profit, apparently
because the accounts, though not so bad as the other lot,
were too decomposed to be viewed with any certainty.
A good many banks seem chiefly to have three kinds of
advances. One is putrifled ; the second is only gamey ; and
the hoard is busy all its time keeping the flies off the
third lot.
♦ * * *
Melb. Permanent Building Society, which lately
got its debenture interest reduced from 5 to 21 per cent.,
reports sorrowfully that, despite the saving of £9300
under this head alone, it has failed to make ends meet for
the past year. The shortage is £SOB. The net income is
about 1J per cent, per annum on the assets. House and
land property to the nominal value of £456,000 forms
four-fifths of the said assets, and the net returns under
this head are about 13s. per cent, for the year. The board
is of opinion that the property won’t yield more than £1
per cent, for a long while to come. Among the assets are
150 Commercial Bank of Australia preference shares at
£lO (£1500), said shares being now 7 worth something under
355. ; also, 150 Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank
preference shares at £5 (£750), present value an abso-
lutely unknown quantity. The institution will probably
pull through in the long run, its present loss being owing
to reconstruction expenses : still, its balance-sheet is a
remarkably steep document in mere ways than one.

Freetrade S.M. Herald, in its “Notes from
Western Australia” (7/9/’95), at last acknowledges to
Protectionists its fatal fiscal error:—“ It will be gathered,
therefore, that the W.A. Government are holding out
every inducement to practical agriculturists in the shape
of cheap land, a protective tariff, and an entire absence of
any harassing conditions.” Since a protective tariff is
inducement to immigrants from distant homes, the P.T.
must indeed be a part and parcel of good government.
The S.M. Herald says so, and surely she is an honorable
woman.

Dear Bulletin,—Your remarks re examination
of meat, for exportation may also apply to the N.S.W. ex-
port butter-trade. If butter-consumers only got a
glimpse of the process of making butter at many of the
Australian factories, I think an anti-butter-eating society
would be formed. Not long ago, at a meeting of share-
holders of a district milk-and-butter co., the city manager
of the co. publicly staled that the “stuff ” they were_ ex-
porting was “ damnableand no wonder, for to go into
many milking-yards on a wet morning and see the filth
scraped off, in the milking, into the milk-pails would be
sufficient to satisfy any appetite, save a hog’s, for either
milk nr butler. The dairyman being either too busy or
too lazy, saves unnecessary trouble by reckoning on the
separator to free the milk of all filth, and the butter pro-
duced from this blend of milk and cow-excrement is ex-
pected to compete against the cleanly-produced article
of such countries as Denmark, Belgium and Holland. —
Rousbabout.

* • » ♦
A pathetic old bushman called in recently at
Toowoomba (Q.) Savings Bank, and wanted to deposit his
little pile as a provision for his last days. Said pile con-
sisted principally of notes of the Bank of Queensland
which burit disastrously in ’66. The pathetic old man
had hoarded them carefully for over 30 years. He had
never heard of the historic collapse of Queensland’s first
big bank, and when the matter was mentioned to him he
replied that it didn’t signify—the Govt, would pay up the
value of the notes. Then the officials tried to explain
that Govt, wouldn’t pay anything ; that bank-notes w ; ere
simply the I O U’s of a crowd of private speculators who
were responsible to nobody, and that the value of his
savings was nil. The old man, however, refused to
believe this monstrous assertion, and went away smiling
with his decomposed paper-money in his hand. He died
shortly afterwards, perhaps through learning the solemn
reality of the situation. The local paper calls this “a
remarkable proof of the faith which some men have in
the face-value of a bank-note.” So it is. It is also a
remarkable evidence of the iniquity of the private-bank-
note system which Queensland has abolished, but which
the other Australias still adhere to with such touching
fidelity.

* » • *
The Great Boulder line of lode has now been
traced for nearly 20 miles, and unless something happens
this weary formation will die of exhaustion and sore feet.
Asa traveller it is beginning to outrival the good old
Broken Hill lode, which bad an obliging habit of travel-
ing into every God forsaken little lease floated on the
market, until at last it died in a vain but noble effort to
reach Sydney. Only the other day the Great Boulder
lode was discovered on the Murchison, and there are dis-
tinct traces of it at Charters Towers and Ballarat. The
Great Boulder line becomes bigger as it proceeds—puts
on muscle as it travels ; and will be at least 30,000 ft. wide
when it crosses the S.A. border. By the time it reaches
Adelaide it will be big enough to crush the whole popula-
tion underfoot and not notice it.

The Break-o’-day (Vic.) lead has been traced
through Rokewood township, and down over a mile to
the southward. One fortunate machine has washed a
day’s dirt for 450 z. Owing to the lamentable ignorance of
the miners, wtio mostly believe the preliminary step in
acquiring a claim to be an agreement with the landlord
(w'hich is quite unnecessary), all the companies to dale
have ceded exasperating royalties to freeholders. It is
likely that the agreements are illegal, and one company at
the proper lime will decline to pay and will test the case.

The Charters Towers gold-returns are entirely
sat isfactory, and unless prospects are much belied, there
can be no doubt that the ’94 total of gold won will be ex-
ceeded, and another record for the field put up. The
Aug. average is slightly less than in the previous year—
due to the very large* tonnage put through by Mount
Leyshon, an open quarry, which is treating over 3000
tons monthly for about 3dwt. to the ton. Mills’ United
reappears on the div.-list after a couple of months’
absence. The eight months’ div. reached £161,351 ; calls
for same period, £57,895; margin of profit, £103,456.
The “ outside” favorite line is the Lady Florence, which,
says Bright’s Circular, certainly looks like a permanent
thing. The splendid developments in the Brilliant and
St. George United—big reef in the sink, big reef in the
levels, big reef in the slopes, and regular 2oz. crushings—
have helped all surrounding properties. The Aug. div.
was 9d., equal to £5400, and the crushings 1320 tons for
27670 z. The Brilliant is still raising lots of stone, and is
assured of a profitable future. Aug. crushings, 2215 tons
for 2394 oz. ; div. of 3d., equal to £3250. Brilliant Block
looks well as ever, and the output of about 2500 tons a
month will be easily expanded when the 40-head battery
is finished. Aug. crushings, 2773 tons for 2668nz. ; div.,
6d., equal to £2500. The mill is being erected out of
profits remaining after the monthly £2500 div. is paid.
Day Dawn Block and Wyndham seems to have become a
steady producer, and a div. should soon be forthcoming.
Aug. return, 2074 tons for 16510 z. Mills’ Day Dawn
United—Aug. return, 4161 lons for 60640 z. (three fort-
nightly clean ups). Victory shows no sign of improve-
ment.

Dear Bulletin, —Re “ Economy ” and “ Orneo ”
on Democrat mine, Glen Wills (Vic.). Taking stipendiary
director’s screw at, say, £7 per week ; the mine-manager,
£6 per week ; underground-manager, at £3 5s. ; and two
shift-bosses at £2 15s. each, you arrive at a total of £21
15s. per week. This, with a reef averaging 3in. wide,
hard ground, with 19s. lOd. for carting and crushing
added, gives the shareholders “ Buckley’s ’’ chance of
profit. The whole business is over-managed, and could be
fully attended to by a competent miner as manager and
two shift-bosses, who have to be provided under the
Mining Act. —Rad.

The Celestial has not yet got footing on the
West Coast of Tasmania. About five years ago two
venturesome ones made an attempt to land at Trial Har-
bour (the then seaport of Zeehan). The punt-men gave
them a rough time and dumped them into the sea.
Eventually they were rescued by the policeman and
locked up till an opportunity came to ship them away
again. Tas. miners don’t love John. They had enough
of him on the E. Coast tin-fields, from which they were
practically ousted by the almond-eyed.

Out of the 11 members of the Cairns-New
Guinea prospecting party, in less than six months three
have died and four returned invalided, leaving only four
able to continue the exploration. The remedies used in
N.Q. for Gulf fever, ague, &o., appear to be of little avail
on the other side of Torres Straits, and the fiercest Aus-
tralian myall is a poor specimen compared with the war-
like and crafty natives of Papua.

Lucky Australians .
Dear Bulletin, —Be Stead’s “Penny
Poets.” I would like to believe that the “masses”
love poetry. Unfortunately, the love of poetry
in the true sense is rather rare. Good jingle
tickles most ears; maudlin sentiment in rhyme,
like that in some of Sims’ ballads, stirs the
emotions —especially those of women ; and stirring
martial verse, like Macaulay’s or Kipling’s, is
generally relished ; but, for all that, the great
bulk of the people have no appreciation of
“poetry.” Keats, Matthew Arnold, Poe,
Swinburne, if obtainable at Id. each, would
hardly be read, and certainly not understood, by
more than 25 per cent, of the people—if that.
This is no defamation of Demos, but a statement
of fact, regretable, yet easily accounted for. It
takes a long time for culture and light to diffuse ;
and, perhaps, the spread of knowledge and the
appeals to the imagination and better instincts
by such works as “ Looking Backward,” under-
standable by an ever-widening circle, are as good
a preparation as any. — Dingo.

to the Australian market, and without doubt its
Rothschilds are the best value a refined taste can
desire for Gd. The B.L. Corto Rothschild cigars
arc now stocked by all high-class tobacconists/

Society
■ A person signing himself “Petitioner”
writes to S.M. Herald saying; “It does not
seem likely that the deep instinct of prayer which
almost universally exists, is a mockery.” Well,
what about the universal instinct of drink ? Also,
the instinct of dirt, which was practically uni-
versal until a very short time ago, and is nearly
universal even yet? Where does the tobacco-
instinct come in, and the instinct of “ immorality,”
which is a lot more general and more deep-rooted
than the praying instinct? The most universal
instincts which The Bulletin can remember just
now are drink, tobacco, profanity, dirt, ghosts,
dishonesty, making love to another fellow’s wife,
leaving things till to-morrow, prayer and lying.
And, if the universalness of one instinct croves
anything, how about the others?
• * *
The people who prayed for rain the other
day, in N.S. Wales, were wise in their day and
generation, and took no chances. They waited
patiently through weeks of drought and bush-
fires, until one morning three meteorologists—
lodd, Russell, and Wragge—all announced that
rain might be expected in a few days. That same
day they fixed their prayers for tile ensuing Sun-
day, and the rain came, and since then they have
suffered from an aggravated attack of swelled
head. One clerical gentleman even wrote to the
Herald pointing out four previous occasions
when rain came within 11 days after prayer. In
reply to him a scientific person explained that
on one of these occasions the rain was only a few
drops, and then the drought settled down once
more, whereupon they prayed again and got no
rain for four months. Moreover, even in the
worst drought it almost always rains to some
extent in some part of the province every week,
and as any rain anywhere can be quoted as an
answer to prayer, the answer can always be relied
on. The rain-making I nisi ryjs.s is very much like
tossing with a two-headed copper.

The rumor as to a confession by “ Possum ”
Dean, the now historic Sydney ferry-steamer cap-
tain, though denied by Dean himself, has greatly
tended to discredit him, and his character has
been further prejudiced by the diplomatieally-
evasive nature of the answers given to Parlia-
mentary questions on the subject. Chagrim d
Officialdom emphatically is not fighting on the
side of Dean. Dean should in an affidavit reiterate
his denial as to ever having made any such state-
ment and send it to the press.
“ Possum ” has now been promoted to a
new ship, and has been successfully summoned
for 12s. (Id. a week for maintenance by his wife,
whom the crowd loudly hooted as she left tin-
court. This is the very “peripheral neuritis” of
humorous agony. She said he poisoned her ; she
nearly hanged him, and all but buried him alive.
He can’t divorce her, and she can’t divorce him
for any Windeyeresque offence. She’s got their
child, and also alimony. He’s got promotion, so
that, after the end of the fixed alimony period,
which is six months, she’ll probably “rise” him
to £l. 0 gee-gee of matrimony, goo-goo of parent-
hood, but ye are strange monsters !

News that Lady Brassey is an ardent
wheelist has set many Toorakiennes a-praetising
the uncertain “bike.” The Bourke-street Rotunda
holds quite a bevy of leading social lights every
day struggling to get a dignified balance on the
wobbly wheel. The only male present at these
giddy functions is the trainer, and he, poor man,
must have a lively time. He is not allowed to lay
hands on Toorakia’s sacred body, so he has rigged
up a wooden handle arrangement fixed under the
seat wherewith he keeps the wriggling dame steady.
The momentous question as to how much shall
remain uncovered will be decided after note has
been taken of what is displayed by the wife of the
Golden Calf, which, though it has not yet arrived,
Victoria is already so assiduously worshipping.
Gov. Brassey is believed to be worth at least six
uillions sterling.
* # «
News of Baron Brassey is cabled to Mel-
bourne from every port where he stops to take in
coal and fresh provender. The other day he rested
his craft at St. Helena, and the Governor of that
dismal rock gave him a grand banquet, consisting
probably of idled leg o’ mutton and trimmings.
There isn’t much pomp and magnificence at St.
Helena. The place has no population worth men-
tioning, bar his Excellency and suite, who (when
not out fishing) spend most of their time in gazing
at Napoleon Buonaparte’s tomb and wishing they
were dead.
There has been in Melbourne a bicycle
church-parade, attended by 200 riders. Very con-
ducive to devotion, this pious rallying of the Calf-
and-Breastbone brigade. The man who will goto
church on a bicycle is the sort of person who will
typewrite his love-letters.

(fovt. House, Melb., wants refurnishing
badly. The stuff at present filling the rooms is
old-fashioned and shabby. It was bought in the
early Loch days, and was not conserved by the
Hopetoun children—two boys, with destructive
instincts that ought to make them useful Radicals
some day. It is scarcely likely that Brassey and
Mrs. B. will be content to squat about on chairs
with the stuffing bulging out of them, and every-
thing looking like a second-hand shop. Perhaps
they will take pity on a hard-up province, and re-
furnish the place themselves. At present, all
that is being done to Govt. House is to repaint
the outside gates.

9 * *
An aristocratic drunk, whose Bacchanalian
vagaries are the subject of numberless yarns,
wandered over from Melb. Princess’s Theatre to
the Burke and Wilis monument the other night.
It was pitch dark, and after resting against the
granite base for half-an-hour, he concluded to
move onward. Feeling with his hands carefully
before him he traversed one side of the plinth,
carefully turned the corner, moved onwards again
to the next corner, and so on until he had walked
completely round the stone. Then with a super-
human effort he made another circuit. After a
rest of 10 minutes he repeated the exercise, and in
an hour and three-quarters had performed 24
revolutions. Then, utterly exhausted, lie lifted
up a weird cry. “Oh! the blankers have locked
me in ! ” and sank in a limp heap to the pavement.
- # •
One of the deah boys of a big Maoriland
squatting family married about a year ago, and
the day preceding the ceremony called to take
leaye of the Hebe officiating at a city liar. On
arriving, however, the prospective benedict found
a brother of the lady on hand, the hitter being of
opinion that his sister had not been fairly treated
by the man of flocks and herds. A short dis-
cussion on the subject led to somebody getting
“ stoushed,” and 20 minutes of hard “dealing it
out’’ resulted in two black eyes and numerous
minor contusions on the countenance of the bride-
groom-elect. A hansom was summoned, and the
sufferer was conveyed post-haste to an eminent
surgeon, who worked all night, and hang the
expense, fixing up the gilded one’s face for the
ceremony. With the aid of surgery and grease-
paint, he was brought to a condition that none
but the front row of the church and the immediate
participators in the affair could distinguish from
normal, but the blushing bride, one of Maoriland’s
prettiest girls, had the unique experience of honey-
mooning with a husband with two black eyes.

Dr. Lloyd. J.P., the ma rvied- worn an’s
friend of N. Melb. court, lamented, during the
hearing of a recent wife-beating case, that the
law did not allow him to prescribe a dose of cat:
I suppose this sort of tiling will go on till lialf-a-dozen
murderous husbands have been hanged, and then the re-
mainder will quiet down.
But murderous husbands have been hanged times
out of mind, and the wife-beater is still here. For
thousands of years people have been hanged and
otherwise slaughtered for doing the things which
they continue to do to-day quite regardless of the
alleged deterrent. Experience does not teach fools
wisdom, for some continue to murder—and others
to advocate hanging.

There is a clamor in N.S.W. backblocks
for more J.sP., and the Colonial Secretary pro-
poses to add to the 4000 and odd now on the roll.
Mr. Brunker might find time to enquire and
strike a few useless items off. Notably one in
Sydney, whom no seasoned policeman will run in.
A new-chum constable occasionally gets hold of
him under the “ vag.”—only to be told by the
watch-house recorder that he, the N.C.0., is a
fool; result, that policeman never runs-in that
J.P. again. Another backblocks J.P., now in the
city, lives chiefly on borrowed 3d. bits. Justices
of this type had a good time in Sydney before the
Stipendiaries were appointed. They had only to
offer to sit at one of the city courts to be imme-
diately bought off by some of the great unpaid
with the price of a pint of beer.
There is to be seen in a
Melbourne shop - window a
machine designed for use by
ghosts who may be desirous of
imparting their opinions on
things in general to a fleshly
world. It consists of a revolving
disc with two pointers, and an
external circle on which appear
the alphabet and numerals, and
the words “ Yes ” and “ No.”
This saves the ghost the trouble
of counting off the letters by
raps. All lie has to do is to jab
at the machine and stop it when
the right letter is under one of
the pointers. If it isn’t under
that one it may happen to be
under the other, or something of
that kind. The advantages of
the machine are obvious, and no
well-equipped ghost should go
on'tour without one. It is pre-
sumably of ghostly manufac-
ture, though not bearing a label
to that effect. Anyhow, the
ghost that is muscular enough
to throw tables about ought not
to be stumped at a bit of easy
carpentering work. Here is a
new danger to the sweated
toiler. Ghosts can live much
cheaper than anyone else, as
they don’t require any food
beyond an occassional box of
chocolate-creams.

Charity-secretary Maxted
has urged on the directors of
Sydney Benevolent Asylum the
grave necessity for an alteration
of the N.S. W. law of affiliation,
“ in the direction of compelling
support by fathers from or
before birth.” Which recalls
something to the present un-
regenerate scri be. Fifteen years
ago, in the Queensland sugar-
country, a young white man
was courting a comely white
housemaid belonging to his
boss’s plantation. The house-
maid aforesaid, having made a
certain communication to Mrs.
Sugar Planter, Mr. Planter put
it to the young man that he
had better marry Mary if he
didn’t want to lose his excellent
billet. The young man did
marry Mary, and when the little
stranger arrived it proved to be
a half Kanaka. There are, after
all, one or two reasonable arguments in favor of
the eash-on-delivery system.

vert the N.S.W. shearers by means of services and

to 21 years. The pious political hen is jealous of
grog, jealous of smoke, jealous of young women.
If she could, she would raise the age of consent to
her own age—say, about 54.

Melbourne has many people willing to be
martyred in the anti-vaccination cause. The
other day, F, J. Sincock, solicitor, accepted three
days’ gaol rather than pay a 10s. fine for refusing
to have his child vaccinated. His conscience
would not permit him to pay the fine, he said.
Lawyers usually have their consciences more
under control.
* * *
Now that the daily newspapers of Aus-
tralia have abandoned separate cable services, or
services representing a two or three combiim,
what moral or party principle or business prospect
hinders those in one city agreeing to the employ-
ment of a single reportorial staff? The point has
struck newspaper managers before to-day, and,
in a tentative sort of way, excursions in that
direction have been made, but the fear of some
undefined evil overshadowed the experiments.
The amalgamated cable-service will, if it succeeds,
give these enterprising spirits hearts of hope, and
some day we shall see one reporter only where
four now earn bread and butter.

DUDLEY WYATT, the Calculating Boy.
A serious-looking man in a black suit last
week entered the office of an Australian daily,
and, accosting a reporter, asked for the editor.
The sub-editor, leader of the staff, and the pro-
prietor being all “ out,” the serious man turned to
the reporter himself. “Well, perhaps you’ll do,”
he began, producing a piece of paper from his
pocket. “Last Monday a young fellow came to
my parsonage—l’m a clergyman—Rev. Zedediah
Jagson is my name—and he had a young woman—
a rather comely young woman—with him, and he
said, ‘My name’s Jobkins, and I’m a compositor
on the Daily Tinpot, and this lady’s my young
woman, and we want to be married.’ Then I
married them in the usual manner; and after that
Mr. jobkins asked me what my fee was. I replied
it was a guinea. ‘ But that’s far too little for
such a happy occasion,’ Mr. Jobkins remarked—
‘ Let me write out a cheque for £5 ss.’ And be
did, and this is the cheque he gave me ; it’s been
dishonored—marked ‘No account.’ Do you know
Mr. Jobkins? I don’t think it was right for him
to give me a cheque like that, do you? So I want
to see whether your editor will give me the £5 ss.
Do you think he will ? ” Of course the editor did.

Jeimings-Carmichael mercifully refrains
from dragging the eternal Southern Cross into her
poetic discourse. She has discovered, no doubt,
that the Cross is the most absurdly overrated of
Australia’s national institutions. Who first began
to blow about the S.C., anyway?
# # *
Melbourne's moribund Institute of Jour-
na ists has held a meeting to consider what ought
to happen to M.sP. who occasionally kick the
press. The precious institute doesn’t seem to
know that it invites the contumely of Hancock
ami Co. Every reporter abases himself before tin
gods in the ’Ouse or elsewhere. The gabble of the
member for Muckborough is quoted as though be
were the Voice of Heaven; a semi-insolvent soft-
goods importer is styled a “merchant prince’ by
the ungrammatical interviewer who crawls back-
wards from his presence; a muddle-headed mayor’s
luncheon is paragraphed until each person who
guzzled freo at the festivity imagines himself a
pillar of the universe. And yet the instituted
journalists object to being bruised by the boots
they are so fond of buttering. Eatted pigs have
good reason to despise their greasers.

Somebody who has forgotten all that he
ever knew about the circumstance, writes to
Melb. Herald re an old-time proposal to place
Marcus Clarke’s widow on the Victorian pension-
list. The misleading person says “an appeal was
made some years ago on Mrs. Clarke’s behalf,
when that large-hearted, though small-brained,
couple, Service and Patterson, were endeavouring
to control affairs, and they promised Parliament
that some arrangement should be made, instead of
grantingapension.” Gillies, thesuccessortoService,
controlled affairs in those days, whilst the “large-
hearted” Patterson distinguished himself by snort-
ing at Marcus Clarke’s memory, on the ground that
“the country wanted thinkers, not scribblers.
The meat-man beat his chest as he mentioned
“thinkers,” just to let the country know that one
living gas-bag was worth more to posterity than
99 dead novelists.

Acting on advice he gave himself in charge to a

with the mondaine Oliva posing as Marie
Antoinette.

Three years ago a Sydney lad
passed the N.S.W. pupil-teachers’ exam,
with credit, but received notification that
there was no vacancy for him. He forth-
with found employment as a clerk, begin-
ning at 10s. a week, which salary was raised
to 20s. and then to 305., and at Xmas he
is promised a further increase to £2. A
few days ago he received an official letter
appointing him to a school at 15s. per
week (minus 4 per cent, and cleaning-
fees). The young man, now 20, declined
with thanks. Evidently the Dept, had
expected him to gladly wait three years
for 15s. a week ! Lots do.

Dear Bulletin, —lie the conten-
tion that Jessie Nicholls was killed by the
scorn of her own sex. Such scorn affects
women very little, if not reinforced by
the neglect of the other sex. Few, though
willing to marry a widow, will propose to
a girl with “an encumbrance.” Besides,
there is a practical aspect to the question.
The support of an illegitimate child falls
upon the mother, while the father, who
ought, under the present arrangement of
society, to make provision for his own
child, can repudiate the whole responsi-
ble ty, unless forced to share it by the
affiliation law, which is seldom appealed
to, either from fear of the cruel exposure
in court or the woman’s love for her
deserter. Undoubtedly abortion is the
best alternative for the woman whose
love has overcome her instinct of self-
preservation, and it will be resorted to
while the present cruel conditions last,—
Ware a.
Here is an anomaly in the N.S. W. “Act
for the Supervision of Dairies ” ; A struggling
milk-retailer selling the produce of even only one
cow, has to satisfy all the stringent requirements
of the Act before he can sell one pint of milk; and
is also subject to regular inspection. On the
other hand, a dairyman—no matter how large his
establishment —is free from the operation of the
Act so long as he does not retail his milk or cream,
but converts it into butter. If it is necessary to
supervise the sale of milk, why should not the
same precautions be taken in the case of butter?
In the latter case, the spirit of the Act is evaded by
technicalities which need immediate attention.

A breach of promise case, talked about in
London, pivots on the sweetheart seeing his
fiancee one night painting her face before the
mirror in a strong gaslight. He was passing on
the opposite side of the street, and so much
aversion did her gestures and grimaces arouse,
that he asserts he “loathed her from that minute.”
Moral: Pull down the blinds.
The latest photographer’s joke. Two
fashionable misses, in search of novelty, were
piloted with their heads stuck through a shee.t of
Sydney Evening News. The girls fainted clean
away when the proof came home. Right across
the sheet was a splash clothier’s advt., with the
black-letter information, ‘‘Our pants are made
extra strong in the seats !’’ The ladies blushingly
decided upon a fresh sitting.
By the way, The Bulletin notes the appear-
ance in above par. of a new verb —the_ verb to
phote, and passes it as a distinct acquisition to
the English language. “Phote” the noun and
“phote” the verb are a great improvement on
“ photograph,” and even on “photo.”—just as
“phone” is a sensible abbreviation of “tele-
phone.” Also, this paper directs the attention of
Australian advertisers to the ’cute American prac-
tice of omitting the word “street,” and saving
time and money. “302 George” and “419
King ” are intelligible by everybody.
# * *
There is a vacancy for a preacher in Sfc.
James’s Church, Sydney, and, as is always the
case when there is a good clerical salary going,
the billet has been offered to a high-toned parson
in Hingland. The Australian church-goer is con-
vinced that only the Rev. J. Bull can communi-
cate successfully with the Deity, and whenever
there is a good enough salary to induce that
person to come out he gets the billet. If the
salary is so small that the Hinglish toiler in the
vineyard won’t look at it, then the miserable
Australian gets a show. Evidently the idea that
the Deity speaks with a strong Cockney accent
still prevails in this country. Next time_ N.S.W.
wants prayers for rain the Australian cleric should
go on strike, and tell the people to cable to Eng-
land and get a superior kind of British prayer
offered up there. If he is such a lamentably
inferior article, then his petition for moisture
surely can’t amount to much.
# * #
The Jessie Nicholls tragedy, like the
Deeming case, and the Dean baby-murders, and
almost every other sensational horror on record,
is an essay on the utter want of originality com-
monly displayed in getting rid of “the body.”
Anyone who knows how to effectually get rid of
the body can do murder with impunity so long as
he takes a few simple precautions. The body is
the beginning, middle and end of the case every
time ;no jury would convict without it; no Attor-
ney-General would prosecute if it couldn’t be
found ; and in 99 cases out of 100 there would be
no shadow of suspicion but for the discovery of it
clumsily stowed away in a box, or under a hearth,
or floating in the water, or in some other equally
foolish place. Many assassins are evidently
adepts at getting rid of the body, as witness the.
large number of unaccountable disappearances,
some of which must almost inevitably be due to
violence; but still the ignorance that exists with
regard to this comparatively simple subject is
amazing. The Bulletin has four different
schemes, each one of which is simple, speedy, and
so absolutely effective that not a vestige of the
deceased would remain. This paragraph may
help at some future date to account for the utter
evanishment of —well, several people.
LOVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wealth, joy, and radiant happiness,
Dear love, be never thine,
Unless in thy lot’s loneliness
Such blessings I entwine :
May Heaven vouchsafe to thee no friend
(The dearer shall ./ be) ;
No joy upon thy path descend
Until it come through me :
And should great griefs thy life befall
Ere many years elapse.
It warms my heart to think of all
I’ll do for thee—perhaps !
M.L.
C. CLYDE.

Ladies who value a clear skin and fresh
complexion, and who appreciate the possession of
clean, white teeth and a sweet breath, should
follow the example of their European sisters and
indulge in the use of Amykos. After being out
in the hot sun, or on a dusty road, nothing is more
cooling or refreshing than a little Amykos
sprinkled in the water in which you lave your
face and mouth. One does not require to be
using it all day long, but those who have experi-
enced its benefits say that the morning ablutions
are certainly not complete without it. Purchase
a sample bottle from your chemist.*

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
September 24, 1895.
My Dear Moorabinda—
I’d love to run my fingers reverently through
Mark Twain’s silky grey hair, worn d !a Benno
Scherek, whom M.T. somewhat resembles. He
might think it’s the custom of the country which
almostgrew the veteran Parkes, and bear with mein
consequence as one upon whom hair had an unfor-
seen effect. I prefer Mark’s pretty hair to his
eyes, which he kept half-closed at his first “at
home’’—probably to shut out the hideous spec-
tacle of a large Sydney audience so painfully on
the jump after jokes, that it’s a miracle the inimi-
table pathos of Hack Finn and the escaping slave
got the sympathetic hearing it did ; though one
scarcely blames the fixed grin with which Sydney
settled down to hear Mark Twain after a roar of
welcome which nearly lifted the building off its
four feet. Because Mark’s style is calmly, merci-
lessly comical—his mode of talking recalling
Monkhouse in his most unctuous moments as Dr.
Brierley— I never heard anything more like Monk-
house than Mr. Clemens’ drawling remark,
“Patience is such a virtue.” This tonal resem-
blance to the large and suggestive ’Arry makes
one at first suspect that there will be a double-
entente wrapped up in every sentence d la the come-
dian—but there isn’t, which may account for
the early departure of several fat men on the first
night after they had gazed their fill at the ’Murri-
can literary marvel. I think Mark quite fills the
eye as a lion. Small, spare, nervous, and thickly-
roaned, with a platform manner quite his own, he
ready looks Mark Twain. Thank heaven !
I sorted out Mrs. and Miss Clemens in the huge
audience partly because of the strong facial re-
semblance Miss C. bears to her father, and partly
because they sometimes forgot to smile at the lec-
turer’s jokes. You can always spot a lecturer’s
relations by the I’ve-heard-it-all-before apathy
they bring to bear on the case. Miss C. is a dis-
tinctly pretty girl, with ink-black hair and large,
dark eyes. She wore a black skirt with a yellow
silk blouse and black gloves—the lady who has the
honor to be Mrs. Clemens wearing a black silk
gown. They were supported by the Much-
Travelled, who fairly basked in the success of the
occasion.
The Polo Gymkhana, at Kensington, on the
18th, showed up in bas-relief the good imitation
of old Roman heartlessness inherent in Sydney
society. The steeplechase for hunters (amateurs
up) was a most unlucky race—only two nags stood
up at the finish, for all the rest came to grief.
One horse fell on its rider (Mr. M. Ford), and
pinned him to the ground—the poor animal break-
ing its hack ; another horse turned a flip-flap with
Lieut. Taylor, who was badly stunned—and the
grand-stand took all these casualties in the most
cheerful spirit in the world. Vapours are
no longer d la mode, and the swooning,
weeping variety of female is a hack-number.
Your pain and suffering have to be specially
hideous to corral the sympathy of a “ministering
angel ” who coolly turns away from the sight of a
man doubled up under a horse, and merely flutters
the leaves of her race-book to see what’s the next
show. Even the debutantes took the severe
croppers in a manner so stoical that a jockey’s
wife would blush at it. If it’s the incoming Gov.
Hampden’s humor to start bull-baiting, or sooling
the Tantanoola tiger on to the Salvation Army,
I can promise him lots of audience.
There must be two tutelary geniuses of fashion
—one for Jack and one for Jill, or else how was it
that nearly all the women at the polo wore bright
attire, while the men came anyhow? One patri-
cian—closely connected with a brand-new Earl-
looked a sketch in a creased water-tosh, oyster-
colored linen, a red tie, a round, cork-colored hat,
and the most pronounced basin-crop on the lawn;
though the funniest man was a sort of adjutant-
bird in cerulean blue, with his trousers carefully
turned up over a pair of buttercup boots. By the
way, I don’t think the recent prayers for rain in
the churches (and don’t they wish they could keep
it there so as to make the heathen come raging to
the sacred pile in order to get a dipperful!) are
responsible for the late refreshing drizzles we’ve
enjoyed. I think we have the Johnnies to thank
for it, who piously kept the pedal hems of their
nether garments folded up beyond high-mud-mark,
even when the “ droot ” was at its driest. Evi-
dently Providence was touched by such a mute
expression of good faith.
There was a large vice-regal party at the Gym-
khana. Acting vice-regal parties are apt to look
more swollen than the other sort, though it does
look awfully like making the must of things d la
the crowd in a hired buggy on a public holiday.
The cynosure of the V.R. box was Lady Holled-
Smith—a blonde, Jenny Watt-Tannerish per-
sonage who was tricked out in sea-green alpaca
coat and skirt, with revers of pale-green silk
brocaded in pale-pink roses ; a small hat trimmed
with pink and green ; and a white veil over her
complexion of Lady-Carington pink. Lady Smith
(no relation of mine, remember) is an attractive
sort of woman possessing a striking optical
peculiarity which, if she becomes a leader of
fashion, will probably set Southern society gazing
steadily at the tip of its nose in order to encourage
a similar effect. Australia’s biggest heiress turned
up in a rubbishy-looking dark-blue suit and a lame
black hat trimmed with pink. Contrasting her
appearance with that of poorer but more stylish
women, it really looks as if the worst misfortune
that can happen to a woman, as a woman—from
Daniie right up to date—is a shower of gold.
One spectreish lady had red hair and a
grey suit with a Tattersall - check vest, and
showed the white feather in her hat; and a society
lady, temporarily on crutch-sticks, unwisely wore
a red velvet gown, as if afraid of not attracting
sufficient attention.
The military element is always strong at polo
sports ; and talking of the military reminds me
that whenever a certain spoffish officer had occasion
to don his regimental kilt, he always spent hours
beforehand in a sunny place with his two nude
knees exposed to the glare, trying to get a manly
bronze on ’em for the occasion. It was the same
seasoned old warrior (in a Pickwickian sense) who
attended a ball in the tartan petticoat of Scotland,
and was discovered “ sitting out ” in a corridor of
the Town Hall, with his own handkerchief tied
round one knee and his partner’s round the other 1
He found it “jolly cold,” so he said without
hesitation, though the lady sat uncomplaining in
a slip of a satin bodice and a necklace. But, when
you come to think of it, a lady “sitting out” in
a dark corner always gets sheltered from the
breeze in a most miraculous manner.
The Liedertafel concert at Sydney Town Hall
on the 18th had girls’ voices in the game for the
first time, with the result that the uniqueness of
the society was a shattered thing, and one feels
pleased to record that the choristresses won’t
always be included. The concert suggested the
resurrection of the petered-out Philharmonic —the
ghostly-robed women aiding the effect. The
damsels also suggested a tidal-wave of femininity
which beat the Liedertafel youths right back
against the organ, where they clung in a thin line
like sea-weed. There was a perfect herd of girls.
Evidently, if it takes nine tailors to mi e a man,
it requires ten female voices to balance one male
pipe! I thought it rather ungallant on the part
of the management that they should have burst
out into unwonted decorations. Less trimming
would have been a greater compliment—as it was,
the artistic edging of palms and thick ferns looked
as if the aim was to hide the choristresses’ feet.
I thought the girls sang their one unsupported
item in a painstaking, churchy sort of way,
though, mind you, it was encored ; after which
the Superintendent gave each good little Mary a
bun in a bag—but stop ! I am mixing this notice
with one I mean to give the Y.W.C.A. The
cream of the show was an Italian romance wildly
played by the Liedertafel. It went with a rush
and a swirl like a Melb. Cup set to music. I
wasn’t much impressed with the champion piece,
Olaf Trygvasen. Grief is not to be satisfactorily
interpreted by the first chorus you come across,
and the ’ohestra was too loud for the soloists. A
new baritone, Mr. Noble, debuted. He is a
“ roarer ” —in horsey parlance—his breathing was
beard half-way down the hall, oven if his voice
wasn’t. Edgar Strauss and a pupil played a ’cello
duet with great success, and that was all there
was of the programme. The huge audience wasn’t
altogether up to Sydney Liedertafel standards,
the mixed state of affairs in front being well-
expressed by an agony ad. in a daily next morn-
ing requesting dark gentleman who winked at tall
blonde at the Liedertafel concert to communicate
with Flossie—or words very much to that effect.
The introduction of ladies seems to have a de-
moralising effect.
Ashfield Town Hall was all agog over the per-
formance of “H.M.S. Pinafore” by a strong
band of capable amateurs, stage-managed and
generally shaken up by Miss Josephine Deakin,
who played Josephine in two very special gowns
and a blaze of Parisian diamonds, and sang her
soprano music with professional expertness, Mr.
J. H. Rainford (Dick Dead eye) was the other pro.,,
lending experience and a good bass voice to the
scene. The cast was admirable. Even the tenor
didn’t put in worse acting than his vocal kind are
famous for, and any defects on his part were
amply atoned for by the rest. Mrs. R. Imray
made a jovial gagging Little Buttercup; Mr. W.
B. Beattie sang and acted Captain Corcoran in
tip-top style, his “Fair Moon” solo being one of
the best things in the operetta ; Mr. E. Clarke
was extremely naval as Sir Joseph, and sang his
patter humorously, and Mr. G. E. Weatherill
gave a rollicking sketch of Bill Bobstay. The
voice of the prompter was not heard in the land,
and the show went as smoothly as if the ’teurs
had been playing it for weeks. Mr. Albert Fisher
conducted the evenly-balanced orchestra.
Both Kruse and Hambourg have gone from our
midst, to the sorrow of the truly musical. The
gap will take a lot of filling, and there is a
bad time ahead for mediocrity visiting these
shores. By the way, both Kruse and Hambourg
had a bad time themselves in Sydney in one sense.
The latest society craze is the resurrected birth-
day-book, and all professionals of note suffer
under it. At the last Kruse concert and at Ham-
bourg’s farewell scintillation bevies of decolletd
damsels (Hambourg had 30!) swarmed into the
artist’s room with books, pens, and ink-pots all
ready, and absolutely tore autographs from both
the stars on separate occasions ! That they hadn’t
even been introduced didn’t trouble them one bit.
I doubt if stars are made such game anywhere as
they are here in Australia. Oscillatory demon-
strations occurred at Hambourg‘s finale, though I
haven’t heard of anybody kissing Kruse. There’s
a “ countess ” in his case, for one thing, and then
you can defend yourself better with a fiddle,
which comes in very handy sometimes as a battle-
axe.
The last flutter—Steepiechaseday—of Rand wick
Spring meeting was rather an anxious ceremony,
for there was a stiff nor r -<easter blowing, Bill, and
the many silly women, in flimsies—doctors’ de-

—all three dressed in black and white. The best-

wore a black silk striped crSpon skirt with
a blouse bodice of white satin and real

with silk blouse of duck-egg green brocaded with

on its head and trimmed stragglingly with red and

who, for a time, had bidden his cherished Picca-

knovv. His garments were a hand-me-down fit
with the bifurcations inches too short for grace.
Then his hat was a-cock, his moustache curved
outwards like a bullock’s horns, and last, but by
no means least, the skirts of his coat were as
round and stiff as if they had been built over a

Pretty Miss “ Mark Twain ” attended the
Steeple in a rather tired-looking garment. Mrs.
“Griff’’Jones wore a handsome gown, in which
fawn, brown and strawberry-pink were cleverly
commingled with long fawn gloves to the elbow ;
a portly society woman wisely wore black, but
spoilt the good effect of the paring-down color with
a pair of huge sleeves of a bold pattern, which
made her resemble a captive balloon ; a girl in
Coolgardie “yaller” spotted with black suggested
that the flies had got into the butter, and the trail
of the ingenious Blanche Massey fichu was over a
great many maidens. The feature of the races
was the preponderance of women—women, women
everywhere with not a male to wink, as the poet
almost says. The debutantes stood around,
apparently wondering what on earth mamma had
brought ’em out for, and the enthusiasm with which
women took one another in to afternoon-tea
suggested a tentative experiment in woman’s
right to “shout buns” for her circle. The
visiting contingent came very short of men
this spring—plenty of unsupported petticoats to
add to the already large array on hand locally. I
hear rumors that a well-known society widow of
many thousands is going into double-harness, but
that appears to be all the matrimony on the stocks
—barring the engagement (in Brisbane) of Miss
Finney to an acting aide-de-camp.
“ Ladies’Day,” at Randwick (Thursday), was
remarkable for the absence of bold people on the
Stand. The Jewish Penitential Day was occurr-
ing, and the Chosen were away repenting vigor-
ously. But how short-sighted to fix any event of
a hilarious character without due regard to the
religious exigencies of Hebraism 1 Why on earth
should the Gee-Gees have farewelled on the even-
ing of a Jewish Penitential Day—which day the
dunderheaded A.J.C. committee also fixed upon
to run the Craven Plate 1 A glance round the
Lyceum on any first night will show you what a
monetary loss such want of thought gives rise to 1
The Royal Comic Opera Co. got individually a
warm welcome at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday
from a crowded house full of cackling theatre-
parties of the very best and noisiest people, in-
cluding representatives from Guv’raentouse ; and
everybody felt glad to know that the season of
operatic coherency had dawned again, beginning
with the Frenchy “Ma Mie Rosette,” Although
the co. only arrived from Melbourne at noon the
same day, everybody’s voice seemed to have a
good edge on it. The only serious objection to be
taken to Miss Pollard’s appearance in the pink
garment of “ Rosette ” was that she couldn’t play
Segur at the same time—for though Muriel Carr
filled the part very brightly, yet the faithful can’t
help thinking of Miss P.’s artistic outlines in
those pale-pink hosen. Miss Pollard takes Rosette
very seriously—giving the audience a little too
much back-hair, perhaps, and apparently adding
more petticoats to the part than Nellie did, though
May gets a more graceful effect out of the
bunchy draperies of imminent guilt than Nellie
was wont to extract.
Wallace Brownlow made his usual hawk-like
success of the bold, bad monarch whom Florence
Young (looking very well indeed as Corisandre)
has at last learnt not to address as “ Ongriand
his singing was the feature of the opera. I hear
that Mr. B.’s voice has extended in both direc-
tions since he had his throat trimmed in Sydney.
The opera season is to be one of quick changes of
familiar operas, for the novelty in rehearsal is to
be bottled up for Melbourne Cup-week. By the
time these sapienoies get into print, Miss Flegel-
taub (who is not related to Flo. Young, but is
first cousin or something to a family of well-known
amateur musicians of Sydney) will be cutting
through the air as Clairette Angot.
Writes my Melb. correspondent:—
“ Four Melb. society girls have gone in search
of adventure and novelty to the South Sea
Islands —which seem to have taken the place of
the M.L. Sounds as a fashionable summer-trip.
Interest has been awakened in them by the vivid
stories and sketches of R. L. Stevenson and Louis
Becke.
“Miss Jennings Carmichael debuted as a
lecturess in Melb. the other night, and although
the centre ornament might have been flanked by a
longer introductory address from Alfred Deakin,
on the one side, and a more substantial concert on
the other, the tootonsombull was a mildly dazz-
ling success.
“The poetess, in a simple white gown of crinkly
cr£pon with a spray of wattle-blossom on the
bodice—no jewellery—and her dusky hair daintily
dressed, might have stood for a portrait of the
Bush Spirit as she sees it. Her voice carried well,
and her soulful earnestness seemed to strike young
Australia on a soft place. Jennings Carmichael
talked musically, in prose and verse, of the buslds
better nature. The rising generation cottoned to
the idea that Gordon and Marcus Clarke were
libellers of the joyous gum-tree and the festive
scrub, and when the poetess let herself out on the
subject of the wattle the balcony cried aloud.
“ The Bush Spirit’s resemblance to Lord Hope-
toun’s black-eyed wife was probably her strongest
claim to the Toorak vote. Apropos of the like-
ness, a year or two ago, as Miss Carmichael and a
humble pressman were leaving the Princess’s, a
vigilant flunkey flung open the R’yal-oarriage
dour, prepared to receive Her (vice) Majesty ! The
face ol thernistaken menial as he beheld the lady-
bard walking off witli a common mortal in a cut-
away coat was a thing to remember. For the
moment he feared that his gracious Governoross
was doing a mash,
“ Austral Salonites, for some mysterious reason,
stayed away from ‘The Spirit of the Bush.’ Per-
haps the vision, who is not of this world, worldly,
had managed to bruise the committee’s corn with-
out knowing what she did. All the same, there
were some graceful tributes from the less cul-
chawed masses—areal boomerang trimmed with
Australian flowers, a laurel wreath, and a lyre;
the latter having been ordered by some far-away
Queensland admirers.
“Pianist Hambourg, despite his eye-troubles
and dropsical tendencies, seems more good-natured
than most prodigies. At the end of his first
Melb. visit, he received a letter from a girl of 10,
saying how much she admired his playing and
promising to practise diligently for the next six
years, so that at 16 (Hambourg’s present age) she
might equal his proficiency. The damsel’s reward
was a prompt reply from her hero, who thanked
her for her letter and hoped that on his return to
Melb. she would not only attend his concerts, but
visit the artist’s room, as he was anxious to meet
her.
“ Apropos of the furore aroused by Hambourg’s
rendering of a Chopin polonaise, the story goes
that at the height of the fuss the excited per-
former rushed up to the halo-headed Scherek,
exclaiming rapturously, ‘ Kiss me, kiss me! 1
When the ‘ society ’ conductor refused the caress,
poor little Mark complained tearfully that his
own dear papa wouldn’t have been so unsym-
pathetic. This was too much for Scherek, who
turned away, a prey to the horrid suspicion that
the short-sighted boy, misled by that chic silver
mane, actually regarded him as a contemporary of
Hambourg pere.
“ A Southern Acting-Governor, with a quiver-
ful of buxom daughters, must feel that his guber-
natorial term has been doubly blessed if, as is
generally rumored, both his departing A.sD.C.
are applying to be taken on as sons-in-law.
“At a recent Australian vice-regal ball the
daughters of a very high official ‘ sat down ’ half
the evening. This phenomenon is credited to the
influence of a society league, having for its object
the insulation of the damsels in question until
they recover from the intermittent shortness of
vision and memory to which they are alleged to
have been more or less subject ever since pa’s
accession to power.
“Dr. Kirkland, one of the Catterthun survivors,
ascribes his safety to the strength of his arms.
As the seas dashed over the wreck, it was all he
could do to hold on, and, time and again, he
realised that, with a fraction less muscle-power,
he would have been worsted in the fight. The
unlucky Dr. Anderson, on the other hand, is
described as a fragile young fellow, physically un-
fit to escape from that veritable murder-hole, a
ship’s cabin with its door jammed tight by the
weight of water in the passage outside.

“ The Melb. wedding, at which the bridegroom
was 50 years older than his bride, was not sanc-
tioned by the presence of the latter’s father. Th s
incorruptible patriarch carried his opposition i<
the union of May and December to the point of
refusing to engineer marriage-settlements for In.-
daughter, who, nevertheless, took the risk without
apparent anxiety.
“At present debutantes part their well-frizzed
tresses down the centre, droop them slightly over
the ears and finish off with a coil on the back ol
the neck. The fashion of parted hair is chiefly
responsible for the demure Madonna-like expres
sion so noticeable in pictured damsels of the early
thirties, though, to-day, the hallowing effect of
the thin white line is minimised by fluffy ban-
deaux, which represent the sleek ear-weepers of 5U
years agone.
“These hard times it is refreshing to hear of
anyone making money against his will. Such is
the pleasing predicament of an up-country doctor,
whose district includes the native heath of almost
all Victoria’s wealthiest equal ting clans. In addi-
tion to general practice, this favorite of Fortune
receives yearly retainers from local magnates to
ward off disease frjm themselves and families,
which entails weekly visits to the show stations o.‘
the province, with lunch, afternoon-tea, and
dinner engagements for almost every day in
the week. Desirous of more leisure, Medicus a
well-preserved bachelor and effective carpet-
knight—has often engaged assistants, and even
induced rival practitioners to settle in the neigh
borhood, but to no purpose. His patients,
especially the ladies, relentlessly boycott th <
new-comer until he leaves in disgust.
“J. M. Davies, late Minister for Justice, recently
defeated when seeking re-election to the Legisla-
tive Council, suffers from the unpopularity of his
clan. Many people condemn him as part and
parcel of the execrated Matthew ; others con-
fuse him with another brother, J. B. Davies,
whose insolvency led to the recent Molesworth
controversy. Asa matter of fact, the ex-M.L.C.,
the eldest and most weather-beaten of the boom
brothers, has, since the smash, lived in retirement,
and devoted almost the whole of his legal earn-
ings to paying off his enormous calls. So far, he
has made no attempt to seek the shallow water of
Insolvency Harbour, which, at all events, is a
great point in his favor.”
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

The Kangaroo gazed in astonishment mild,
At the Man and the Camera blended !
The Bull with the focussing cloth got wild ;
And the Artist’s career was ended.

Personal Items.
Juliet Wray is so thin that it is actually
said she scratches the chairs she sits upon.
The three last “ Gents One ” haled before
Vic. Law Institute are all prominent pietists —
two of them churchwardens in the suburbs where
they reside.
Dr. Grant, a Presbyterian Minister, last
week celebrated at Nowra (N.S.W.) the sixtieth
anniversary of his ordination. The doctor is now
in his 94th year.
A certain notorious Australian social feud
is said'to have originated as far back as ’B7, in a
struggle for the possession of Jubilee Juggins
Benzon. Hence many things.
He Justice Williams's “New Religion,” a
brother Judge says ; “There are only two faults
to be found with it; first, that it is not new, and,
secondly that it is nob religious.”
Two ex-Supreme Court and three ex-Dis-
tricb Court Judges draw pensions, totalling £5890
per annum, from N.S.W. Treasury. The length
of their service varied from 38 to 6 year
A MELB. SMOKE-NIGHT IMPRESSION.
A "Lightning” Sketcher —Leon Pole.
Plutocrat J. H. Angas, the richest South
Australian, will be 72 on Oct. 5. Ex-Chief Justice
Villeneuve Smith, of Tasmania (whose son is one
of the brightest of S.A. lawyers), will be 76 on
Oct. 3.
Q. Justice Harding died intestate, which
recalls Spenlow, the solicitor, in “ David Copper-
field.” Better perhaps than Lord Chancellor St.
Leonards, whose intricate will was shattered by
the Courts.
The Rev. Donald Fraser, Presbyterian
minister at Newcastle, is suing his neighbor, the
Rev. C. Bell, of Hamilton, for alleged slander,
and wants £5OOO damages. “How these Chris-
tians love one another! ”
Eugene, the
bookmaker, who
turned prophet
and led his fol-
lowers into the
interior of Africa
to avoid a tidal
wave and earth-
quake which were
to wipeMaoriland
off the map, is
prospering as a
“clairvoyant and
magnetic healer”
at Johannesburg.
Millionaire
Tyson, who owns
more cattle than
any other person
on this continent,
stated, in a cattle-
duffing case heard
at Ipswich (Q.) the
other day, that he
could recognise
any of them.
Taken out to the
court-yard toiden-
tify certain young
ones, he unerr-
ingly selected his
own from the mob.
No amount of
cross-examination
could shake his
opinion. “I know
my cattle when I
see them, and
there’s some of
’em down there in
the yard.” The
sequel proved the
old man was right.
