A £lO investment in Westralian mining
shares by Ada Crossley, including an interest in
the Great Boulder, is now said to be worth £BOOO.
Lucky contralto !
Judge Boucaut (S.A.), who has been on
the Bench since Sept., ’7B, has never sentenced a
prisoner to death. Lynch, charged with the
murder of his mate (Ross) at Balaclava in August,
will be tried before him in October.
The latest version is that on his death-bed
Salmon Wilson gave the heir de facto to the Hun-
tington title a lump sum of .£lO,OOO to sign an
undertaking to abandon his claim. Whatever the
existing heir signed, however, does nob bind his
successors.
Comedian Coppin was not always a mem-
ber of Vic. Leg. Council. Once he had a seat in
the Assembly, and once suffered defeat for the
Lower Chamber. On the latter occasion he
thanked the electors, “ on Mrs. Coppin’s behalf,”
for rejecting him.
Pastor Haweis has evidently been rawed
by the snubs of Australian Evangelicals. In
London Queen he leathers out against them,
while taking care to praise everyone else. By
implication, he makes Bishop Goe out to be a
hogshead—of cold water.
A Melb. preacher has been expatiating
on Fowell-Buxton, the new S.A. Governor, and
his renunciation of the beer biz, through religious
scruples, bub didn’t say how he smashed up the
plant, turned all the beer into the gutter, and
gave away all the beer-made money.
Three Vic. Judges have their sons as asso-
ciates. Justice Hood is the latest to appoint his
“little boy.” Sons of Justices Holroyd and
Williams are accumulating legal experience as
associates of their respective pas. No young
man need ever trouble himself about selecting a
calling if his father is a Judge.
When Sturt the explorer died in ’69, he
had almost been made a K.0.M.G., and the right
to style herself “Lady” was conferred on his
widow. A similar concession has now been
granted to Mrs. Bessie Hawker, widow of the
late Geo. Hawker, M.A., M.P., of Adelaide, to
whom she was married in that city in Dec., ’45.
All the three S.A. Supreme Court judges
were once Ministers of the Crown, and Judge
Boucaut was several times Premier. Messrs.
Way and Boucaut are senior to any other judges
on the Australasian bench —the first dating from
March, 1876, and the latter from Sept., 1878. Mr.
Bundey began his judicial career in July, ’B4.
Meteorologist Wragge attributes to Sam
Griffith his position in the public service. Wragge
had sunk his private funds in unremunerative
investments, and found himself one day almost
stumped. He then offered his services to the
heads of a few Australian Governments, with the
result that Griffith set him up in a little shop of
his own.
Dear Bulletin, —What’s in a name? If
you are aiming for success in literature, a great
deal. Note a few of the names of the
successful younger men of letters at ’Ome to-day :
Rudyard Kipling, I. Zangwill, Jerome K. Jerome,
Stanley Weyman, Guy Boothby, Hornung, Conan
Doyle, Eden Phillpotts, Oscar Wilde, Anthony
Hone. The moral for the youthful aspirant is
obvious.—S.S.
A man, committed to gaol for 14 years at
Adelaide long ago for robbery under arms,
turned up in Victoria the other day with his
throat cut. He has been sewn up and is doing well.
He threw up a good business to turn bushranger,
being anxious for fame rather than riches, but his
wild career as an amateur Ned Kelly ended
ignominiously. He was too diffident to kill a
policeman.
HARD TIMES.
DEAD-BEAT MASHER : “I see you give
an extra pair of trousers free
with each suit!”
TAILOR : “Yes, sir.”
DEAD-BEAT MASHER : “ Well, I think
I’ll take the extra pair. It’s
all I can afford at present.”
It is surprising to hear of that experienced
craftsman, barrister Villeneuve Smith, of Ade-
laide, applying for a criminal-libel summons
against young practitioner George Ash—all over a
law-case, too. Ash must be something pheno-
menal to rouse the cool old bird with the eye-
glass.
Many think that Alfred Deakin’s orations
are struck off hot from the anvil of his genius,
But that they are not spontaneous is indicated by
the fact that whenever Alfred means to address
Parliament, the handsome Mrs. Deakin invariably
occupies a place in the gallery. He had a speech
bottled up in his indignant soul in re the Argus
assaults upon his friend and political godparent,
Berry, and Mrs. L). was there to hear it. But the
storm blew over, and the “ few remarks ” which
Deakin, in seconding Premier Turner’s motion,
said he should make hereafter, remained unde-
livered.
GENT ONE.
LAWYER (referring to evidence): “Have
you got it down anywhere?”
WITNESS : “ Yes, in my pocket-book.”
LAWYER : “Let me have a look.”
WITNESS : “Can’t; I’ve got £1 in it.”
Ex-Professor Jenks (Melb. University)
has just published in England a History of Aus-
tralia down to the year he left this beastly coun-
try. How like him ! Jenks would find it difnculb
to realise that Australia has had any history since
he left.
The Chief Justices—Griffith, Darley, Mad-
den, and Way—divide Australian Christianity
pretty fairly among them. Griffith is Congrega-
tional ; Darley, Church of England; Madden,
Roman Catholic ; and Way, Bible Christian.
“The mental faculties,” Countess Wacht-
meister says, “ should not be clouded by the use
of animal food.” And yet her 16 years of vege-
tarianism don’t seem to have cleared the lady up
to the moderate extent of comprehending that
Nature, which does nothing for fun, would hardly
have supplied a purely graminivorous Countess
with flesh-eating teeth and a flesh-assimilating
digestive apparatus.
Prior Vaughan is receiving a rather cool
welcome from the Northern. R.C. clergy—chiefly
because he is not an Irish Catholic, and partly
because he’s a Tory. Whereat a Northern editor,
indignant, revives the story of the Italian priest
on the Hodgkinson whose ministrations were so
badly attended by his Celtic parishioners that he
was finally driven, as a last resource, to paint the
door of his chapel green. Even that ingenious
device failed.
Geo. P. Doolette, according to the cable,
is about to marry Miss Dale, daughter of the Con-
gregational preacher who visited Australia. Doo-
lette, now in London, is an Adelaide man who
made a big rise in Brokens, and sold out at boom-
prices. Then he lost a lot of it as an amateur
pastoralist, but the Brockmans put him on to
Great Boulders, and the erstwhile fashionable
tailor, a rather popular man, is now worth at
least .£lOO,OOO.
Of the men elected in March, ’57,
to the first S.A. Assembly under Constitu-
tional Government, there still live W. H. Bur-
ford, Luther Soamraell, John Harvey, Alex.
Hay, G. M. Waterhouse (now resident in Maori-
land), Charles Bonney (the only S.A. pensioner,
now residing in Sydney) and E. Krichauff. In
addition, W. Rogers and H. B. T. Strang-
ways (now in England) were returned at bye-
elections to the same Assembly, which was dis-
solved in March, 1860.
Garrick, vacillating as ever, has finally
declined the Q. Supreme Court Judgeship, after
being gazetted. When Acting-District Court
Judge lie used to hesitate so long over his de-
cisions that the parties found it cheaper to settle
than wait. His place as Agent-General is now
not worth an hour’s purchase. Twice already he
has seen the writing on the wall. His friends are
puzzled as to why a man constitutionally lazy and
with a good income should have gone out of his
way to look for work.
Baron Von Mueller loves to relate how,
when director of Melb. Botanical Gardens, he was
one day accosted therein by a man apparently
chockful of botanical knowledge, which he longed
to impart to the world. Eor some time the
director listened with amazed interest to the
stranger’s original remarks, but when the latter
wound up by declaring that no good could come
to the Gardens until they got rid of that sanguin-
ary old Dutchman who bossed them at present,
the Baron lost his temper: “My vriendt,” he
cried, “I vosh dot blangy old Dudgeman.”
Sydney D.T. found Mark Twain drinking
cocktails at Australia Hotel bar, and reports him
as saying that he used to drink whisky with
lemon and sugar, but now prefers it without these
adjuncts. Platform Rechabites and magazine
anti-liquor writers have been in the habit of
referring to Mark as an example of the intellectual
worker who deprecates stimulants. In “Study
and Stimulants,” an interesting work which
makes good per contra reading to the Baron
Bramwell glorifiers of Bacchus, it is set down that
“ Mark Twain considers two glasses of champagne
an excellent stimulant to the tongue, but never
knew the time when he could write to Ids satisfao-
tion after drinking even one glass of wine.
True ; alcohol is not for work-time, but for after
work.
The most prominent article of furniturt
in Vic. Speaker Graham Berry’s house at St.
Kilda is a marble statue of himself, done while nt
was Agent-General in London. Upon the pedestal
is carved the grandiloquent legend : ihe Honor'
able Sir Graham Berry, K.O.M.G. And this
stands in the vain old gentleman s best Sunday
room, where he can look at it and admire himself
in marble. The big carbuncular boss, which u
the most prominent feature of Berrys head, ib
absent from the statue. None of your Grom
wellian “ warts and all ” candor about the
bebaubled Australian.

Dear Bulletin,— Your Judge Barry anec-
dote agrees with the fact that, whereas that model
of deportment was urbanity itself with the unpre-
tentious person, he was promptly down upon the
slightest vestige of “frill” —upon anybody but
himself! The “ futile and interminable ” Slack v.
Winder case got an unexpected show once, because
the opposing counsel called it so. “That is for
me to say, sir ! ” snapped Sir Redmond. “Go on,
Mr. Slack ! ” And the inexhaustible Slack went
on, rejoicing, for five mortal hours. “Judge
Barry,” the afterwards-murdered Slack used to
say, “is sui generis —he’s a lawyer and a gentle-
man ! ” —A.M.
A septuagenarian Victorian mining mag-
nate and capitalist was married last week to a
young lady, say, half-a-century his junior. The
bridegroom attended the ceremony in the old.
dingy, black, shiny suit which has served him so
long and faithfully, and immediately the final “ I
will” was spoken he limped off to a directors
meeting, leaving his blushing bride to the
care of her bridesmaids for the next couple of
hours. That’s the sort of man to become a mil-
lionaire ; the average poor devil who places his
pleasure before his business generally dies leaving
14 children to share a hypothecated .£lOO life-
policy.
Andrew Luke Byrne, a native of Sydney
aged 64 years, was buried at Rookwood on Sun-
day. The father of deceased lies buried in the
R.C. Cemetery, Devonshire-street, Sydney, and
on his tomb may be read :
To the memory of Andrew Byrne,
Who died April 22, 1863,
Aged 89 years.
He was born in Wicklow, Ireland, and arrived in thit
colony as a patriot of his country in the year 1800.
Which means that Andrew Byrne took part in the
Irish rising of 1798, and, with many others, was
exiled to Botany Bay without even the formality
of a trial.
From The Author re Mark Twain :—“li
is an open secret that Mr. Clemens has lost most
of his own fortune and part of his wife’s by the
failure of Charles L. Webster and Co. He has
also sunk thousands of dollars in an invention
from which it is not expected that he will get a
penny. The American papers used a few year*
ago to make fabulous statements as to the magni-
tude of the fortune Mark Twain had earned by
his books. They used to speak of two millions of
dollars. Certainly the humorist lived in splendid
style at Hartford, in Connecticut, where he was a
neighbor of Charles Dudley Warner. It is, how-
ever, beyond dispute that Mark Twain made
more by his pen than any other American
author. Success had an unusual effect upon
this creator of a nation’s smiles. It soured him.
He got a name for taciturnity. He would sit
smoking big cigars for hours on end without en-
riching the surrounding flow of reason with s
single remark. He used to tell his most intimate
friends that his happiest days were those he spent
as a pilot on board a Mississippi boat. His periods
of clowning and gold-digging were also productive
of good digestion and even temper. Now that
Mark Twain has to buckle to again he may re-
sume Ids old cheerfulness. There is no living
lecturer to surpass him in the interest of his per-
sonality or the engaging nature of his diction.
He is still handsome, with a splendid head set on
square shoulders, a deep chest, keen, quick
moving eyes, and hair that has been silvered by
the labors that the world has laughed at.”
“ Influenza, like the law, is no respecter ®f
persons.”— Age. This in Melbourne, Sept., 1895!! I

NOVEL METHOD OF HUNTING.
(moil OUB COESEftPOITDENT. I
Q . DUBBO, Thursday.
Sciilp-hunting has reached such act extent that
the directors of the Pastoral Board notify that no
fee will in future be' paid for any scalps exeept
natiye dog scalps. The board has just paid for
16,000 scalps month. Numbers of men are
employed on stations shooting kangaroos and
wallabies. Some kangaroo shooters use bicycles
and shoot with rifles. They go full speed, and
then make a circuit round the escaping mob, and
get other shots. The shooters can go full speed
through timber, though there are broken limbs
of trees on the ground.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Angus Cameron—“ Anxious” Cameron.
App’int Maloney. Also, De Pentheny
O’Kelly.
William M‘Millan, M.P., evidently aims
at becoming the Jimmy Service (Vic.) of N.S.W.
Things seem to be moving towards a per-
manent Russo-Chinese alliance against England
and Japan—land-powers v. sea-powers.
The anomaly of having the Chief Justice
as Acting-Governor is shown in the announcement
that Darley, C.J., will preside at next Sydney
criminal sittings.
Little Cowley, the incapable Q. Speaker,
is having a “ bad time.” He has quite lost control
of the House, and is even bullied by his god-
fathers on the front Ministerial bench.
When will the Reid Tariff be?
In the Greek Kalends,
When the last male ends.
Then will the Reid Tariff be.
Mark Twain’s “instinct teaches him that
Protection is wrong ! ” A man’s instinct teaches
him a good many things that he has afterwards
carefully to unlearn with the help of his reason—
when he has any !
Trenwith has too much brain and equa-
nimity ever to become the revolutionary leader that
some followers would like him to be. Witness his
conduct at the Snowden meeting in Melb. Town
Hall the other night.
S.A. public service reduction —increased
this year—will have the support of the Parlia-
mentary country party to avoid an increased
land-values tax. Labor M’s.P. solidly oppose,
as the reductions hit the large staff of railway
men.
Everybody starts to howl when a “lay”
politician gets preferment like that which has
come to George Dibbs, but it seems to be taken as
quite natural that political lawyers should be
raised (practically by themselves) to lucrative
judgeships. Why ?
Castlemaine, Yic., has a zoological curio-
sity in the shape of an eccentric duck that persists
in laying black eggs. The Bulletin takes it that
the black ball is an intelligent duck’s most
energetic protest against the member for Castle-
maine, J. Beef Patterson.
Premier Reid alleges that the proposal to
borrow money for the purpose of thinning forests
is “supported by the best authorities.” (“The
best grandmothers!” interjected Lyne.) Question :
Who are the best authorities on the subject of
thinning out forests with borrowed money ?
There is friction between Premier Turner
and the Yic. Defence Department in re the ap-
pointment of an adjutant to the Mounted Rifles.
The Dept, has nominated an officer; Turner
refuses to sanction the appointment. Now it is
known in well-informed military circles that the
officer nominated is Lieut. George Patterson—J.
Beef Patterson’s nephew. See ?
The river Murray al-
tered its channel recently,
taking a short line across the
neck of a peninsula on the
Southern side, and shifting
a good-sized piece of Vic-
toria into N.S.W. It cut
right across a cattle-station,
and left half the bullocks in
N.S.W., and now the owner
is expected to pay stock-
duty on the animals when he
brings them across, and as
he states that he will see the
Government boiled first, or
something to that effect, the
matter has been referred to
Melbourne for decision.
Also, there is a serious diffi-
culty about collecting taxes
on tiie escaped territory, and
the complication has grown
so bad that the Works De-
partment is taking steps to
shift the river back again.
The Cabbage Garden can’t
afford to lose any territory
these times.
Maoriland is thinking
of shutting down on her Old
Soldiers for good and all. It
is m arly 30 years now since
the last. Maori war ended,
and still the old soldiers, the
deserters, the men who were
run out of the army for mis-
conduct, the men who were
never in it at all, and the
gaudy veterans who served
in tin* forces but were never
within 200 miles of the scene
of action, keep dropping in
to collect their land-grant.
Most of the genuine warriors
got their grants long ago,
and the applicants who come
along now are mainly bogus.
The Government has rejected 1500 military im-
postors of various degrees, and, after this year, it
will probably enact that the business is definitely
iilused.
South Australia proposes to square its
finances by reducing civil service salaries to the
extent of .£30,000; taking .£15,000 off road main-
tenance; making the municipalities pay .£7OOO
towardsthesupportofthe police force; and stopping
the annual payment of <£60,000 in reduction of the
public debt.
The impending reduction of S.A. Gover-
nors’ salary from .£5OOO to £4OOO will, if it becomes
law, place Gov. Buxton in a peculiar position. It
is a rule of the Colonial Office that all posts in the
Empire carrying salaries of <£sooo and upwards
rank as first-class, while those carrying less are
second-class—and so on.
Michael Davitt—who had a private con-
ference with the N.S.W. solidarity Labor-party,
the other day, about which, so far, nothing has
appeared in the jiapers—is absolutely delighted
with the S.A. village settlements on the Murray,
and strongly urges all benevolent politicians to
become acquainted with them.
Education Minister Dalrymple had the
audacity to deny the other day that Queensland
possessed any other piebalds than aboriginals. A
visit to some of the elementary schools in the
North would show him that numbers of half-bred
Chinese children (clever ones, too) partake of the
blessing of free, secular and compulsory education.
Saturday’s announcement that certain of
the Gilbert and other South Sea islands are to
remain closed against recruiting till further
notice, really reduces to fact the suggestion of
Cecil Wilson, Bishop of Melanesia, who, in April
last, advised the appointment of close seasons for
certain islands to preserve the human game from
being exterminated.
N.S.W. Comptroller-General of Prisons
strikes out boldly in his classification of fiist-
offending subjects for the ideal of Australia
against the world. The “ colonial yard ”at Dar-
lingliurst he reserves wholly for Australian
natives, while into the “ foreign yard ” he dumps
English, Irish, Scotch, Welsh, French, Chinese
and all other nationalities.
At Townsville (Q.) a frantic Mayor
threatened to send for the police to shift an alder-
man who sp.ike of the Governor as “Mr. Nor-
man.” He didn't really send for them, however,
so it remains legally undecided whether it is a
felony in the Far North to speak of the Gov. as if
he were an ordinary person, and whether grovel
is compulsory under the Constitution.
“Paddy” Glynn, 8.A., who entered S.A.
Parliament as a Land Nationaliser in 1887, and
who was defeated, in 1890 and 1893, because he
still advocated the Georgian doctrines, was one
of the principal speakers at the National >->ss
meeting at Adelaide, Sept. 14. As a good
R.O. and a loyal Home Ruler he will look
beautiful behind the yellow (gold-color) rosette
which is now the badge of the Ass.
Railway Commissioner Eddy’s re-engage-
ment is a remarkable arrangement. N.S.W. i
bound to retain him for seven years at .£3OOO a
year, if he wants to be retained, but lie can depart
at any time on giving six months’ notice. Also,
he is to have a “ long holiday ” after four years’
service. Probably the proposed <£4ooo a year,
with a definite engagement on both sides and no
long holiday, would have been cheaper.
Attention was drawn in Bananaland Par-
liament the other day to the circumstance that it
is not the Japanese coolie who is to be feared, but
the free immigrant Jap, who leaves his country
on his own account just the same as a free Euro-
pean. Even the Northern Fat Men seem afraid
of the Japs, one of them summing up the situation
thusly:—“Our danger comes not so much from
the Japanese serf as from the competition of
Japanese capital.” Thursday Island has taught
them this.
Miss JOSEPHINE THYNNE (Mrs. Harry Plimmer).
Australian Parliaments are full of unsuc-
cessful parsons—who make conspicuously poor
legislators. The fact is that p ilitioians were left
out of the estimate for gulden halm s—and, of
course, every parson aspires most diligently to the
heavenly coronet. Couldn't.wo take example by
the most democratic country on earth (Switzer-
land), and keep these holy persons completely out
of the contamination of politics?
If N.S.W. ex-Minister Slattery can abso-
lately disprove the charges made against him
before the Civil Service Commission re the ap-
pointment as inspectors of agriculture of useless
drunks and political friends of a shady character,
who hardly knew a vegetable by sight, he should
set about it at once. If he can’t, he should be
declared incapable of again sitting in Parliament,
or holding any political office; and any other
members of the Dibbs-Slattery Cabinet who can
be shown to have concurred in the smellful pro-
ceeding should be similarly dealt with.
“ LOVE IS BLIND"
A George-street (Sydney) street-scene.
A deputation of Sydney unemployed re-
presented to Premier Reid last week that their
class now numbers between 15,000 and 20,000 men.
N.S.W. Parliament appointed a committee 36
years ago to ascertain the number of local unem-
ployed, and 3000 were found to be so circum-
stanced. Then old man Parlies, who presided
over the committee, invaded the Assembly, and
yelled with such effect that Parliament resolved
“that something must be done.” Henry Parkes
followed that up by saying that the something
should be “a moderate system of Protection to
native industries. ”
Says Melbourne Punch, in a complaint
about the anti-sweating meeting at Melb. Town
Hall; “The agitator is a man who is never
satisfied.” Also, it is worth adding, everybody
else is a man who is never satisfied. Further,
everybody is agitating, more or less energetically,
for something. The term “agitator,” however, is
only applied to the man who wants something for
the masses. When Fat Man agitates for a sub-
sidy, or a new loan, or for State assistance for his
crumbling bank, or for a railway, or Imperial
Federation, or anything large or grovelsome, he
isn’t an agitator. An agitator, in short, is the
other fellow.
Chaffey Baker, M.L.0., presiding genius
of S. A. Toryism, doesn’t feel comfortable. In ’B9,
when the Trades Hall Bill was before S. A. Legis-
lature, Baker and his Ass colleagues violently
opposed a State grant. To make it clear his
motives were above suspicion, Baker said, on
Dec. 4, ’B9 : “lam willing to help this section by
giving .£5O, and I am willing to guarantee that I
will collect £lOOO from members of Parliament if
this Bill is lost, and if I do not do this I will pay
the guaranteed amount myself.” The Bill was
rejected accordingly. Chaffey’s pledge is em-
balmed in the pages of S.A. Hansard, and
although six years have elapsed only .£560 of the
amount promised has been received from Baker’s
friends. As the trades-unionists are now erecting
the hall they are anxious to know when Baker is
going to pay up.
The wholesale contempt now poured upon
N.S.W. Supreme Court could not have come to
pass had Governor Oarington been anything but a
glorified stable-boy with his eyes and ears ever
opim for public applause. At the Mount Rennie
time lie personally held the absolute prerogative
of mercy, and if, instead of smilingly receiving
the brutal Sydney Exchange deputation which in
his ignorance he took to represent public opinion
and which insulted himself and his office by
ashing him, in as many words, to hang the boys
of whose fate he was the final arbiter, he had
been a man imbued with a sense of what was
due to justice, and had sat down and written
iu the minutes of the Executive Council that he
declined to take the advice of his Ministers and
would not be guilty of slaying even a mangy dog
condemned after such a trial, he would have
sponged out Judge Windeyer and the miserable
legal ring who to-day einbastion that official. Who
can expect Opposition leader Lyne, of the Mount
Rennie Cabinet, and the present Attorney-
General Want, who was also of the Mount Rennie
Cabinet, to directly censure themselves by
now doing what is emphatically their duty towards
the Mount Rennie Judge ?
The Argus is pleased with Vic. Railway
Board for recommending the reduction of rail-
way laborers’ wages to a the rates
ruling outside the department. The rates ruling
outside are anything from half-a-crown a day up
to 6s. The practice in America, says the con-
servative organ, is to pay railway employes less
than the ruling rate in consideration of the regular
employment. Fortunately, every citizen who is
not a railway employe hopes to be one, so that it
is not probable that the railway men will be re-
duced to subsisting upon the departmental waste.
Melbourne Ar-
gus, which always
raves against every
Victorian industry
in the interests of
the importers, has
systematically de-
nounced the local
coal as compara-
tively worthless,
and urged the use
of the Newcastle
product on the
(State railways. The
Koinmburra Coal
Co. now suggests
that the Premier
shall appoint an
officer who shall
send ten tons each
of Korumhurra and
Newcastle coal to
the Tasmanian
Railway Commis-

on their relative
merits shall be ac-
cepted as final. The
coal co. offers to
hack its product for
<£so, and asks the
Argus to bet a
similar amount on
the imported fuel,
the loser’s money to
go to Melb. Hospi-
tal. So far the
Argus hasn’t been
heard from on the
subject. This ex-
treme confidence in
Vic. coal certainly
surprises N.S.W,
The Ass crowd
in S. A. Legislative
Council recently
worked a smart
point. The ad-
journed debate on
the second reading
of the Employers’
Liability Bill was
called on, and a
Tory speaker was
about to continue;
noticing, however,
that Kirkpatrick and Bice, M.’sL.C., were out of
the chamber, another Ass member stopped the
speaker hurriedly, and the second reading was
put and lost by 9 votes to 8.
At the settling after the ’94 N.S.W. general
election a country returning-officer could not stand
his deputies being paid for their services, whilst
nothing came his way save honor. However,
having a Govt, cheque-book, he thought he would
endeavor to mend this injustice, and drew and
cashed a cheque in his own favor for £5O. When
a statement of accounts was sent, “head-quarters ”
asked him to explain. He explained—and had to
refund the <£so.
The forest-thinning industry has been
already started on a large scale in N.S.W., so if
Parliament makes any difficulty about passing the
Loan Bill it will be told that the money is already
spent, and it has no voice in the matter. It is
intended to have about 400 men on the job—it is
indeed a job—by the end of next week, and the
Labor Bureau is to send another 300. The maxi-
mum wage will be 7s. per day. As Varney Parlies
says : “ Why not spend <£200,000 at once, and ask
the sanction of Parliament afterwards? ”
Sydney Daily Telegraph declares it is
the duty of N.S.W. Government to have the Par-
liamentary motion of want of confidence in Justice
Windeyer discussed instantly :
To dispose of the question is an obligation which cannot
be put off. It requires instant fulfilment, as one of the
most serious and urgent that a Government can under-
take.
The fact is that Judge Windeyer, the best lawyer
in N.S.W. and the worst judge south of the
Equator, is passively supported by the N.S.W
legal ring; though most of the members thereof
don’t love him they are afraid to say so. Their
sober sentiments in the matter are very
much like those of the historical Brown,
who, when drunk and off his guard one day
met his life-long chum, Smith. “Look here, old
man,” he said, “we’ve been friends for many
years, but I—hie!—never liked'you.” Judge
Windeyer’s strength has lain and lies in his thick
skin and his uniform aggressiveness. He is the
Pie Biter of the Bench-the great American Pie
Biter, it will be remembered, had a hand like a
whale’s ear, and when he reached out for a pie
that was his pie. His Honor has hitherto reached
out for everything he fancied—and he has got it,
from the N.S.W. Attorney-Generalship to the
Chancellorship of the University. But, spoilt by
success, he has presumed too far on public endur-
ance. No Government would now dare to
make Judge Windeyer Chief Justice of N.S.W.,
and no Australian Parliament would dare to
vote money to pay him a salary as an Australian
representative on the Privy Council.

A DISEASE, not a CRIME.
The constant abuse of alcohol fosters a crave—
a crave insatiable and deadly. It deprives valu-
able men of all reason and respect; it drives them
to acts of indecency and crime, and to drink what
their palates may even repel. At this stage
pledges and punishment prove useless, and
physical, as well as mental attention, is urgently
required.
The Orient Institute
has established a wonderful reputation in the
curing of this awful disease, and, during the past
18 months, many prominent men of our colony
have been restored to health, sobriety and
position. Alcoholic stimulants become not only
unnecessary, but, in the majority of cases, un-
palatable. Patients undergo no inconvenience or
restraint, and are free to eat and drink what they
like.

“I had heard of the excellent results attending your
work, and was thereby induced to send my friend to you.

F >r the previous five years he has always been under the
influence of alcohol and gradually losing power to resist
it. Since he lias been under your treatment a marvellous
change has been wrought in him. Not only does he
appear to he thoroughly cured of drinking, but his
appearance, which had been degenerating, has improved,
and ids color is healthy and bright. In short, the man
seems not only to have been purged of disease, but also
renewed mentally and physically.”

Parsonic scorn for the girl who rides a
bike, or a trike, is not to be wondered at. If she
made a point of dashing up to the sanctuary on
the godless vehicle, Rev. Obadiah would gladly
give it shelter in the porch—but she doesn’t. The
wheelwoman is a brazen Sabbath-breaker, who
flaunts her knickers abroad when she ought to be
saying her prayers, and represents a clear loss of
threepence a week to the Cause.

Hamilton (Vic.) cannery buys 20,000 pairs
rabbits weekly, and turns out about 5000 tins of
bunnies daily. It employs in all 80 men and boys.
London and Glasgow are the chief markets. The
Euroa factory turns out about 12,000 cases per
annum.
The Maoriland acclimatisation societies
have done splendid work in stocking the rivers
with trout and the country with deer, quad,
partridge, pheasant, &c. The Southland society
is now trying to acclimatise the opossum, having
liberated 25 at Milford Sound and 35 on Stewart
Island.
Up Hughenden way some of the squatters
have turned scalpers. One man three weeks ago
netted 180 kangaroo scalps at Is, 6d. per scalp—
£l2 for seven days’ sport. In drought-times this
pays better than trying to keep life in starving
sheep ; and besides —the bank mostly owns the
sheep, and the scalp-money is “bunce.”
Ed. Bulletin, —A frequent theme of dis-
cussion in the North is the Southern limit of the
alligator. The writer has it upon excellent
authority that the various creeks flowing out in
the vicinity of the “Narrows” (Central Queens-
land) form the Southern boundary of Alligator-
laud. Would some Bulletin reader inform me
is this correct ? —P. Luftig.
In another column appears the advt. of
Mr. W. Mentiplay, of the Botanic Institute,
Bourke-street, Melbourne. Mr. Mentiplay claims
that he has achieved many remarkable cures in
the treatment of various diseases, and his claims
are backed up by published testimonials from a
large number of patients. The medicines sold at
the Botanic Institute are guaranteed to be of
purely vegetable origin, and have an extensive
sale throughout Australia.*
Eight Hours Day, the date of Labor’s
great annual demonstration, draws near, and so
far the carnival of 7th October, 1895, promises to
easily break the record for magnificence, good as
that record is. A very large number of entries
have been received for the sports events, and the
railway arrangements for the day will be as per-
fect as expert management can make them. The
committee has engaged Mr. T. A. Kennedy, the
fearful and wonderful mesmerist, to entertain
holiday-makers at the Town Hall in the evening.
The attention of country ticket-purchasers is
specially drawn to the fact that they can send
postal notes or stamps and receive their face value
by return mail.*
Dear Bulletin, —Can you match this
true instance of presence of mind? Years ago I
was standing by a deep, narrow pool talking to my
mate. He had a heavy “ spud ”in his hand. He
turned to go without thinking, and fell 10ft. into
the pool. Neither of us could swim, but before I
had collected my wits, he crawled out on the other
side, 12yds. away. He said afterwards he knew
he must drown if he tried to swim, but he had
seen divers stop under water a minute, and he
knew he could hold his breath longer than most.
He knew also that the weight of the potato would
counteract the buoyancy of his body. So he shut
his mouth, held his breath and the potato, and
crawled along the bottom to the other side. —
Verity.
Dear B., —Tu a recent Bulletin Mr.
H. J. M'Cooey scornfully snorts at the alleged
botanical inaccuracies of bush bards generally ;
instancing his remarks by allusion to “an im-
posiible and indiscriminate jungle” of various
timbers mentioned in a fragment of my verse
which yon recently published. I now beg to in-
form him that 05 miles east of Barringun, and on
the smith side of the Q. Border-fence, is a 3 x
paddock on Mr. J. Ryan’s “ Wycheproof ” block ;
where, amongst oth r varieties of timber, blood-
wood, box and pine are fairly abundant. Also, in
this same paddock are three myall trees. How
these last came there I don’t know, as they are
not usually found in that soit of country, although
plenty of myall grows twenty miles away. Pos-
sibly they were planted long ago, by a far-seeing
Providence, for so suitable and patriotic a purpose
as the cock-sure naturalist suggests, i.e. that he
may “hang himself on either!” Moreover, I
have ridden in half of a summer’s day, from the
late Charley Norman’s homestead “Morendali”
into Walgett town, and from that track (which
runs from the Narran to the Barioon, via Gom-
borrah Springs) those trees mentioned in “In
Such a Night ” may, with a score of other sorts,
be seen at various intervals of the journey.
There are myalls and bloodwood sufficient to cre-
mate the whole M’Cuoey clan If the captious
M/Cooey will really keep his word, I will gladly
lend him a greenhide rope wherewith to do the
trick.— The Breaker.

the work is done for nothing—but the rabbits.

Queen Victoria was not at all angry with
the ancient Duke of Argyle for getting married a
third time. She gave the bride, who had been
one of her mature maids-of-honor, a choice assort-
ment of wedding presents, and congratulated her
heartily on having found an old man to take care
of.
Constable Crowe, of Fitzroy (VicQ, com-
piler of the alleged “Australian slang dictionary ”
has also turned out a technically useful publication
called “Manual on the duties of a Constable.’’
In this Tittle book the heavy policeman who
doesn’t know any more law than a J.P. is told
what to do on every conceivable emergency.
Many policemen have just sufficient brains to ring
the tirebell as soon as they hear of a distressing
case of refusing to pay for alcohol, and this book
tells them that the procedure aforesaid is very,
very incorrect.
Dear Bulletin, —Re “ W.’s ” letter anent
original Broken Hill holders. My note referred
to the men of the Barrier, not to original holders
of the big mine. Consequently “ W.’s ” remarks
beg the question. I said, and say again, that the
prizes of mining are generally not to the men who
do the work ; that to the Wilsons and the Horns
and the M'Gregors and the M'Oullochs belongs a
success partly fallen over in the dark, and
partly gained by their possession of a capital
to trade with and a vast ability for doing
nothing enthusiastically and breathing with
regularity; whereas “ W.” would lead us
to believe that the Wilsons and the Horns
put the big lode where it is and then discovered it
before it was lost. Long before the Proprietary
was known, there were the Day Dream, the Hen
and Chickens, the Apollyon, and the Bonanza
mines around the old Thackaringa, and with the
h-orn silver days of the field are inseparably con-
nected Sully and Reid. Reid started his paper
under heart-breaking circumstances and condi-
tions, and he and Sully, who never stopped the
shop ” to hard-up prospectors, deserve more
that which they got than does any capitalist or
moneyed worker the field ever knew. When you
get down to such primitive conditions as the con-
ditions of a mining-camp, the capitalist is at a
discount and the muscle of a strong man is gold.
So in all successful mining fields labor more than
cash makes the success, and he who toils the
hardest and the longest is infinitely more deserv-
ing of his win than he whose “ graft ” was repre-
sented by a cheque-book.— The Reckless.

The late Bishop of Winchester (Dr.
Thorold) from the day of his appointment to the
rectorship of St. Giles’s down to the day of his

The latest extension of the penny-in-the-
slot idea is the “cure-all.” There are slots for
about 50 ailments. You put a penny into the
slot marked “ bruise ” and you get a tiny phial of
arnica, and you put another into the “liver”
compartment and out rolls a sugar-coated pill.
In the slow-going old city of Perth (W.A.)
there are no street numbers. Perth, till quite
recently, was a kind of overgrown village where
everybody knew everybody else, and his business,
and all about him, and the authorities can’t under-
stand that these petrified days are gone for good.
In the West Indies, if you offer a friend a
light from your cigar, on no account knock the
ash off. It is a deadly insult. And in Peru, be
exceedingly careful not to pour out wine for a lady
without rising from your seat, wherever you may
be. A challenge from her nearest male relation
follows at once. So, at least, a South American
paper says.
Under the heading of “Peculiar Case of
Assault ” the Argus reports the fracture of a
woman’s skull by her lady companions through
her refusal to “shout” threepenn’orth of beer.
Anyone, though but superficially acquainted with
the usages of Australian ladies on washing-day
would know that such reprehensible meanness
could have but one termination.
“ Peculiar charge of bigamy ” is how the
papers describe the case of a Richmond (Vic.)
man who, according to the charge laid against
him, had a wife and seven children. He married
another wife, and went to live quite close to the
residence of his first family. He paid the parson
who married him to his second wife with a value-
less cheque. Then he was run in on a charge of
false pretences, and both wives came to weep over
him in the lock-up, and naturally they met and
explained things to each other, and after that the
trouble began in real earnest. The man who takes
his second wife to live almost next door to his first
one is a person of such desperate valour that it
seems a pity he should get into gaol instead of
being publicly decorated by the Governor.
“The caddie is an indispensable adjunct
to the golf-player, and something new in caddies
has just appeared in the links at San Francisco.
Miss Dent has two Formosa apes which are
thoroughly broken to the r6le of caddies. They
wear liveries of white duck, and each has a small
Turkish fez.” “ Scotty the Wrinkler ” has it from
Scotland that lady-golfers are hated by profes-
sional caddies because they tip too meanly, and
one was even heard to say (he was a Highlander),
“Maybe the saxpence wull no pay for the whusky
your leddyship trank fae’ ma flesk. ’’ Perhaps the
honor of being attendant to and shouting for a
real “ leddy ” is considered payment enough.
Gamekeepers often refuse point-blank to attend
she-shootists ; although gillies like the lady-
stalker, just on the off-chance of what has been
and will be—an elopement.
The S.M. Herald again lifts up its voice
against free education. N.S.W. raises .£70,000 a
year from school fees, and the Herald states that
it is “quite unaware” of any public demand for
the abolition of this impost. This may be true,
but it is equally true that there was never any
public demand for free newspaper postage, which
is probably as heavy a tax on the people as the
abolition of the school-fees would be. And the
public money is much better spent in removing
the last barriers to the spread of knowledge than
in subsidising the daily ’orror with the full details
of the disembowelled female who was found in
Woolloomooloo, or the shopkeeper’s catalogue re-
gistered as a newspaper. Victoria has reversed the
cornstalk method by levying postage on the tale
of the disembowelled female and making education
absolutely free to everybody, and its neighbor
should follow suit.
Dear Bulletin, —It wasn’t the late in-
spector Duffy who shot Gibson, the murderer of
senior-sergeant Wallings. Two of Mr. Duffy’s
troopers were camped one night near the War-
rego, with an aboriginal murderer in custody,
when Gibson, alias Midnight, rode up to the
camp. Being challenged and fired upon he left
hurriedly, leaving his packhorse, with all firearms,
in the possession of the police, who guaranteed
their black prisoner a free pardon to faithfully
follow the outlaw’s tracks. The shooting of
“Midnight,” together with his horse, a valuable
stallion, Sir William, stolen from a Coonamble
squatter, appears not to have been absolutely
necessary under the circs. Inspector Duffy was
in charge of the Warrego district at that time.
This inglorious affair evidently furnished Rolf
Boldrewood with material for the finale in his
“ Robbery Under Anns,” where Starlight and his
thoroughbred, Rainbow, was shot on the War-
rego.—Chevron.
“ Apropos the Curse of Deeming,” writes
a Bulletin correspondent, “ it may be of interest
to mention that a large firm of Melb. jewellers
with whom he had extensive dealings had to pay
a fine of, I think, £l5OO to the Customs the other
day for fraudulent entries. A Melb. ironmongery
which he favored with his custom has not
prospered for some time past. Gov. Shegog, of
Melb. Gaol, who presided at his hanging, was
thrown from a buggy soon after, and has not since
recovered from the shock. Deputy-Gov. Foy, who
had the responsibility of looking after Deeming
when in prison, shortly after succumbed to a brain
trouble; and hangman Jones did not live to place
the rope on the neck of another client. Warder
Hogan, who kept the gate at the gaol, was plunged
into ruin after Deeming’s death through the bank
smash. Even Marshall Lyle will tell you that his
connection with Deeming lost him more business
than the notoriety of the case ever brought him.
The two journalists and the artist who set out on a
provincial tour with a Deeming panorama fell on
hard times. No one would go to see the thing, which
they hawked about the country in a spring cart;
and the horse, as soon as he knew what he was
dragging after him, refused to go any further.
When whipped for insubordination, the animal
sent his heels through the splash-board, and bolted
along a country road till he collided with a tree
and emptied the panorama and its proprietors
into a water-hole. A pianist, who was also of the
party, has not been able to strike a note since.
Lastly, Shiels, who as Premier and Attorney-
General had the fixing-up of Deeming’s death-
warrant, has been in a chronic state of bad health
for the past two years ; while detective Considine,
who had charge of the Windsor murder-case and
tracked Deeming down, succumbed to a bad cold
only a few days ago. Where, I would like to
know, is the curse going to stop? Why not have
a day of humiliation and prayer and ask Provi-
dence to arrest the mischief ? ”

The old Duke of Gampridge, one is tired
of reading in the “ loyal ’’press, “always had the
good of the Service at heart.” How superfluous.
Every member of every Royal Family wishes the
local Army well, and prays that its days may be
long in the land. No army, no monarchy.
Henry Lawson’s book, which may be
called “ Block and Backblocks : Australian Verses
by Henry Lawson,” or something of the sort, will
be out shortly and will, like “ Banjo’s ” (orders
for which are rapidly flowing in), be a handsome
volume printed in new type on fine paper. The
price will be ss. 6d., post free from this office, and
The Bulletin, after seeing the material of which
the book is composed, has no hesitation in saying
that it deserves to be, and will be, an immediate
and permanent success.
“ This is the true&nd natural principle of
clothing.” Thus says the Lancet of cellular cloth
clothing. The manner in which cellular cloth is
woven reproduces, on a large scale, the construc-
tion of the pores of the skin. The two sides are
not alike —the side that is worn next to the skin
being open work, while the other side is partially
closed by fine threads—thus the body is sur-
rounded by cells of non-conducting air. The
agents for Victoria, Messrs. Hicks, Atkinson and
Sons, of Melb., state the cellular cloth is be-
coming very generally used for underwear, and is
invariably appreciated, as it possesses many ex-
cellent qualities in addition to its healthy attri-
butes—amongst which not the least are economy
and cleanliness.*
Sydney Telegraph, in its leading article
of Sept. 18, urges the N.S.W. Legislative Council
to compromise on the Land-tax difficulty by strik-
ing out the exemptions. If it does, The Bulletin
devoutly hopes chat the Assembly will reinstate
them and fight out the question to the bitter end,
whatever that may be. A Land-tax which falls
in equal proportion on the miserable little selector
with 50 acres and on the huge landholder wich
40,000 or more may be a profitable revenue-pro-
ducing device. But it will entirely miss its real
object, or what should be its real object—the dis-
couragement of the big rack-renting monopolist
and the foreign land-syndicate, and the encourage-
ment of the small, independent agriculturist.
Moreover, when the Reid Ministry went to the
country at last election its programme distinctly
included the exemptions, and if it got any distinct
mandate on the subject of the Land-tax, that
mandate included the exemptions, and it can no
more go back on them without absolute treachery
than it can go back on the tax altogether, or on
Freetrade, or on Upper House reform.
THE GOOD SLEEP OF A BAD MAN.
'ln a certain prison that we all have heard of lay
a convict upon his narrow iron cot. He was to be
hanged the next morning. Yet he lay there,
covered by a rough blanket, sleeping as quietly
and soundly as a tired schoolboy. Occasionally
the guard in the passage outside peered between
the bars of the cell, only to find his charge breath-
ing deeply and regularly. This man had violated
the law prohibiting murder ; yet he had not vio-
lated the physical laws governing his own body,
and nature rewarded him as if he had been the
noblest of his race.
That same night, less than a mile away, a rich
man tossed and tumbled upon his luxurious bed.
He was a good and useful member of society, yet
he could not sleep. And, worse still, this hap-
pened to him every night. Sleep—that blessing
which the Psalmist says, “God giveth his
beloved,’’ was practically a stranger to this man.
What ailed him? The tortures of conscience?
Want of money ? The fear of enemies ? Nothing
of the sort. Then why didn’t he sleep as well as
the murderer ? You would like to know ? Right,
let us look into the matter.
“ I got no sleep at night; I would lie for hours
tossing about. In the morning I was worse tired
than when I went to bed.”
Thus writes Mrs. Eliza Mathews, of 1, North
Road, Burnt Oak, Edgware, near London, under
date of September 22nd, 1892. Just two years
before this time she lost her health. A foul taste
in the mouth, loss of appetite, and great distress
after eating were among the first things she com-
plained of. She craved food at times, and fancied
she could eat heartily, yet when the very dishes
she had asked for were set before her she turned
from them as though they were filth from the
gutter. Her skin grew sallow, her eyes yellow,
and she had a constant pain at her chest, sides,
and between her shoulders. Her bowels were
constipated, and the least exertion set her heart
thumping as if it must jump up into her mouth.
At such times it was as much as ever that she
could get her breath. She got so thin and weak
she was no good for work. She couldn’t walk out
doors- without stopping to rest every few rods
almost.
The doctor did what he could for her, all any
doctor could do. At first he said he thought her
illness was owing to the smell of the farm-yard.
This looked possible. Even the smell of violets
has made strong men turn pale and faint dead
away. Yet the doctor was wrong. If he had
been right, she would have got better when the
family left the farm at Bently Priory and went to
live at Burnt Oak. But she was not improved by
the change of air ; she grew worse and worse.
“In May, 1887,” says Mrs. Mathews, “I went
over to Ghlemsford to visit my aunt, Mrs.
Trougliton. She told mo of the good Mother
Seigol’s Curative Syrup had done her when she
had indigestion and dyspepsia. She bought me a
bottle, and 1 began taking it. After a few doses I
felt relief. I kept on taking it, and in two months
I ivas strong and well as ever. My husband and
friends were astonished, yet I assured them that
Seigel’s Syrup had done it. Yours truly, (Signed)
Eliza Mathews.”
The point is plain enough. The convict slept
soundly because he was a healthy man, although
he was a wicked one. Our rich friend rolled about
all night because his nerves were unstrung by the
state of his stomach. Our cor res pon dent was
nrostrated by the same thing—indigestion and
dyspepsia. The remedy named cured her because
it has that power. The reason remains a secret
with the roots and herbs from which it is made.
Yet so long as it drives away disease and gives us
back our health and strength, who cares for its
mystery? Results, not arguments, are what we
all want.
“Burnt Oak House, Edgware, September, 22nd,
1892. I have known Mrs. Mathews for some
seven years, and remember her long and lingering
illness. She informs me that Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup cured her, after medical and other
means failed. Mrs. Mathews is a lady of respecta-
bility, and her word can be implicitly relied upon.
You can use this statement in any way you may
think proper. Yours truly, (Signed) T. H. House,
Grocer and General Provision Dealer, Burnt Oak
Stores, Edgware.”
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Word comes from the Monaro that trout are
being acclimatised in the Snowy River ; thus dots the old
order change and give place to lhe new and the Devil
fulfils himself in many ways. The Snowy River dis-
trict, the home of the mountain cattle and the rough-
riders, will now be overrun by English tourists, with loud
check suits and knickerbockers. These men will write
articles for English magazines descriptive of “A day’s
trout-fishing on the—haw —Snowy Bivah,” and they will
say “I summoned my guide, a trusty fellow, and, taking
my rod, I bade farewell to my hostess, a most civil per-
son, and filling my pocket-flask from my own supply, I
walked down the precipitous declivities that lead to the
bank of this most agreeable little rivah.” Fancy one of
the Tyries, the crack rough-riders from the Tindary
Mountains, being pressed into the service of an English
boiled owl and referred to as “my guide, a trusty
fellow!" That is what it will come to. All the romance
goes out when the tourists come in.
* * * *
The formation of the Vic. Cricket League, and
the consequent secession of the rich clubs from the asso-
ciation, is a very mean device. The alleged reason for
the withdrawal is the “unwieldiness” of the associa-
tion ; the real reason is that the Melb., East, and South
cannot boss Vic. cricket as they like, and because they
grudge the small clubs the share of profits of iuterpro-
vinoial matches and desire to divert this to their own
treasuries. Under the league rules the small clubs are
.charitably permitted to affiliate - i.e., they are allowed to
pay a subscription lor the privilege of doing whatever the
league tells them. They are to have no share in the
government or the profits, and are to play matches among
themselves.
Among theclubs not connected with the league—
which the league, in fact, refused to admit to its charmed
circle—is the University, the club which stood fourth on
the list of the A division last year, and which possesses,
without doubt, the best wicketkeeper in the province.
The omission of the U.U.C. thus shows that the object of
the league’s formation is to confine to an inner ring not
the crack but the gold-glutted clubs, and the powerof the
latter in “ amateur” cricket is only understood when the
lists of their best players are studied. With half-a-dozen
exceptions, every good man in the Melb., the East, or the
South was a good man in one of the smaller clubs before
the temptations of free trips, notoriety, chances of dis-
tinction, and offers of good prizes lured him into one or
other of these elevens.
At every annual meeting is heard the gag about
“drawing on the second elevens.” How many men now
playing in the first elevens of the clubs named came out
of the seconds ? If the association, composed as it is of
the remnants of the pennant clubs, has any pluck, it will
fight. Why not try to procure the co-operation of other
associations to continue playing interprovincial matches
only under its auspices, and to refuse to recognise in any
way the combination that is doing its best to reduce
cricket to the level of pony-racing?

The knowing crowd who all the season seemed
to pull the strings for certain football clubs fell into the
soup over last Vic. premier match. Fitzroy was to go
down to South, and Geelong to beat Ports, and then one
or two other things were to occur. But something went
wrong, and the little game didn’t come off; after 12
years’ hard battling, Fitzroy got home by a neck, and the
curtain was rung down on the worst season the “great
national game ” has ever seen in Vic.
♦ » * *
Act.-Gov. Madden was not only advertised as
patronising the final Vic. football premiership match at
South Melb., bu the turned up with secretary, equerry and
suite. At the first gate he was blocked by the gate-
keeper, and had to “part.” He had also (through the
Aide) to pay for admission to the reserve. The Aide’s
protests were in vain. The gate and reserve keepers
“ didn’t believe he was no Governor.” To make matters
worse, there was not a soul on hand to officially receive
H.E., and it was only after a vigorous hunt that the
president was found. That potentate eventually handed
over to the equerry the money paid for admission.
• ♦ ♦ *
A unique contest recently took place at the hall
of a Melb. pug., when the mate of a China steamer backed
his monkey to kill more rats within a given time than a
certain crack dog. The pup entered the ring first, and in
60sec. had settled 22 rodents. When the corpses were
cleared, the mariner directed that 23 more rats should be
thrown in together, and, this being done, he produced
his little anthropoid from a sack and, placing a tack-
hammer in its hand, let it go. The grinning simian
set to work with a will and bad the ring cleared, every
rat as dead as a door-nail, in 28sec. less time than the dog
had taken. But just think of an ourang outang or a
gorilla, trained to kill humans instead of rats, out on the
loose with a 21b. hammer!
• * ♦ *
An American gentleman residing at St. Kilda
(Vic.), who professed to own a dog which could “ destroy
anything that lives of its own weight,” w'as recently much
troubled by the nightly howling of a one-eyed, mangy-
looking, black tom-cat, owned by a neighbor. An angry
altercation between the American and the neighbor re-
sulted in the feline's owner accepting an invitation to
“feed his cat to the terrier.” A two-minutes’interview
between the animals, when much eloquence was on tap,
resulted in the neighbor becoming proud possessor of a
Yankee canine windpipe.

master, the poor widow was proud. But the reckoning
arrived one day—the local policeman came and took up
with one foot (in top of the other all the space in the poor
widow’s small kitchen. “Oi’m come,” he said, “for the
mhurderin O'Flanagan.”

If this, from American Bearings, is all true,
champion cyclist Zimmerman has a “soft thing” in his
Australian engagementHe will be handed $5OOO in
gold upon his arrival at Sydney ; in fact, that amount has
already been deposited to his credit. All his own, as well
as his wife’s, expenses after reaching there will be paid by
his Australian managers. He must be there by Nov. 1,
and ride his first race not later than Nov. 16. Three races
a week are the least he can start in, and in every race he
will be guaranteed one-half the amount of the first prize
if he loses, or, of course, the full amount if he wins. At
all race meets where he is advertised to appear, he will
receive one-quarter of the gate-receipts. His contract
terminates in time to allow him to leave for America by
May 1, ’96.
J * * * •
The Court of Queen’s Bench lately decided that
there was not sufficient evidence to prove that the Jockey
Club stewards did knowingly and wilfully permit the
offence of betting—and may the Lord have mercy on the
Court of Q. B.’s soul, if it has a soul. Judge Hawkins,
for instance, is an inveterate racegoer who must have had
a bit in many a stewards’ wager.
Whilst tins wigged tribunal was affecting not to
know of Taitersall’s enclosure, and the revenue which
the Jockey Club derives from big bookmakers, two small
members of the craft were charged at the Guildhall and
West London police courts with having caused an obstruc-
tion by betting in the street. The result in each case was
a £5 fine, and an alderman announced his intention to
put such offences down. One of the cases fell a long way
short of proof. The evidence was much less conclusive
than the facts advanced against the Jockey Club in the
higher court. But circumstances made a difference
between the cases. Whereas an alderman could feel justi-
fied in assuming that bookmakers bet, a Supreme Court
could gammon to assume that the Jockey Club stewards
don’t know why bookmakersshout “the fee-eld a tenner!”
&c., in a special ring at Newmarket.
• * * *
The Street Betting Act, now before Vic.
Assembly, is laughed at by the bye-way bettors, for the
reason that, in spite of the elaborateness of the Act’s
definition of a street or place, the framers of the bill have
left half-a dozen big loopholes through which the books
can “drive a coach and four."
• » • •
Pathetic fact that Adelaide bookies who, ere
the reign of the tote, used to bet in “fivers,” are now
only too glad to book humble shilling wagers. The local
betting industry is now almost solely confined to
“ double ” books and commission wagering.
The writer was at Adelaide Globe Hotel, the
resort of the few remaining bookmakers, recently. The
books were betting on a billiard-match. “I’ll lay 60 to
30,” said a penciller who was a big man in the ring in the
ante-tote days, and is still in the first flight. “ I’ll take
it—£6o to £30,” said a mining man. “ Sixty pounds to
thirty! Not me! Make it £3 to 30 bob!” said the
bookie, appalled. To lay the heavier wager would kill
most of the Adelaide books.
* ♦ • •
The Sydney Polo Club Gymkhana, at Kensing-
ton, on 18th, was a great social function, and, from a
sporting point of view, a distinct success. The mem-
bers of the Queensland polo team competed, and one ol
them, Mr. W. Peak, of Toowoomba, proved himself a con-
siderable way ahead of the local leatherflappers. The
ponies were a very poor lot this year, as the champion
polo pony, Irene, from Muswellbrook, was not competing.
The Cup fell to the pony Donation, and she also secured
the bracelet. In the cigar-and-umbrella race one pony
got terrified at the gingham waving over his head and
rushed blindly into a high galvanised-iron fence, rolling
over and over his rider. When the dust cleared the rider
was seen sitting on the ground amongst his pony’s legs,
still smoking his cigar and holding his umbrella. In the
tandem race, Mr. Peak collided violently with another
team and got a terrible spill, but recovered sufficiently to
compete in the final heat. His ridden pony became dis-
gusted at the whole show and jumped the racecourse
rails with him, but the rider immediately whipped him
round and jumped him back again, amidst loud applause.
The steeplechase brought a lot of horses to grief, Briga-
low, last year’s winner, being killed. This was the well-
known show-jumper—he won the high-jump at last
Sydney show, clearing sft. 7in. in mud. The race was
won by Gossip (by Stamford from a Chatterbox mare),
nominated and ridden by Mr. A. B. Paterson (“The
Banjo ”). A wild scurry in the costume-race brought a
very amusing show to a close.

The most striking peculiarity of Randwiok to a
Vic. visitor is the poor chance occupants of the Leger
Stand have of seeing the finish of a race. The nearest
part of the Leger is a furlong from the judge’s box, and
onlookers are in doubt as to the winner until the numbers'
go up. In fact, when a close finish occurs as much bet-
ting takes place after it as before.

The backing of Paris down to 7 to 1 for the
Cambridgeshire, a month before the race, seems some-
what absurd. Unless the horse has been very highly
tried with reliable “ tackle,” he is certain to start at a
longer price, and the enthusiast who took (according to
last week's cable) seven “monkeys” about the little ’un’s
chance will regret his haste. The Frenchmen have a re-
putation for getting good things into the Cambridgeshire
at a light weight.
* * • •
Apropos Paris’s short price for the Cambridge-
shire—as a consequence of Australian backing. Patriotism 1
is a fine thing, but fetishism is folly. Years ago White’s 1
Narellan was eagerly backed in Melb. at 20 to 1 for Epsom
Derby, whilst 100 to 1 could at the time be easily obtained
in London. Narellan ran about last. Paris’s friends 1

October, by the way, is Paris’s lucky month, as
two Caulfield Cups testify.

The Harvester having retired from Melb. Cup,
Mr. Cook is left with the gallant old Admiral, who will
be found more at home in Melb. Cup than at Caulfield,
despite his prominence in the betting.
Should Palo Alto (who, carrying Bst. 31b., ran
very prominently in the A.J.O. Handicap, IJm., though
starting friendless) represent Mr. Porches in Melb. Cup,
he should run a great horse with but 6st. 121 b, on his
0-year-old back.
The Wycombe Stakes, at A.J.O. Meeting, was
a perfect picnic for Mr. Long’s champion Hopscotch, who
was hammered down to evens in the betting, and scored
his fifth win. He has been well looked after, and not
raced off his legs ; result, a good harvest at this meeting,
with other plums to be picked later. The long, striding
giant Atlas tried to get up to Grand Flaneur s son at the
but was shaken off like a hack. Hopscotch s easy
score reminded old sports of the races won by his unoeaten
sire. _ .
* * * •
Yet it would be only in accordance with the
eternal unfitness of matters racing if Quiver were to beat
his heavi iy-backed stable-companion, Hopscotch, and win
Caulfield Cup.
• • • *
Hopscotch has earned the position of Caulfield
Oup favorite, and is likely to maintain it to the day.
There are a few others who will spring into pencilled
prominence before then, however, and Idolater is one.
Delaware, though lusty in appearance, ran a
good horse in the A.J.O. Spring Stakes and easily
annexed the Craven Plate. Bar accidents he should go
near winning for*Mr. Walter Hall his first Melb. Cup.
« t * •
From all accounts Wallace, Challenger and
Auraria should all be capable of beating the best of the
N.S.W. three-year-olds when theymeet in V.R.O. Derby.
Extraordinary reversals of form were shown at
Randwick meeting. Nags backed for thousands by the
confiding public promptly “ went down, but when only
the stable clique were on then they “bobbed up serenely.
The good old weather is supposed to account for all this.
In the stoutly-built Tullamore, rumor hath it,
Foulshara possesses a second Q’naroo.
* * * *
At the A.J.O. meeting Maoriland Stead collared
a solitary plum—with Musketry, winner of the High
Weight, in which four fell. Mr. Stead shipped his trio
back to M.L. same night.
• * * , ,
Ury’s turn up in the Members race, and

£3161) during the A.J.O. meeting. See how the devil
whispers again ! The other day “ Bill ” Long tried to sell
his horses. Now the Evil One sends him some wins, and
tells him to go on and not be a fool. Not that there is
much dancer of the gentle William ever behaving rashly.

Though Nobleman was the star of the Metrop,
his early backers never expected to cash their tickets
after his unpardonable Epsom mile exhibition. He
originally cost 150g5., and, although faster than most of
his contemporaries, 8 seconds chalked to his credit.
Solanum’s Final Handicap penalty should not
prevent him from running forward at Caulfield Oup time,
when he ought to be quite in racing form.

field of 10 for a handioap-trot, at Moonee Valley (Vic.).

else. And in a trot, too 1 The most marvellous thing
heard of since the sun stood still.

Sporting glorification of the new vice-regal
family has made a fair start in Victoria. Lady Brassey
(Tat. 51b.) won the Trial Handicap at Ballarat.
There are many alleged ways to start a jibbing
norse, ranging from kissing him on his baby brow to
lighting a Are under him, but they mostly remain
“ alleged 99 A quiet bystander in a crowd the other day,
watching a jibber, told the driver to get a stone and tap
on one of the fore shoes as if driving in a nail. The
driver did so, and the horse then started at first asking.
Asked why and wherefore, the quiet one remarked:
“ Mental diversion. Sets him wondering what s up, and
he forgets all about wanting to jib. Looks like sense,
doesn’t it? . ,

It was eccentric Earl Glasgow who didn’t believe
in naming his horses until they earned it. Hence they
used to race as “He Hasn’t Opt a Name,' “He s Not
Worth a Name,” and so on. He didn’t even give the mighty
Musket a name until he won a race.

At Croydon (Q.), the other day, a lorry-horse
was backed for a wager to pull eight men over a mark. A
’•op© was passed round the cart, and four men had bold of
each end, but the horse failed to shift the men.

One of the players of a leading football club
was lately "wiped out” by his committee for “conduct
detrimental to the interests of the club’’—such conduct
being startling reversals of form alleged to be not un-
connected with the financial arrangements of a local
bookmaker. As the player is “a star,” the club, if its
decision was just and on good evidence, deserves con-
its courage. # #
Amateur cross-country running has taken a
firm hold in Victoria. For the 10-inile cross-country
championship, held at Caulfield last week, 190 runners
Started, including: teams from Ballarat. The race was
won by Tom Blake, with Herbert, of Ballarat, second.
Last year the positions were reversed. The rest of the
runners were, as far as Ist or 2nd places are concerned,
simply nowhere.

lish sporting authorities are beginning to see some force
in the conveniences which are supplied on Australian
courses. They are actually going to rail the tracks in.”
That paper would be puzzled to name any English race-
course of note which isn’t railed.

WOOL-TRADE WITH JAPAN.
The conclusion of the war between China and
Japan is sure to cause increased commercial
activity, especially in the latter country, and
will doubtless mark a historical period in
the commercial advancement of those Eastern
countries. The National Wool Company, of
Melbourne, has taken the initiative in bring-
ing under the notice of the Wool-brokers’
Association the importance of developing the
present wool-trade with Japan. The first
attempt was made in this direction 20 years ago
by the late Monckton Synnot; a second one in
1885. The movement was properly organised, and
would no doubt have succeeded, but for a sudden
rise in wool-values—a rise of from 25 to 30 per
cent. —which at once killed all interest in the move-
ment, by making wool-producers content with
present gains, and indifferent or lethargic in their
attitude towards a movement which then only
seemed to aim at providing for a vague necessity
which might arise in the dim and distant future.
However, past investigation has proved : —l. That
woollen manufacturing in China and Japan is_ in
its infancy. 2. That China, while possessing
sheep, produces no wool which will compare favor-
ably with our lowest qualities, and that sheep are
there propagated for food chiefly. Also, that
what wool is made use of, is used in the method in I
vogue in Scriptural times, 2000 years ago and up-
wards, with the skin attached. 3. That Japan
cannot breed sheep without growing food to keep
them alive in winter. It is highly desirable that
the efforts of the National Wool Company to de-
velop trade with Japan should be supported
vigorously.— Advt.

HIS GAME OF NAP.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
He was a youth his mates all vowed,
“ An all right chap,”
And nightly with the tote-shop crowd
His little scrap
He wagered, in oheck-suit arrayed
And shook the dice and gaily played
His game o! nap.
And though the tote-shop blossoms still
One finds a gap—
Shocked at one hero s dwindling till
They laid a trap—
And now, beneath the warder s gaze,
With never-ending beat he plays
His game of knap.
M'G.

The Genuine New Woman.
The latest enlistment under the “New
Woman’s ” banner is Mrs. Gambie, of Howard,
Queensland. She lucidly compares the new with
the old, show’s what it really means, and throws a
new light on the subject. If this is what is meant
by the “New Woman” no husband can object,
for not only is her happiness enhanced, but his
comfort; for what is more conducive to home
comfort than a cheerful, energetic, contented,
healthy wife? She writes: “Having been a
martyr to indigestion for several years, from
which I was mercifully cured by the use of
Warner’s Safe Cure, I desire to publicly make
known the facts, so that others may be induced to
use it and thus take a short cut to health. My
greatest suffering was after taking food, and the
pain was so intense as to cause a reluctance to eat,
and as a natural result I became weak and exces-
sively nervous, and was unable to attend to my
household work. I tried every known means,
without obtaining the ease I so longed for, until
Warner’s Safe Cure was brought under my notice.
I do not remember how many bottles I took, but
I know that the effect was wonderful from the
very commencement, and that in a few months I
was quite cured. My husband has also proved
the Safe Cure to be all that is represented in re*
moving an affection of the kidneys.”*
Paradoxical as it may appear, there are
stations in Queensland where it is found more
economical to ration employes on tinned-meat
than on freshly-killed. The advantage comes
in the cheapness consequent on the wholesale
methods adopted by the canning factories.

One of the mysteries of every Australian city
is the man who sits all day in a dingy office with a table
and two chairs for company, and whose sole means of
support is set forth on the battered door-plate outside
in the words : “ John Smith, Financial Agent.” A case
which recently cropped up in an Australian law-court
throws some light on the problem. Z wanted to borrow
£SOOO on freehold security, and he went to Y, a financial
agent, who promised to get it for him. Then, as Y didn’t
know where to get the money, he asked X, another of the
tribe, to do it, and promised him half the commission. X
went to W and offered him half his commission, and W
went to V on the same terms, and V applied to U, also on
the same terms. U got the money, so the dividing pro-
cess went no further. The commission was £SO, so that
Y got £25 for passing on the job; X got £l2 10s. for
ditto; W got £6 ss. for ditto ; V received £3 2s. 6d. for
more ditto; and U received £3 2s. 6d. for finding the
cash. That is how the financial agent lives. X makes a
decent income out of the work, but U, the small man
with the cobwebby office and the battered door-plate, has
to bustle for his crust more vigorously than a corporation
laborer.
* * * *
“For private sale, on the hill, Argent-street
South, stone house of six rooms, with verandah, together
with five detached iron rooms, outbuildings; quarter-acre
land ; suitable for good dwelling or boardinghouse ; only
£100.” This is a bona-fide Broken Hill advt. Nor was
the place snapped up. It must have cost largely over
£IOOO. In this case, it is true, the value has been
destroyed to a great extent by proximity to the smelters,
pouring out their lead and arsenic fumes. Still, pro-
perties a mile from the smelters will sell for hardly more
than one-eighth of their original cost. Land is almost
valueless. An acre, sold seven years ago for £650, was
last week offered for £BO. It was outrageously dear at
that. Here, by the way, is a problem for those who
assert that the municipalities should raise their revenues
entirely from the unimproved values of land.
Broken Hill is on a goldfield, as are so many
scores of other N.S.W. towns, whether gold has been
found near them or not. Land up to one acre can, there-
fore, be held under miner’s right or business license. The
object is to make and keep land cheap, so that every
miner may have his own home. Speculators forgot this,
and ran up prices, with the result that men who wanted
land simply moved out, where it could be got from Govt,
for almost nothing. Then prices fell, and the land is now,
what it was all along supposed to be, of only a nominal
value—at any rate, it has no monopoly value. A tax, as
suggested, on the unimproved value of the land at Broken
Hill” and other similarly-situated mining towns, would
thus not yield more than enough to pay the town-clerk’s
salary. The writer, let it be added, is a strong land-taxer.

Witheford, a Maoriland newspaper man, has
netted £30,000 in London during the past few months,
through the successful flotation of Coromandel mining
ventures. Coromandel has, since the early fifties, been
noted tor the exceeding richness and disheartening
patchiness of its lodes.
“I hear,” writes The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent, “ that M’Eacharn, of MHlwraith, M'Eaoharn
and Cos., is going to take a hand in the reconstruction of
the Eaglehawk, a Victorian mine floated in London by
M‘lntyre some years ago. The tip is to buy, as the shares
are to go to a good figure.”
The Morgan mine, Wales, run by Pritchard
Morgan, seems to be a pretty big thing. It is located on
a 5000-acre property ; there is a 40-stamp self-feeding mill
(run by water-power), and the ore is chiefly handled by
gravitation throughout. The machinery cost ±60,000,
and the property is electrically lit. The 180 men em-
ployed are barracked at the mine. A river has been
diverted three-quarters of a mile to supply power. The
compressed air for the purpose of working the rock-
borers and hoisting the stuff out of the shafts
is furnished by hydraulic pressure. The same com-
pressed air works the pumps, and the water pumped
out of the shafts assists in crushing. All the machinery
in the fitters’ shop is worked by compressed air, and the
same power generates the electricity with which to light
the mines. There is no rest for the water. After it has
accomplished all this it is diverted into another turbine,
where sufficient power is obtained to strike ninety blows
a minute, day and night, with the forty half-ton stamps.
There is some very rich stuff in chutes; early in the
summer four tons were treated, which yielded 400 oz. gold.
But the mine relies for its success on big bodies of
low-grade ore. For the nine months ended April last
10,747 tons of ore were treated at the mine, which yielded
an average of 12|dwt. per ton. During the month named,
the last of the nine, 1385 tons were treated, yielding an
average of loz. 6dwt. 19gr. to the ton—official figures,
given on the authority of the Chancellor of the Exchequer,
ho receives the royalties. The cost of working, includ-
ing royalties, rent, and everything else, is not more than
nine or ten shillings a ton.
♦ * * *
Jim and Jack were mates at the begin-
ning of Hannan’s rush. Jim pegged out a claim,
Red Hill, going 7oz. a day ; Jack one in a gully, about
200yds. off, going loz. When they had been working
three weeks, news came to Hannan’s of a great find 40
miles N.E. Jim and Jack, afraid of missing a good thing,
held a consultation. They were certainly getting good
gold between the two claims ; but, according to report,
nothing like the quantity that was being unearthed at the
new rush. So they decided that while Jim Went to try
the new country, Jack should stay at Hannan’s and shep-
herd the claims. Next morning Jim set off with two
horses and a month’s tucker, Jack going to work on the
Red Hill, leaving a spade, pick and riddle in the other
claim, as if the owner had just gone away for a spell.
Presently a big Swede came along, jumped into the
claim, threw the tools on the bank, and started to break
down some wash that was stripped. Jack rushed down
with an indignant, “Hello, mate, what the deuce are you
doing in there? Can’t a chap leave his claim for a
minute without some blessed foreigner jumping it?” The
Swede got out and said he was sorry; he thought the
claim was deserted ; but, as he had put none of the wash
through the sieve, there was no harm done. With that,
he picked his tools up and walked away. Jack filled his
sieve and started to work, but left off to curse his fate
when he saw “ the blessed foreigner ” walk straight up
and take possession of his Red Hill claim. Jack took
Boz. altogether out of the remainder of the claim he
rescued ; the Swede got 7oz. daily till the other claim was
worked out. A week later Jim turned up, half dead, and
carrying ins swag. The rush w 7 as a failure ; one horse
died, and the other was “shook ” along with his tucker.

The Murobison has nearly 200 head of stamps
supposed to be working. Yet the gold-returns are less
than 9000 oz. per month—including alluvial, specimens,
&c., from Lawler’s, Peak Hill, and all sorts of little dig-
gings up to the tropic of Capricorn.
