A correspondent : —Ballarat district is usually
considered well advanced in mining, but from what is to
be seen near Pitfield, 30 miles from Ballarat, one would
not think so. The new 7 Pitfield co. is driving an
inclined tunnel, about 1000 feet, to save sinking a
shaft 60 feet. The claim is on rising ground, 30ft. above a
c reek—and wash is about 60ft. from surface of hill. The
co. has started a tunnel as low as it can, and has driven
about 800 ft., and is now crosscutting. The face of the
tunnel is about 30ft. below entrance, and the little water
that is making is baled into trucks and wheeled out. For
stupidity, this style of mining surely takes the cake.
* » * *
Ed. Bulletin,— Re Central Queensland G.M.
Proprietary Cos. “An Old Q. Miner,” in issue June 1, is
fair on it. The iand would never have been freehold at
all if the Chinaman could have made rice. All the fos-
sickers about know this, and did not oppose the applica-
tions. Let me remark that one individual who testifies to
the good prospects of the scheme, before I left the dis-
trict, wanted to sell his land to the co., and another had
already done so.
I will not say those gentry lie as
Did in old times one Ananias :
They suffer from unconscious bias.
The only gold most of the old residents have extracted for
a long time has been from the udder of the fertile cow.
And they would cheerfully subscribe to a scheme for
obtaining sugar from the local gum-trees to get a job at
the factory. Briefly stated, the land is worth, I should
say, about 10s. an acre for grass, and the vendors are
going to register with a capital of £200,000 in order
to find something payable. I, too, sniff at the dredge on
the Boyne. The few reefs in the neighborhood that have
paid gave their best results nearest the grass, and the
gold duffered out as depth was reached. The shoots are
mostly short, and, till someone proves that there is any
gold below 300 ft., such a capital is ridiculous.—R.F.

The Coolgardie telegraphic hummer says of the
“ Westralia” mine; “A number of costeens about 15ft.
deep also uncover the lode through the property. Rich
gold has been found in these pits, bulk tests giving yields
up to 138 oz. to the ton, whilst 12 or 13oz. to the ton can
be obtained from stone lying about on the surface.”
When the writer remembers that within a quarter-mile
of this mine there is a 15-head battery which has often
wanted public crushing during the last 12 months, talk
like this makes him feel very tired.

* ♦ ♦ *
G. G. Turri, in the Banker’s Magazine,
alleges that the Victorian patent of the Australian Gold
Recovery Cos. is worthless, “since the patentees have most
unaccountably neglected to put in any complete specifi-
cation of their (cyanide of potassium) invention.” The
larger question, as to whether the cyanide process is a
valid patent anywhere and anyhow, has been already
raised. Judge Romer, in London, decided that there can
be no invention in the mere use of cyanide solutions. The
Supreme Court at Berlin has annulled the patent; a suit
to prove its alleged invalidity is pending in South Africa ;
the Maoriland Attorney-General is moving in the
same direction, though the Q. Government has
refused to follow suit. Thus far, Australia doesn’t
feel the pinch of the Gold Recovery Cos., because the cya-
nide process failed in test-oases where the ore contains
copper also. But the squeals of South African mine-
owners, who have been paying heavy royalties to the
patentees since 1891, testify to the bigness of the pinch
when it comes.

Messrs. W. 0. YUILLE & CO.’S CERTIFICATE.
We hereby certify that we sold on account of Mr. John Crozikr the St. Alban’s Estate to Mr.
W. R. Wilson on March 30, 1890, for the sum ot .£70,000 cash. At this time the property was very
lightly stocked. We have examined Mr. Wilson’s books, and certify further that blood stock to the
value (actual cost) of £32,041 has since been added to those originally purchased. Further, that the
sides of St. Alban’s Yearlings conducted by us during the seasons 1890-1-2-3 and 1894 realised a
total of 33,841 guineas, or an average of 210 guineas each.
Bourke-street, Melbourne. (Signed) W. 0. Yuillk & Co.
Mr. COLIN TEMPLETON’S (A.1.A.V.) CERTIFICATE.
certify that the St. Alban’s Estate, as shown by Mr. Wilson’s books, gave a net return of
5i per cent, on the capital invested (.£108,300) for the four years from the date of purchase to
March 30, 1894.
31 Queen-street, Melbourne. (Signed) Colin Templeton,
Incorporated Accountant.
Number of Prizes as under—l3o.
I.—THE ST. ALBAN’S ESTATE, con-
sisting of 842 acres, situated miles east of
Geelong, Victoria, Australia. The property is
divided into 30 paddocks, many with live hedge
fences.
As a Breeding and Racing Establishment it is
questionable if any more complete place exists in
Europe; certainly it has no rival in Australia.
Originally selected and laid out by Mr. James
Wilson, one of the keenest judges in the colonies,
money has continued to bo freely expended on
improving and perfecting it by Mr. W. R.
Wilson, its present owner, so that it is now one
of the show places of Victoria.
The land is extremely fertile and sound, nearly
all the paddocks being laid down in English
grasses. The Barwon River (2 chains wide and
20ft. deep) intersects the property, thus ensuring
a never-failing supply of fresh water, alike for use
and irrigation. The system for the latter is per-
fect, the drains being most extensive, and the
steam-pumping plant has a capacity of 120,000
gallons per hour. The rainfall is, however, so
good that it is rarely necessary to irrigate more
than two or three paddocks during a summer.
The plantations and hedges, of which several
miles exist, add to the usefulness and beauty of
the estate in a marked degree.
Shelter Sheds of large capacity are placed in
many of the paddocks, while the Yearling
Quarters consist of 40 loose boxes, cool and well
ventilated, built in quadrangular form, and of
ample size. Stone and brick tanks, windmills,
and feed-bins are placed in convenient places in
the various paddocks, and the yards, &c., are
ample and periectly arranged.
In the centre of the Estate the Training Track
and Course proper are laid out (1£ miles and I
mile 5 furlongs round respectively). Both may
well be compared to Flemington or Randwick,
the going being good in all weathers.
The Scraping Sheds are most suitably placed,
and an Elevated Stand commands a most perfect
view of the tracks, river, and estate generally,
with the far-famed Barrabool Hills in the dis-
tance.
The Stallion Boxes are lofty and well-designed,
opening into a large covered-in exercise ground.
Feed rooms, men’s rooms, &c., are under the
same roof. The Training Stables are perfect, and
3011 tain 15 loose boxes, feed rooms, men’s rooms,
md every necessary adjunct to a first-class estab-
ment. Two cottages for stable boys are in close
proximity, and the out-buildings are extensive
stud ample.
THE HOUSE —Brick and of modern design,
oousists of about 30 rooms, and is a perfectly
appointed gentleman’s residence, beautifully situa-
ted, having a superb view of the You Yang
Ranges and Cono Bay, the latter only two miles
distant.

In a wife-desertion case recently heard at
Freeman tie (W. A.) Police Court, defendant denied
that plaintiff was his wife, and swore she was his
brothers. Complainant averred she had arrived
only on the previous day from N.S.W. She was
married in Sydney in April, ’8G, and her husband
deserted her last April. As the asinine Bench
had only a photo, of the alleged delinquent—her
husband—to adjudicate upon, they decided to fine
defendant ,£5 !! 1
Cann, M.P. (N.S. W.), in denouncing
newspaper publication of the details of crimes
alleged against accused persons, would have im-
pressed Parliament more deeply had he directed
attention to the daily newspaper habit of publish-
ing fact, fancy and gossip when the accused is of
the shady or shabby class, and of being discreetly
reticent regarding details when he is supposed to
possess influence. In the one ease the crimes are
indubitably exaggerated ; they are given features
of horror which scare the unwary public into a
clamor for the wildest vengeance; in the other,
facts are sifted and irresponsible gossip rejected.
One day, about a fortnight ago, an elderly
Sydney lady appeared before the Chief Justice to
prosecute another old lady to the utmost limits of
the law. The other elderly lady appeared to
defend the case, and they fought it out with
bitter determination for eight days, with the
aid of a Q.O., three other barristers, and two
firms of solicitors. The trouble arose through
certain alterations made in one back-yard which
were said to have depreciated the value of the
yard adjoining, and the whole damage could have
been remedied at a cost of <£4. Case ended with
a verdict for defendant, and, as the Chief Justice
pathetically remarked, the ruin of both parties in
costs. It is a settled delusion with the average
woman that she can run a big litigation for about
60s., and it is only when the bill comes in for
<£684 16s. 8d. that she realises the nature of the
situation. If Parliament would only enact that
every lawyer’s bill should be handed in at the con-
clusion of the ease, and the amount be published
at the end of the newspaper report, it would do a
great deal to suppress useless law proceedings.

Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials; 10, an Ornament Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition; 12, a Wonderland;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long. *
Editor Bulletin, — History more or less
repeats itself. The celebrated Justice Byles, of
England, “ in cases of poisoning where he had
made up his mind, showed neither mercy nor
pity.” Byles presided at the trial of the cele-
brated Catherine Wilson, who was accused of
having poisoned several people. Some of her
alleged victims were disinterred, and rigid
examinations conducted by the most eminent
authorities of the day. At her trial for the
alleged poisoning of a Mrs. Soames, Byles, with-
out any medical testimony whatever, summed up
dead against Wilson, and “guilty ” was the ver-
dict. In passing sentence, he said; “I am as
certain that you also poisoned Mrs. Atkinson as
if I had seen you do so with my own eyes.”
Catherine Wilson was first to have been tried for
the alleged poisoning of the woman Atkinson,
Dr. Nunneley, the celebrated chemist, after
several days’ investigation, having found traces of
arsenic (the examination took place after the body
had been two years buried). Everything was
ready, and the trial about to begin, when Dr. N,
announced that he wished to withdraw his
chemical evidence in toto : he had used the test of
Riensch, and the arsenic he found came from his
own copper-plate. This, of course, stopped the
whole proceeding, but the authorities proceeded
to dig up the body of Mrs. Soames, after six
years’ burial. This was a farce, however, for
Byles had Wilson convicted —as before stated—
without any medical evidence whatever,
although the chemists openly stated that they
utterly failed to find any trace of poison. The
press protested, but the Judge’s view prevailed.
Byles further told Mrs. Wilson he was certain she
had poisoned a man named Mawer; yet a six
days’ analysis, conducted by Dr. Taylor and
others, failed to reveal poison. Baron Martin, in
sentencing the celebrated Pilizzoni, made use of
the now historic expression, “I am as sure,” &c.,
yet the man’s innocence was afterwards conclu-
sively proven.— Medicos.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin font, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a

A Maoriland paper finished up a maudlin
account of the hanging of Mrs. Dean, the Winton
baby-farmer, with the words: “On Monday
morning last Mrs. Dean was met in the Kingdom
beyond the grave by half-a-dozen little cherubim,
who in life she was so kind to, showing that such
acts of kindness are not forgotten even beyond
the grave.” For mild disgustingness this is
unique.
A drayman, brought before Me’lb. City
court on a charge of cruelty to a horse, the animal
being galled under the collar, deposed that he
received 10s. a week and had a family to support.
Fined half-a-crown. Poor wretch ! What an
alternative —kindness to his children or cruelty to
his horse !
Dear Bulletin, —Shearing has come again,
and with it the usual poetry and big-tally yarns,
cheques as big as blankets, and so on. Like many
more, I was once fool enough to believe that
shearing at £1 per 100 was merely picking up
money. I’ve had a bitter experience since and
have lived to curse the day I first took hold of a
pair of blades ! There’s nothing in it. After
riding or walking half across the continent and
wearing out your wardrobe, you may, if lucky and
there’s no strike on, drop into a shed. Ten to
one the rain pours down in buckets (droughts are
exceptional) as soon as the cook is “put in,”
which means three or four days’ tucker to work
out for a start. Then you have to buy your
shears, stone and oil, &c., and one pair of your
shears and the stone are pretty sure to be duffers.
Shears are 4s. 6d. a pair; stone, 2s. 6d. per lb.
Then you make a start, and first day a ram puts
his horns through your flannel. Bang goes ss. 6d.
Next day a big wether tears your brand-new moles
to fringes. That’s 50 sheep—los. fid. The next
thing is a subscription-list. All the deaf, dumb,
blind, and lame seem on the warpath at shearing-
time, and hardly a day passes but some cripple
arrives with a collection-box. Then there are the
ever-cadging parsons, priests, Sisters of Mercy,
Catholic-bazaar-ticket-vendors, and many othei
holy beggars; as well as circuses, wire-walkers,
boxers, minstrels, and other shows. Presently
the boss comes on the board and announces that
he is thinking about raffling a couple of horses at
£1 per member. Of course, he wishes you dis-
tinctly to understand that he doesn’t care a pin
as to whether he gets the affair off or not. Cer-
tainly not; but if you don’t ante-up your “ quid ’’
you can bet an oil-rag against a grind-stone that
you’ll get the “ sack ” the day after the raffle.
Add to these hospital-donation, union-ticket, &c.,
and calculate where you’ll be.—6 x 8.

The Two Macks .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
M'Oarty was sitting in the sun on the big log by
their bark hut; he had a large iron bucket between
his knees, and was moodily peeling potatoes with
a jack-knife. The two Macks always ‘ ‘ barked
the spuds ” on Sundays. The plum-duff was
boiling leisurely in a nail-keg on the open-air fire.
Inside, Mackenzie superintended the i ousting of
the leg of mutton, and the chant of the camp-oven
mingled with the drone of the locusts in the still,
tall t-ees, and made the old hut seem wonderfully
home-like.
The two Macks were the best “ batchers ” in
the district; they were as neat and as careful of
things as two old women, and their hut was
always trim and tidy. No salmon-tins littered the
ground about their stringybark home. Tinned-fish
is the lazy, shiftless man’s “grub.” The two
Macks cooked regularly and fared well.
They had been true mates for twenty odd years,
in luck and out, in sickness and in health, and
had been better friends than brothers. Their
mateship had, after two years, become a synonym
at Keep-it-Dark for straight-going, square-dealing
and real grit.
M'Oarty was about 50, short, grizzled, burnt,
and silent; Mackenzie was three or four years
his junior—tall, spare, hard, healthy, and also
silent. These two men, each of whom had more
than once during their long friendship proved his
readiness to lay down life for the other, sea cely
exchanged a dozen words in a week.
But of late a coolness had been noticed between
Jacky Mack and Harry Mack. M'Oarty had
deliberately turned his back upon Mackenzie on
Saturday night in Grafton’s har, he had scorn-
fully refused Harry’s assistance when going home
“ with his load,” and had walked off the tramline
into the creek in consequence thereof ; moreover,
he had neglected to take Mackenzie hot crib when
the latter was on afternoon shift and he day-shift
at the Morning Star.
Keep-it-Dark remarked this and wondered.
Dick Howard had it in his mind as he perched on
the other end of the log, chipping at his plug and
eyeing Jacky Mack bent sulkily over his spuds.
“Don’t seem very gay this mornin’, Jack,” he
said.
M'Oarty viciously gouged the eye out of a
potato, and replied with a grunt.
“ M’be you’n Harry Mack don t hit it off es
sweet es you might, eh ? ”
“He ain’t what I took him fer,” said Jack,
mournfully.
“ Es how 2 ” queried Dick.
Mackenzie now appeared outside the hut, and
began gathering chips into his hat.
“I bin a good mate t’ that feller,” said Jack,
impressively, pointing his knife at him, “ more’n
24 year, an’ now he’s goin’ back on me after all I
done fer him.”
Harry Mack jooked painfully guilty, and
clutching his hat fled under cover again.
“Bin robbin’ yeh2” suggested Curious Dick,
shifting nearer and sinking his voice to a whisper.
Jack thought it over for a few minutes in his
slow way, and he replied in the negative, with a
heavy contempt for the insinuation.
“ He ain’t actin’ the clean pertater me, that’s
all,” he added. “ He can t say 1 aint bin a true
mate,” he continued with sudden energy. “At
Eaglehawk there I nursed him through the fever
an’ shakes, an he had a bad hand at Fryingpan. I
did all the cookin’ an’ washin’-up fer a month. He’s
bin comfortable all the time, an’ ain’t wanted for
nothin’, an’ now he goes an’ turns dog.” Jack
looked down like a martyr, and the hand holding
the potato trembled visibly.
“You ain’t tellin’ how, I s’pose,” insinuated
Howard.
‘ ‘ Know that widder up the hill what sells the
milk ? ” continued M'Oarty. “ Great, ain’t she?”
“ Well, medium,” answered Howard, dubiously.
“ I think she’s great,” said Jack, decisively.
“ Anyhow, I s’pose you’ve noticed Harry bangin’
round there. I don’t blame him for that, mind.
He’s a bit gone, nor I don’t blame him fer that—
I ain’t unreasonable. I’ve bin shook on em meself
in my time, an’, above all, I ain’t unreasonable.
But I do think it dirty mean to go back on a mate
what’s stood by you fer over 20 year, an’ what
sorter trick d’ye think that Harry Mack’s playin’
me 2 He’s goin’ ter get married — (join’ ter get
married II”
Dick whistled for sheer amazement; Jack, the
wronged mate, groaned and cursed over his
potatoes; and Harry, standing in the doorway,
shamefaced, plucked at his shirt-buttons, the very
picture of conscious guilt.
“Coin’ ter get marriedl!” repeated Jack.
“ There’s a discontented beggar for yeh ! ”
EDWARD DYSON.

our lives.”

The sole legacy left by Minnie Dean, the
officially-strangled M.L. murderess, consisted of
57 closely-written pp. of MS. of a quasi-religious
nature, intended for publication, the proceeds to
go to her husband after £5 had been paid to a
certain widow. Though supposedly educated,
Minnie Dean’s composition shows ignorance
throughout. Her “ confession ” amounts to this :
She gave one of the children mentioned at the
trial laudanum to keep it quiet whilst travelling.
An overdose killed it, but she never intended
death. In the other case the child died on her
hands ten minutes after receiving it, and fear
prompted burial in the garden, bulbs being
planted over the grave to divert suspicion.
Dear Bulletin, —Your omniscience is at
fault for once—for twice! Be correction of Mrs.
Campbell Praed (Sept. 7), men out-back do travel
with water-bags slung round their horses’ necks
—up Bourke way you hardly ever see anything
else. As to spinifex bushes, a man likes to die
under something ; and, if he couldn’t get anything
bigger, no doubt hff would take a spinifex bush.

His One Virtue.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
We had been swapping cynicisms, diluted with
whisky and soda. There were three of us, and
The Man Who Didn’t Count. Certainly he paid
for the drinks, but we never took any notice of
him.
The conversation had fallen into a rut on the
subject of virtue in Man. Even the best of us
get a craving now and then to talk about the
impossible. Pottinger stated authoritatively that
Man is not virtuous by nature, and never by
education. I agreed with Pottinger. I always
agree with big men. Besides, it seemed self-
evident. Erskine, who is young and therefore
experienced, made an advance on Pottinger. He
said, “There is no such thing as virtue at all ”
Without subscribing to this liberal doctrine we
agreed that, at any rate, man hadn’t any. Then
The Man made one of his remarks.
“I knew a man once,” he said, “who had a
virtue. It was not an ordinary virtue, as you will
see when I tell you that he drank heavily, gambled
—with other people’s money—had no morals of
any sort, and never did a charitable action in his
life. He beggared a few widows and orphans,
and then committed suicide because they were
going to put him in gaol, and he hated mixing
with honest people. But with all his vices he had
one virtue and I believe I am the only man
who knew of its existence.’
The’ Man turned his glass upside down and
drummed meditatively on the bottom with his
fingers. We waited.
“ He owed his first start in life to a good-
natured idiot who befriended him because he was
an idiot; took him out of the gutter, literally;
put food into his stomach and clothes on his
back ; and finished up his idiocy by giving him a
sum of money. Had it not been for that fool the
man would probably have died respectably in the
streets. Years afterwards, when he was well up
in the world, he had the chance of ruining this
man who befriended him when he was nobody.
He had the chance of wiping out the obligation
by constituting himself the worst enemy that
fellow had. And, remember, he owed everything to
him ” —The Man pushed the glass away from him
slowly—” and —he didn’t do it 1 ”
The Man nodded to us and turned to go.
“ But ” observed Pottinger.
“Well, you see,” said The Man, “I was the
idiot.” Then he went out.

S.A. Obituary notice :
HORNE.—On the 20th June, 1895, at her son’s (Alexan-
der) residence, near Auburn, Ann, relict of the late James
Horne, late of Alberton, aged 93 years. A colonist of
fifty-five years, arrived April, 1840, in the ship Morley,
leaving- seven sons, one daughter, thirty-nine grand-
children, seventy-five great-srrandchildren, and five great-
great grandchildren to mourn their loss ; all but five of
her children were born in South Australia.
Throusfh this world she toiled,
With many ups and downs,
And often listened to hear
That sweet trumpet sound.
The angels would not blow it
Until they uot the word
That her days were ended.
She now has gone unto the Lord.
Francis Clark, the “ faithful gillie ” in
succession to John Brown, has also left Her
Gracious Majesty lamenting, it seems. High
living and no work is the worst course of treat-
ment that a braw Heelander can be subjected to.
Queen Victoria is as much responsible for the
premature demises of John and Francis, as a
missionary is accountable to Heaven for the lung-
disease of any dusky savage whom he converts to
Christianity by the aid of a pair of blankets and a
woollen comforter.
Mackay is on the warpath. Its virtuous
saccharine soul is indignant at the apathy of the
Government on the local Kanaka-outrage question.
The citizens of Sugar-town have met in solemn
conclave and formulated a charter which runs
thus :
1. That all Kanakas be put to bed at 10.
2. That all Kanakas going to and from Sunday-school
be escorted by the police.
3. That any Kanaka found in possession of anv other
weapon than a “ Glory-be-to-God” book be immediately
arrested.
4. That a day of public humiliation and prayer be im-
mediately appointed, &c., &c.
Seriously, how can anyone sympathise with the
Mackay people ? They only sowed the wind to
reap the whirlwind.
The humorists of Tas. Assembly have in-
vented a new joke. Recently, when a member
was in the midst of a fervid oration, another
M.L.A. deposited a glass of water on his seat,
and when he finished speaking he naturally sat
down in the refreshing fluid. The victim fixed
on a certain politician as the perpetrator of the
outrage, and next time the suspected one was
speaking, got a friend to surreptitiously plant a
glasa of water on his seat, by way of retaliation.
Then he rose to a point of order, and, as the other
man had to sit down till the question was decided,
he naturally sat on the glass of water and em-
bedded part of the vessel in his system. Little
amenities like these go a long way to brighten the
dreary paths of politics.
The pious platitudinarian has bobbed up
by the dozen to tell Sydney D.T. “ Outis ” that,
though it’s a precious and beautiful thing to stick
the knife into cant—when it happens to be the
other fellow’s! —he is but sounding brass and a
tinkling cymbal when he gets on to the particular
brand of humbug anent which these holy men
proceed solemnly to discharge themselves of the
same old gags in the same old sequence. “The
drought,” pronounces one painful ass, “is ob-
viously a punishment for neglect of God ! ” —by
the suffering cattle and sheep ? “ Only the Bible,”
snorts another saint, “can explain the cause of
droughts ! ” Well, let us put our trust in the
Bible, but keep our Wragge dry. “ According to
your faith shall it be done unto you,” quotes yet
another text-worshipper, who conveniently for-
gets both the counter-assertion that “Hesendeth
His rain upon the just and upon the unjust ” and
the fact that He is at present withholding said
rain no less from the man of snuffle than from the
man of scoff.

Pactolus.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Let us camp!” ’Twas my mate who had spoken.
The long Summer sunlight expired
As we threw down our swags—we had broken our
recox-d to-day and were tired.
Tired, tired! There is something to tire, and
something more poignant than pain.
When work is your quest and desire —and for
months you have sought it in vain.
No work could be had for the buying, as tho’ the
world’s people were past
The needs of all toil, and were trying to live the
millennium at last.
With a miser’s reluctance we parted the coins
that would daily seem less ;
And the time-honored method was started, that
never is quite a success.
You only can know when your lack is of pence to
exchange for your bread
What the charm or romance of the track is in the
tales of the bush you have read.
We lighted a fire, then I went down the hill to
the township in sight,
While my mate cut a pole for the tent and made
things secure for the night.
I met an old fellow, a hawker, and I thought I
would give him a trial,
But appalling he proved as a talker, and ended at
last with denial.
He grumbled, stretched out on the ground, at the
numoer of tramps, and he came
Right on deck over Labor and found every evil
was done in its name.
Time teaches you where to solicit: time teaches
you also to pitch
The tale of hard times and to visit not always the
homes of the rich.
But at length I made up to a house and my way
round the premises found ;
The place was at least worth a thousand, with
orchard and garden around.
I thought it too wealthy to trouble, but the night
was too near then to choose
’Tween the poor, whom I know to give double,
and the rich who will often refuse.
When upon you devolves, without feigning, the
mendicant's task, you repair
To the province of trenchers, where reigning, the
cook is the president there.
The cook was not there, as I reckoned, but a lady
whose face made me start;
I faltered and felt for a second a beat that was
new to my heart.
She was plucking some delicate roses in the shade
of the house where they grew,
And she saw me, and came from the closes of
vines that were dampling with dew.
By her marvellous eyes, by her face not of beauty,
but tenderness, sweet,
I knew her, and seemed to embrace all the past in
a vision complete.
She didn’t know me. I was only one more of
those swagmen that came
From the red dusty road, and the lonely harsh life
that was open to claim.
I told her my tale, but before I had finished what
pity it took —
That face I had seen moved of yore ; I remem-
bered with anguish the look.
She gave with a hand that had spared not the
larder, nor had it forborne
To search for a shilling—we fared not so bad, with
some left for the morn.
Round the pine wood that sparkled and crackled
and sent up its odorous smoke.
My mate asked me where had I tackled to make
such a rise at a stroke.
I told him my venture. He pressed me to tell him
of her I had seen,
And marvelled to know what possessed me to
mention those days that have he m ;
He thought to hear something exciting, with
infamy woven therein;
But I said: “It is not worth reciting, there’s no
incident heightened of sin.
It’s part of the life of a child, on whom fata laid
its perilous hand,
That I knew in those fierce days and wild, when
gold was the god of the land.
Thirty years, at the least, have flashed over these
plains and the far-away sea,
Since I went to ‘Pactolus.’ A rover I was, and
am fated to be.
The Warden, he gave it that name, because in the
stream there was gold,
And he talked of the mystery and fa no that clung
to some river of old.
But the diggers they called it Red Creek, for the
water was turbid and red
Wi h the sludge that was swept from the streak
of wash that they worked in the tied.
Bud back from the stream was the lead, tbit
wound away on to the hills,
That into the distance recede where the stampers
were set and the mills.
Ten thousand there were on the field, with scarcely
a hund ed on gold,
But they got a magnificent yield —our claim was
so wet that I sold
Out my share to a friend, and I built some pud-
ling-machines, and I made
A fairly good thing, but the gilt wont to him who
was first in the trade.
I was nett man to him on the dam—a swaggering
hector and vain.
But they afterwards proved him a sham, and his
fast reputation was slain.
His daughter, he made her of use —the horse for
the pump that supplied
The water to puddle and sluice, she drove on the
dam at my side ;
She drove under clanking and stir of chains
through the dark days and bright.
And then was the girlhood of her, whom we owe
for our supper to-night.
I was then on the field a new-comer. She was
twelve, and barefooted, and brown
With the great naked sun of the summer, who-e
light was as flame sweeping down.
At the mill there, her father presided o’er the
round, draggled pit of gilt mud,
And the harrows went round till they grided and
mixed the red wash like to blood.
There were always a lot at the mill; the idle men
met there to glean
Any news of fresh rushes (that fill the digge with
fervency keen).
Large oaths and small talk there were mini led
with manlier converse that found
Ears that heard, ’midst the gear-chains that jin-
gled, and the harrows went round and went
round.
The mullock-heaps, seeming to grow, rose up in
the tree-fallen rents;
And away on the broad flat below lay the clay-
colored army of tents.
To the summers not only indebted, that flamed,
was the girl for her grace
(Tho’ the sun with her cheeks had coquetted and
deepened the tone of her face),
But she somehow was free; and she shared in that
fine vital spirit that springs
With the freedom of toil unimpaired by the help-
lessness slavery brings.
Four years had slipped by, and I sold to a digger
my mills, and I went
Further south. But before I had rolled together
my blankets and tent
I went down one evening to settle my score at the
pub. and I found
The new man asserting his mettle, and the puddler
distressed in a ‘ round. ’
He had always cut capers of flashness, but for
space he was hindered and fast;
Though it seemed that his insolent rashness got
scope to be measured at last.
He came to his feet and they parried, for the
other was seeming to fence —
Who danced round his man, then he carried his
aim with a mad rush intense.
He drove back the puddler, unheeding a cry from
a child’s lips that broke.
For she saw that her father was bleeding (and tho
instinct that slumbers awoke) ;
She picked up a spike, nor dissembled, but sprang
as a tigress may spring,
And the new man reeled backwards and trembled,
and clutched at a miner to cling
For support, but he pulled him right over the
edge of the mill and they fell
In a pit whose dire horrors would cover mmy
stories related of Hell.
When the horse could be stopped in another half
circuit we sprang down with dread,
And the man dragged in by the other was the one
that we lifted out dead.
I left shortly after, and never of one from that
field could get sight
ThrQ the long years that kill men and sever, till I
went for that tucker to-night.”
Wollongong.
HUBERT YOUNG.
Baronet Newnes, the Tit Bits pirate, lost
his seat in the House of Commons at last election.
He was one of the notoriously silent members, so
silent that he ne v er once opened his mouth during
his parliamentary career. At first he was a
pretty regular attendant, because he had heard
that the speakers occasionally made original jokes,
and he wanted to crib the flashes of wit for his
publications. But when he discovered that all
the jokes were Tit Bits rehashed, which he might
just as well cut from the back numbers of the rag
that founded his fortunes, he lost all interest in
vulgar politics, and left the Commons to their fate.
One of these days the paste-and-scissors baronet
will buy a peerage and a seat in the Upper House.
One of the oldest N.B.W. police-pen-
sioners died the other day, aged 85, at the old gold-
mining town, Sofala, on the historic Turon.
Sergt.-Major Edward Giles belonged to what was
known as the “gold-police,” and retired on
pension in ’59. Two other pensions of the fifties
are still on the roll —one at 4s. 6d. per day, the
other at 3s. An interesting N.S.W. pension item
is the allowance of Is. per day to Mrs. Louisa
Codrington, widow of Const. R. D. Codrington,
who, in ’5B, was shot dead by supposed bush-
rangers at Wyagdon Hill, between Bathurst and
Sofala, The culprits were never discovered.
Imitation and Originality—A Back-
blocks Study.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Imitation is the first condition of progress,”
says Win wood Reade, slightly paraphrasing
Goethe. “ Paradoxical as it may appear—lmita-
tion is the necessary antecedent of Originality.”
Imitation is, certainly, the first condition of
progress—sometimes.
In the north-western backblocks you may see
the above aphorism tested.
Vision the first. A Chinaman sits at the door
of his opium-den, smoking. An aboriginal passes
and gazes wistfully on the opium-smoker. Next
day John sells the woolly barbarian opium -char-
coal to the value of one shilling.
Vision the second. An aboriginal lies in his
gunyah smoking opium-charcoal out of a pipe cun-
ningly devised as follows :—an inkbottle serves as
the bowl of the pipe, a bamboo reed fastened with
clay to the top of the bottle serves as a stem. A
small hole drilled into the side of the bottle serves
to feed the bowl when exhausted. General aspect
of the smoker—beatitude in ebony. A gin goes
by and gazes wistfully on her lord and master.
Vision the third. A gin is diligently scraping
out an ink-bottle opium-pipe. The burnt and
blackened cinder-like remnants thus obtained she
carefully collects, dilutes with water and drinks.
Fact!
Imitation, then, is the necessary antecedent of
Originality. The blackfellow was Imitation ; the
gin was certainly Originality.

P. LUFTIG.
S. A. Garrison Artillery now has 65officers
and 68 men!
Maoriland is the place for primitive old
patriarchs with a fine Scriptural flavor, full of
sand. The history of one of them, say Broggles,
would make an interesting Sunday-school tale.
Broggles came out in 70, married, and, working
away industriously, he was soon the possessor of
a 200-acre freehold and several children. When
his wife ran away with Goggles he somehow let
her carry off his farm, or sell it. But Broggles
was full of sand. He went to California, arriving
with only 20, but in a few years, working away
industriously, he had acquired a valuable farm, a
wife, and several children. Then he heard that
his old wife was deserted by Goggles, and he left
his Californian property in his second wife’s hands
and returned to Maoriland to disconsolate No. 1
and the new brood who called Goggles father.
Broggles only had <£lo, but he was full of sand.
He leased a place, working away industriously, and
saved up until he managed to buy it, doing this
in his wife’s name, because he was a good bit in
debt at the store. Then she led him a dog’s life,
and he went and took up with a Mrs. Doggies,
whose husband had run away with a dressmaker,
and Mrs. Doggies’ five existing children soon
became seven. The other day Broggles was work-
ing away industriously clearing timber, when a
tree happened to fall on him and out short a life
of public usefulness which isn’t often matched,
even in Maoriland. If the residents had given
him his due, there would have been a large funeral
and an elaborate tombstone with the inscription :
“Here lies Broggles. He was full of sand.” But
they were a stolid, agricultural community, and
the corpse was laid to rest without any fuss, and
Mrs. Doggies went to Wellington and took in
washing.
